The Three Causes of Knowledge and Stages of Intellect
The author enumerates three causes of knowledge and develops the stages of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three causes of knowledge bestowed upon humans (innate intellect, necessary knowledge, and the capacity for reflection) and the four stages of intellectual development: material, disposition, acquired, and actual intellect.

Core Idea
Human knowledge is God-given, progressing from innate intellect to the direct vision of all intelligibles.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif reveals how Allah honored humans with three foundational causes of knowledge: innate intellect, necessary knowledge, and the ability to reason and ascend. It then describes the stages of intellectual perfection, from material intellect to the acquired intellect, culminating in the rational soul's direct observation of all categories. Let us unpack this rich classification and understand the journey of the human intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms for each stage of intellect.
The Arabic uses technical terms that are defined in the passage.
2
Identify the three divine gifts
The passage begins by stating that Allah honored humans with three things. Find them: innate intellect, necessary knowledge, and making them fit for reflection.
These are the three causes of knowledge.
3
Understand the terms for intellect stages
The passage names several types of intellect: material (hayulani), by disposition (bil-malaka), acquired (mustafad), and in act (bil-fi'l). Note their definitions and sequence.
These stages represent the development of the rational soul.
4
Follow the ascent from necessary to theoretical
The author explains that one ascends from necessary truths to theoretical truths, then through repeated observation acquires a habit, and finally reaches the ultimate goal.
The ascent is both epistemological and spiritual.
5
Reflect on the ultimate goal
The passage concludes with the definition of acquired intellect: the rational soul observes all its categories at once. This is the ultimate perfection.
That is the highest stage of human knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكرم بني آدم نوع الإنسان على غيره ( بالعقل الغريزي) أي بالقوة المستعدة لإدراك المعقولات التي جبلت عليها فطرتهم ويسمى عقلاً هيولانياً ( والعلم الضروري) الحاصل لهم بلا اكتساب المسمى عقلاً بالملكة ( وأهلهم ) جعلهم أهلاً، نسخة وفي الأصل، وأهله بتأويل الإنسان ( للنظر والاستدلال بالعلوم الضرورية ( والارتقاء في مدارج الكمال) وذلك بأن يرتقي أولاً من الضروريات إلى مشاهدة النظريات، عقلاً مستفاداً، ويسمى ثم تتكرر مشاهدتها مرة بعد أخرى حتى تحصل له ملكة استحضارها متى أريد بلا تجشم كسب جديد، ويسمى عقلاً بالفعل وهو وإن كان عن المستفاد في الحدوث لكنه وسيلة إليه متقدمة عليه في البقاء، وقد يقال : العقل المستفاد هو أن تصير النفس الناطقة بحيث تشاهد مقولاتها بأسرها دفعة واحدة، فلا يغيب عنها شيء منها أصلاً، وهذا هو الغاية القصوى في الارتقاء في
And He honored the children of Adam, the human kind, over others – by innate intellect, i.e. the faculty prepared for apprehending intelligibles, upon which their nature is created, and it is called material intellect (ʿaql hayulani); and by necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍaruri) acquired without effort, called intellect by disposition (ʿaql bil-malaka); and He made them people – He made them fit, and in the original, 'and his people' by interpreting 'human' – for reflection and deduction from necessary sciences, and for ascending the ranks of perfection. That is by ascending first from necessary truths to the observation of theoretical truths, as acquired intellect (ʿaql mustafad). It is called then his repeated observation again and again until he acquires a disposition to bring them to mind whenever wanted without undertaking new acquisition, and it is called intellect in act (ʿaql bil-fiʿl). And although it is from the acquired in origination, it is a means to it, prior to it in endurance. And it is said: the acquired intellect is that the rational soul becomes such that it observes all its categories at once, so that nothing of them is absent from it at all. And this is the ultimate goal in ascending the
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the causes of knowledge and the stages of intellect. The author explains that God honored humans by three things: 1) Innate intellect (al-ʿaql al-gharizi), which is the faculty for grasping intelligibles, also called material intellect (ʿaql hayulani). 2) Necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍaruri), acquired without effort, called intellect by disposition (ʿaql bil-malaka). 3) Making them fit for reflection and deduction from necessary sciences, leading to ascending the ranks of perfection. The author then details the ascent: from necessary truths to theoretical truths, attaining the acquired intellect (ʿaql mustafad); then repeated observation yields a disposition to recall them effortlessly, called intellect in act (ʿaql bil-fiʿl). Although intellect in act arises from the acquired in origination, it is prior in endurance. Finally, a definition of acquired intellect is given: the rational soul observes all its categories at once, nothing absent. This is the ultimate goal of ascent. The passage is a classification and explanation of the four stages of intellect.

Key Terms
العقل الغريزي
العقل الهيولاني
العلم الضروري
العقل بالملكة
النظر والاستدلال
العقل المستفاد
العقل بالفعل
النفس الناطقة
مقولات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'necessary knowledge' is included in 'innate intellect' as one gift.
Correction: The author distinguishes them as two separate gifts: innate intellect is a faculty, while necessary knowledge is specific knowledge without effort.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'acquired intellect' (al-ʿaql al-mustafad) in its ultimate sense?
A) The faculty for apprehending intelligibles
B) Knowledge acquired without effort
C) The rational soul observing all its categories at once
D) A disposition to recall knowledge with effort

Key Takeaway
The ultimate goal of human intellect is the rational soul's simultaneous observation of all intelligibles, achieved through the divinely given capacities of innate intellect, necessary knowledge, and the power of reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been given innate intellect and necessary knowledge. When was the last time you consciously used these gifts to draw closer to your Creator through reflection? What distractions dull this use?
?
The ultimate goal is for your soul to observe all intelligibles at once. Do you truly desire that state, or are you content with scattered, occasional thoughts about truth? What practical change can you make today to prioritize this ascent?
?
The passage lists three divine gifts: innate intellect, necessary knowledge, and the ability to reflect and deduce. Which of these three do you neglect the most? What concrete action will you take this week to use that gift as intended?

Why Knowledge of Particulars Is Not a Stage of Theoretical Intellect
Objection and answer about knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of sensible particulars is excluded from the stages of the theoretical intellect and how it relates to the animal soul.

Core Idea
Sensory knowledge belongs to the animal soul, not the rational intellect.

The author anticipated a logical objection: if the stages of theoretical intellect ascend from pure potentiality to necessary truths, why isn't knowledge of sensible particulars a stage in between? This lesson unpacks the answer, which hinges on distinguishing between the rational soul's theoretical faculty and the animal soul's properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objector proposes an intermediate stage, and the author responds with a clear reason for exclusion.
The answer is concise; pay attention to the key terms.
2
Identify the two types of soul mentioned
The author distinguishes between the animal soul (النفس الحيوانية) and the rational soul (النفس الناطقة). Understand why knowledge of particulars belongs to the former.
3
Reflect on the purpose of enumeration
The author states that the purpose is to count stages specific to the rational soul. Consider why this distinction is important for the study of intellect.
This connects to the broader topic of rational judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (المسمى عقلاً بالملكة فإن قلت : لا شك أن بين المرتبة الأولى التي هي المحض، وبين المرتبة الثانية المفسرة بالعلم بالضروريات واستعداد النفس بذلك لاكتساب النظريات مرتبة أخرى هي العلم بالجزئيات المحسوسة فَلمَ لم يتعرضوا إليها، قلت : لأنها ليست من مراتب القوة النظرية بل من خواص النفس الحيوانية والغرض عد المراتب المخصوصة بالنفس الناطقة .
His statement: 'called intellect by disposition (ʿaql bil-malaka)'. For if you say: There is no doubt that between the first stage, which is pure potentiality, and the second stage, interpreted as knowledge of necessary truths and the soul's preparedness thereby for acquiring theoretical truths, there is another stage, which is knowledge of sensible particulars. So why did they not mention it? I say: Because it is not among the stages of the theoretical faculty, but rather among the properties of the animal soul, and the purpose is to count the stages specific to the rational soul.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. The author anticipates a query: between the first stage (pure potentiality) and the second stage (knowledge of necessary truths and preparedness for theoretical truths), there exists an intermediate stage – knowledge of sensible particulars. Why was this not included? The answer: knowledge of particulars belongs to the animal soul's properties, not to the theoretical faculty. The intention is to enumerate stages specific to the rational soul. Thus, the objector's proposed stage is excluded because it is not part of the rational soul's theoretical capacity.

Key Terms
القوة النظرية
النفس الحيوانية
النفس الناطقة
الجزئيات المحسوسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all forms of knowledge, including sensory perception, are stages of the intellect.
Correction: The author clarifies that knowledge of sensible particulars belongs to the animal soul, not to the theoretical faculty of the rational soul.

Checkpoint
Why is knowledge of sensible particulars not considered a stage of the theoretical intellect?
A) Because it is too trivial
B) Because it belongs to the animal soul, not the rational soul
C) Because it is not a type of knowledge
D) Because it is included in necessary knowledge

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of sensible particulars is excluded from the stages of theoretical intellect because it pertains to the animal soul, not the rational soul's theoretical faculty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your senses to judge something, do you ever mistake that sensory impression for a rational truth? How often do you question whether your knowledge comes from the animal soul's properties or from your rational intellect? Commit today to pause and distinguish between what you sense and what you understand.
?
You have a rational soul capable of grasping necessary truths, yet how much of your day is spent chasing sensory pleasures or reacting to sensory inputs? Is your intellect being fed with eternal truths or are you letting the animal soul dominate? What will you change tomorrow to prioritize your rational soul over your animal soul?

The Three Levels of Certainty and the Ultimate Goal
Sanusi presents an objection about the three levels of certainty and defends the ultimate goal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between knowledge of certainty, eye of certainty, and truth of certainty, and how the acquired intellect remains the ultimate goal despite higher stages.

Core Idea
Certainty has degrees: seeing by firelight, seeing the fire, and becoming the fire.

Sanusi presents a critical objection to the claim that acquired intellect is the ultimate goal. The objector points to two higher stages of certainty, illustrated by a powerful fire analogy. This lesson unpacks that analogy and clarifies the relationship between these stages.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing; then read the translation to grasp the flow of the objection and the fire analogy.
Notice that the passage begins with a side issue about the disposition before the main objection.
2
Understand the dispute about disposition
Some verifiers argue that a full disposition for effortless recall is not required for intellect in act; mere ability to recall with effort suffices. Consider why this matters for limiting the stages of theoretical faculty.
This helps clarify the definitional boundaries before the main objection.
3
Analyze the three levels of certainty
Identify the three levels: ʿilm al-yaqin (knowledge of certainty), ʿayn al-yaqin (eye of certainty), and ḥaqq al-yaqin (truth of certainty). See how the fire analogy illustrates each level and how they relate to the acquired intellect as the ultimate goal.
The objector uses this to challenge the claim that acquired intellect is the final stage; reflect on the author's likely response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( حتى تحصل له ملكة (استحضارها قال بعض المحققين وعندي أنه لا اعتبار بملكة الاستحضار في العقل بالفعل بل القدرة على الاستحضار في الجملة كافية، وإلا لم تنحصر مراتب القوة النظرية في الأربعة فإنه إذا أحضرت المعقولات مرة مثلاً، وذهل عنها فالنفس قادرة على استحضارها ولو بتجشم فهذه المرتبة لو لم تعد عقلاً بالفعل، ولم يتحقق الإنحصار كعدم تحققه على التفسير المستفاد بالتفسير الثاني . قوله: (متقدمة عليه في البقاء ولأن في كل منهما جهة تقدم على الآخر، وتعارض الجهتين أشار إليهما معاً بقوله والارتقاء في مدارج الكمال . قوله : وهذا هو الغاية القصوى فإن قلت قد صرحوا بأنه بعد مرتبة العقل المستفاد مرتبتان إحداهما مرتبة عين اليقين وهي أن تصير النفس بحيث تشاهد المعقولات في المفارق المفيض إياها كما هي والثانية مرتبة حق اليقين، وهي أن تصير النفس بحيث تتصل بالمفارق اتصالاً عقلياً وتلاقي ذاتها ذاته تلاقياً روحانياً، وفرقوا بين علم اليقين وعين اليقين وحق اليقين إن مشاهدة كل ما يرى بتوسط نور النار بمثابة علم اليقين ومعاينة جرم النار الذي يفيض ذلك النور على ما يقبل الإضاءة بمثابة عين اليقين وتأثير النار فيما تصل إليه بمحو هويته حتى يصير صرفاً : بمثابة حق اليقين فما معنى قوله وهذا هو الغاية القصوى في الارتقاء في الكمالات العلمية، لا يقال : الكلام في مراتب القوة النظرية ومرتبتا عين اليقين وحق اليقين من مراتب
His statement: 'until he acquires a disposition to bring them to mind' – some verifiers said: In my view, no consideration is given to the disposition of bringing to mind in intellect in act; rather, the ability to bring to mind in general is sufficient. Otherwise, the stages of theoretical faculty would not be confined to four. For if one brings the intelligibles to mind once, for example, and then forgets them, the soul is able to bring them to mind, even with effort. So this stage, if not counted as intellect in act, and confinement is not realized, just as it is not realized on the interpretation of the acquired by the second interpretation. His statement: 'prior to it in endurance' – because in each of them there is an aspect of priority over the other, and the conflict of the two aspects is indicated by both together in his statement 'and ascending the ranks of perfection.' His statement: 'And this is the ultimate goal' – for if you say: They have explicitly stated that after the stage of acquired intellect are two stages: one is the stage of the eye of certainty (ʿayn al-yaqin), which is that the soul becomes such that it observes the intelligibles in the separate effuser as they are; and the second is the stage of the truth of certainty (ḥaqq al-yaqin), which is that the soul becomes such that it connects with the separate in an intellectual connection and its essence meets His essence in a spiritual meeting. And they distinguished between knowledge of certainty (ʿilm al-yaqin), eye of certainty (ʿayn al-yaqin), and truth of certainty (ḥaqq al-yaqin): the observation of everything seen through the medium of the fire's light is analogous to knowledge of certainty; the direct sight of the fire's body which effuses that light onto what accepts illumination is analogous to the eye of certainty; and the effect of the fire on what it reaches by annihilating its identity until it becomes pure fire is analogous to truth of certainty. So what is the meaning of his statement 'this is the ultimate goal in ascending the perfection of knowledge'? It cannot be said: The discussion is about the stages of theoretical faculty, and the two stages of eye of certainty and truth of certainty are among the stages
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage contains a critical discussion and an objection. First, the author reports a statement from 'some verifiers' (baʿḍ al-muḥaqqiqīn) challenging the requirement of a disposition (malaka) for effortless recall in intellect in act. They argue that mere ability to recall (even with effort) is sufficient; otherwise, the stages would not be limited to four. The author notes the difficulty and refers to the 'second interpretation' of acquired intellect. Then, the author addresses a statement that acquired intellect is the ultimate goal. An objector raises that after the acquired intellect, there are two higher stages: 'ayn al-yaqin (eye of certainty) and ḥaqq al-yaqin (truth of certainty), explained with a simile of fire: knowledge of certainty is like seeing objects by firelight; eye of certainty is like seeing the fire itself; truth of certainty is like being consumed by the fire. The objector questions how the acquired intellect can be the ultimate goal. The author's response is cut off but anticipates that those stages are part of the theoretical faculty. The passage is an objection-and-answer (though incomplete).

Key Terms

ملكة الاستحضار
القوة النظرية
العقل المستفاد
عين اليقين
حق اليقين
علم اليقين
المفارق المفيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Someone might think that the acquired intellect is the final stage of human perfection, with no further levels of certainty.
Correction: The objector shows that there are two higher stages—eye of certainty and truth of certainty—which represent deeper union with the intelligible order.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, what corresponds to 'eye of certainty' (ʿayn al-yaqin) in the fire analogy?
A) Seeing objects by firelight
B) Seeing the fire itself
C) Being consumed by the fire
D) The ability to recall without effort

Key Takeaway
The three levels of certainty illustrate an ascending union with the truth, yet the acquired intellect remains the ultimate goal for the human intellect's perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your study of Islamic theology, are you satisfied with knowing about the proofs (knowledge of certainty), or do you feel the urgency to reach the point where the truth becomes so evident it transforms your very being (truth of certainty)? What concrete steps can you take today to move from seeing by the light to becoming the light?
?
The passage describes the ultimate goal as 'ascending the ranks of perfection in knowledge.' When you learn a new aqeedah concept, do you stop at memorizing the argument, or do you push yourself to internalize it until it becomes a part of your intellectual disposition? What is holding you back from the next level?
?
If the fire of truth were to consume your current beliefs and assumptions, would you embrace that annihilation or resist? Reflect on a time when a theological truth deeply unsettled you—did you let it burn away your old self, or did you step back? What would it take for you to surrender completely to the truth?

The Three Degrees of Certainty and the Objection
The objector presents two higher stages and the fire analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three degrees of certainty (ʿilm al-yaqīn, ʿayn al-yaqīn, ḥaqq al-yaqīn) and why they pose a challenge to the claim that simultaneous observation is the ultimate goal.

Core Idea
The three degrees of certainty form a hierarchy beyond mere simultaneous observation.

This passage sets up an objection to the author's earlier claim that the acquired intellect in its ultimate form is the highest scientific perfection. An objector presents two higher stages—eye of certainty and truth of certainty—and illustrates them with a vivid fire analogy. Understanding this objection is key to appreciating the author's later resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the analogy
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the objector presents two stages after acquired intellect and distinguishes them via analogy.
The analogy uses fire to illustrate the three types of certainty.
2
Identify the three degrees of certainty
Find where the author explains ʿilm al-yaqīn, ʿayn al-yaqīn, and ḥaqq al-yaqīn. Understand each definition.
They are defined in terms of observing light, the source of light, and being consumed by fire.
3
Understand why this is an objection
The objector argues that the eye and truth of certainty are higher than the acquired intellect described earlier. See why this challenges the claim that the ultimate goal is simultaneous observation.
The challenge is that there seem to be higher perfections beyond mere observation.
4
Reflect on the anticipated counter
Notice the phrase 'لا يقال' (it is not said), which is the objector's own attempt to preempt a response. This shows the depth of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (متقدمة عليه في البقاء ولأن في كل منهما جهة تقدم على الآخر، وتعارض الجهتين أشار إليهما معاً بقوله والارتقاء في مدارج الكمال . قوله : وهذا هو الغاية القصوى فإن قلت قد صرحوا بأنه بعد مرتبة العقل المستفاد مرتبتان إحداهما مرتبة عين اليقين وهي أن تصير النفس بحيث تشاهد المعقولات في المفارق المفيض إياها كما هي والثانية مرتبة حق اليقين، وهي أن تصير النفس بحيث تتصل بالمفارق اتصالاً عقلياً وتلاقي ذاتها ذاته تلاقياً روحانياً، وفرقوا بين علم اليقين وعين اليقين وحق اليقين إن مشاهدة كل ما يرى بتوسط نور النار بمثابة علم اليقين ومعاينة جرم النار الذي يفيض ذلك النور على ما يقبل الإضاءة بمثابة عين اليقين وتأثير النار فيما تصل إليه بمحو هويته حتى يصير صرفاً نارا بمثابة حق اليقين فما معنى قوله وهذا هو الغاية القصوى في الارتقاء في الكمالات العلمية، لا يقال: الكلام في مراتب القوة النظرية ومرتبتا عين اليقين وحق اليقين من مراتب الكمالات العلمية ومستقره الدار الآخرة، وأما في الدار الدنيا فقد يرتجى لمحات منه للنفوس المجردة عن العلائق البشرية). قلت: أراد بالمستفاد الذي حكموا بأن ما بعده بمرتبتين عين اليقين وحق اليقين المستفاد بالمعنى الأول لا الثاني، إذ لا نسلم أن مشاهدة المعقولات دفعة يحصل قبل الاتصال بالمفارق والمحكوم عليه بأنه الغاية القصوى هو المستفاد بالمعنى الثاني، وبالجملة لا يتصور في نفس الكمال العلمي مرتبةً أعلى من أن تكون جميع النظريات على ما هي عليه مشاهدةً بالفعل على سبيل الاجتماع، سواء قيل: هذه المرتبة تحصل قبل المرتبتين الأخريين أو بعدهما، أو أنها عين إحداهما وأعلوية المرتبتين الأخريين منهما لو سلم فليس باعتبار نفس الكمال العلمي، بل باعتبار اشتمالهما عليهما وعلى مرتبة أخرى فلا إشكال.
His statement: 'preceding it in duration' — because in each of them there is an aspect of priority over the other, and the conflict of the two aspects; he alluded to both together by his saying 'and ascending in the degrees of perfection'. His statement: 'And this is the ultimate goal'. For if you say: They have stated that after the stage of acquired intellect there are two stages: one is the stage of the eye of certainty (ʿayn al-yaqīn), which is that the soul becomes such that it observes the intelligibles in the separate [intellect] that emanates them as they are; and the second is the stage of the truth of certainty (ḥaqq al-yaqīn), which is that the soul becomes such that it connects with the separate [intellect] by an intellectual connection and its essence meets its essence in a spiritual meeting. And they distinguished between the knowledge of certainty (ʿilm al-yaqīn), the eye of certainty (ʿayn al-yaqīn), and the truth of certainty (ḥaqq al-yaqīn): Observing everything seen through the medium of the light of fire is like knowledge of certainty; seeing the body of fire that emanates that light upon what receives illumination is like the eye of certainty; and the effect of the fire in what it reaches by obliterating its identity until it becomes pure fire is like the truth of certainty. So what is the meaning of his saying: 'this is the ultimate goal in ascending in scientific perfections'? It is not said: The discussion is about the stages of theoretical power, and the two stages of the eye of certainty and the truth of certainty are among the stages of scientific perfections, and their abode is the Hereafter. As for this world, glimpses of them may be hoped for by souls stripped of human attachments. I say: By 'acquired intellect' that they ruled that after it there are two stages — the eye of certainty and the truth of certainty — he meant the acquired intellect in the first sense, not the second. For we do not concede that simultaneous observation of intelligibles occurs before connection with the separate [intellect]. And what is ruled to be the ultimate goal is the acquired intellect in the second sense. In summary, there is no higher stage in scientific perfection itself than that all theoretical matters, as they are, be actually observed collectively. Whether it is said that this stage occurs before the other two stages or after them, or that it is identical to one of them, and the superiority of the other two stages over it, if granted, is not due to the scientific perfection itself, but because they include it and another stage. So there is no problem.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is first an explanation of a phrase about priority and then a detailed objection and answer regarding the ultimate goal. The author first clarifies that there is a mutual priority between the intellect in actuality and acquired intellect; each has an aspect of priority over the other, alluded to by the phrase 'ascending in the degrees of perfection'. Then he turns to the statement 'this is the ultimate goal'. An objector raises that scholars mention two stages beyond acquired intellect: the eye of certainty and the truth of certainty, which are higher than mere simultaneous observation. He illustrates with the analogy of fire: knowledge of certainty is like seeing by firelight, eye of certainty is like seeing the fire itself, and truth of certainty is like being consumed by fire. So how can simultaneous observation be the ultimate goal? The author answers by distinguishing two meanings of acquired intellect: one is the simultaneous observation of intelligibles in this world (the second meaning), and the other is a preliminary stage. The two higher stages are beyond the first meaning but the ultimate goal mentioned is the second meaning. Moreover, scientific perfection itself consists of actual collective observation; the higher stages add other perfections like connection with the separate intellect, but that does not detract from the observation being the ultimate in purely scientific knowledge. Thus there is no conflict.

Key Terms
عين اليقين
حق اليقين
عقل مستفاد
مفارق
كمال علمي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the three degrees of certainty are sequential stages achievable in this worldly life through normal intellectual development.
Correction: The passage indicates that the eye of certainty and truth of certainty are primarily stages of the Hereafter, though glimpses may occur for souls detached from worldly attachments.

Checkpoint
According to the fire analogy, what does 'eye of certainty' (ʿayn al-yaqīn) correspond to?
A) Seeing objects by the light of fire.
B) Seeing the fire itself.
C) Being consumed by fire.
D) The warmth of fire.

Key Takeaway
The three degrees of certainty—knowledge, eye, and truth—represent an increasing intimacy with reality, with the latter two appearing to surpass mere simultaneous observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about the three degrees of certainty, which one best describes your current relationship with the truths of your faith—knowledge about God from afar, or a direct seeing that transforms you? What would it cost you to move toward a deeper certainty?
?
The fire analogy suggests that true certainty consumes your old self. Are you willing to let your comfortable assumptions and worldly attachments be burned away to become 'pure fire' in devotion? What is holding you back?
?
The passage says glimpses of the higher stages are possible for souls stripped of human attachments. What attachment in your life right now is the greatest obstacle to even a glimpse of direct spiritual vision? Will you do something about it today?

Resolving the Objection: Two Senses of Acquired Intellect
The author answers the objection by distinguishing two senses of acquired intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author distinguishes two meanings of 'acquired intellect' to resolve the objection, affirming that simultaneous observation is the ultimate scientific perfection.

Core Idea
The ultimate goal is the acquired intellect in its second sense—simultaneous observation of all intelligibles.

Having presented the objection, the author now provides a careful answer. He distinguishes two meanings of 'acquired intellect' and argues that the two higher stages do not surpass the scientific perfection of simultaneous observation; they add other dimensions. This lesson examines his reasoning and conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of acquired intellect
Read the answer and note where the author distinguishes between the first and second meaning of 'acquired intellect'. The first meaning is a preliminary stage; the second is the simultaneous observation.
The author says the objector meant the first meaning, while his claim is about the second.
2
Understand why the higher stages are not higher in scientific perfection
The author argues that scientific perfection itself is simply the actual collective observation of all theoretical matters. The higher stages may include that plus something else, but the observation itself is the peak of scientific knowledge.
The superiority of the higher stages, if any, is due to including additional perfections beyond scientific knowledge.
3
See how the objection is resolved
By distinguishing the two meanings, the author shows that the objection does not apply to his claim. The ultimate goal is the second meaning, and even if the eye and truth of certainty are higher, they do not negate that observation is the ultimate in scientific perfection.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The author concludes that there is no problem. The passage ends with 'so there is no problem' (فلا إشكال). This shows the objection is successfully rebutted.

His statement: 'preceding it in duration' — because in each of them there is an aspect of priority over the other, and the conflict of the two aspects; he alluded to both together by his saying 'and ascending in the degrees of perfection'. His statement: 'And this is the ultimate goal'. For if you say: They have stated that after the stage of acquired intellect there are two stages: one is the stage of the eye of certainty (ʿayn al-yaqīn), which is that the soul becomes such that it observes the intelligibles in the separate [intellect] that emanates them as they are; and the second is the stage of the truth of certainty (ḥaqq al-yaqīn), which is that the soul becomes such that it connects with the separate [intellect] by an intellectual connection and its essence meets its essence in a spiritual meeting. And they distinguished between the knowledge of certainty (ʿilm al-yaqīn), the eye of certainty (ʿayn al-yaqīn), and the truth of certainty (ḥaqq al-yaqīn): Observing everything seen through the medium of the light of fire is like knowledge of certainty; seeing the body of fire that emanates that light upon what receives illumination is like the eye of certainty; and the effect of the fire in what it reaches by obliterating its identity until it becomes pure fire is like the truth of certainty. So what is the meaning of his saying: 'this is the ultimate goal in ascending in scientific perfections'? I say: By 'acquired intellect' that they ruled that after it there are two stages — the eye of certainty and the truth of certainty — he meant the acquired intellect in the first sense, not the second. So there is no problem.

Answering the Objection on the Abode of Acquired Intellect
Objection and answer regarding the abode of acquired intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the acquired intellect's abode is not exclusively the Hereafter is answered through the concepts of potentiality and preparedness.

Core Idea
Acquired intellect's abode is the Hereafter because full actualization of all theoretical knowledge is only possible there.

The claim that the abode of the acquired intellect is the Hereafter faces an objection: if someone acquires only a few intelligibles and observes them in this world, then the abode would be this world. The answer clarifies that 'acquired intellect' refers to the ability to apprehend all theoretical matters, either through their premises or as a preparedness. This preserves the statement that its full manifestation is in the Hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Identify what is being challenged: the claim that the abode of the acquired intellect is the Hereafter is questioned by pointing out that a person could have only a few theoretical intelligibles and observe them in this world.
The objector assumes 'acquired intellect' means actually acquired knowledge.
2
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation to grasp the flow of argument.
Notice the technical terms: معقولات (intelligibles), مبادي (premises), استعداد (preparedness).
3
Analyze the answer
The answer reinterprets 'acquired intellect' as the ability to apprehend all theoretical matters. It offers two interpretations: grasping premises (potential for all) or metaphorical preparedness.
This resolves the objection by showing that full actualization is not required in this world.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: it upholds the doctrine that the Hereafter is the ultimate abode for the full realization of intellectual perfection, while this world is a place of preparation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( ومستقره الدار الآخرة قيل عليه الظاهر أن المراد بالمعقولات المذكورة في هذا التفسير المعقولات التي كسبها وأدركها النفس على ما يشعر به قوله : مشاهدة معقولاتها وبه صرح في حواشي شرح المطالع، حيث قال: التي أدركها ولا يخفى على ذي مسكة أنه يجوز أن يكون شخص من الأشخاص قد حصل له معقولات نظرية لا تزيد على اثنين أو ثلاثة، فيشاهدها في الدار الدنيا أوله زيادة تعلق وعدم تجرد فلا يصح قوله: ومستقره الدار الآخرة وأجيب بأن المراد جميع النظريات، وقوله: معقولاتها من حيث إنه يتمكن من تعقل جميع النظريات، وقوله: في الحواشي التي أدركها محمول على إدراك إما بمباديها أو لنفسها، من حيث إن إدراك المبادي إدراك للمطالب بالقوة وأنت خبير بأن اعتبار حصول مبادي جميع النظريات بالفعل، لكل نفس يعتبر المستفاد بالنسبة إليها مما لا يكاد يصح، اللهم إلا أن يحمل الإدراك على المجاز أعني استعداده فحينئذ لا يحتاج إلى توسط المبادي في البين كما لا يخفى.
His statement: 'and its abode is the Hereafter'. It was objected to him: The apparent meaning is that what is meant by the intelligibles mentioned in this interpretation are the intelligibles that the soul has acquired and grasped, as suggested by his statement 'observing its intelligibles'. And this was stated explicitly in the glosses on Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ, where he said: 'which it has grasped'. And it is not hidden from anyone of intelligence that it is possible for a person to have acquired theoretical intelligibles that do not exceed two or three, and he observes them in this world. Otherwise there would be an increase of attachment and lack of detachment, so his statement 'its abode is the Hereafter' would not be correct. And the answer is that what is meant is all theoretical matters, and his statement 'its intelligibles' [indicates] that he is able to apprehend all theoretical matters. And his statement in the glosses 'which it has grasped' is interpreted as grasping either by their premises or by themselves, in the sense that grasping the premises is a potential grasping of the conclusions. And you know that considering the actual acquisition of the premises of all theoretical matters for every soul that is considered to have acquired intellect relative to it is hardly correct. Unless 'grasping' is understood metaphorically, i.e., his preparedness, then there is no need for the mediation of premises, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the statement that the abode of the acquired intellect (in the sense of simultaneous observation) is the Hereafter. The objector argues: if acquired intellect means observing the intelligibles one has actually acquired, then a person could have only a few theoretical intelligibles and observe them in this world. That would contradict the claim that its abode is the Hereafter. The answer given is that the acquired intellect in question refers to the ability to apprehend all theoretical matters, not just a few. Even in the glosses, 'grasped' is interpreted either as grasping the premises (which implies potential for all) or as a metaphorical preparedness. In that case, it is not necessary that all premises are actually present; rather, the soul has the innate preparedness to acquire them. Thus, the full actualization of all theoretical matters may be in the Hereafter, but the preparedness can be in this world.

Key Terms
دار الآخرة
معقولات
مبادي
استعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the acquired intellect must mean having all theoretical knowledge actually present in this world.
Correction: Rather, it can mean having the ability or preparedness to acquire all theoretical knowledge, which is realized fully in the Hereafter.

Intellectual Perfection's Abode: The Hereafter
Statement on the abode of intellectual perfection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that intellectual perfection is fully realized only in the Hereafter, though glimpses can occur in this world for purified souls.

Core Idea
Intellectual perfection's ultimate home is the Hereafter.

The commentator begins by affirming that the complete realization of intellectual knowledge is reserved for the afterlife. However, this does not mean no insight is possible in this world. For souls freed from bodily attachments, a foretaste can be hoped for.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about perfection's abode
Read the Arabic sentence where the commentator states that intellectual perfection's abode is the Hereafter, with possible glimpses in this world.
Focus on the contrast between 'ghlimpses' and full realization.
2
Understand why full perfection is only in the Hereafter
The commentator implies that the here-and-now is not the full locus; only the Hereafter provides complete perfection. Consider why that might be.
Think about the limitations of worldly existence.
3
Note the condition for glimpses
Glimpses are possible for souls stripped of human attachments. Reflect on what this means for spiritual practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المؤلف الكمالات العلمية ومستقره الدار الآخرة، وأما في الدار الدنيا فقد يرتجى لمحات منه للنفوس المجردة عن العلائق البشرية ) ثم أمرهم) عطف على كرم مع ما عطف عليه، قوله : ( عطف على كرم مع ما عطف عليه الخ ) يعني أنه عطف عليه بعد اعتبار عطف أهله عليه، لأن الأمر بالتفكر مترتب على التكريم المقيد بالأهلية، لا على التكريم فقط، إذ لا تكليف للصبي، ولم يجعله معطوفاً على أهله إبقاء لثم على معناها الأصلي؛ إذ ليس تعلق الأمر بالتفكر متأخراً بمهلة عن الأهلية المذكورة، فإن مناط التكليف هو العقل بالملكة عند الشيخ الأشعري، ولم يحمل كلمة ثم على مجرد التدرج في المراتب، إذ لا وجه لتخصيص، قوله : أمرهم بذلك . قوله : ( وكلمة ثم على معناها أي يجوز إبقاؤها على معناها الأصلي بناء على مذهب الأشاعرة . العمل وآثاره، لانا نقول المستفاد بالمعنى الثاني من مراتب العمل أيضاً قلت : أراد بالمستفاد الذي حكموا بأن ما بعده بمرتبتين عين اليقين وحق اليقين المستفاد بالمعنى الأول لا الثاني، إذ لا نسلم أن مشاهدة المعقولات دفعة يحصل قبل الاتصال بالمفارق والمحكوم عليه بأنه الغاية القصوى هو المستفاد بالمعنى الثاني، وبالجملة لا يتصور في نفس الكمال العلمي مرتبةً أعلى من أن تكون جميع النظريات على ما هي عليه مشاهدةً بالفعل على سبيل الاجتماع، سواء قيل: هذه المرتبة تحصل قبل المرتبتين الأخريين أو بعدهما، أو أنها عين إحداهما وأعلوية المرتبتين الأخريين منهما لو سلم فليس باعتبار نفس الكمال العلمي، بل باعتبار اشتمالهما عليهما وعلى مرتبة أخرى فلا إشكال .
The author's introduction: Intellectual perfections and their abode is the Hereafter. As for the worldly abode, glimpses of it might be hoped for by souls stripped of human attachments. Then He commanded them – it is coordinated with 'honored' and what is coordinated with it. His statement: (coordinated with 'honored' and what is coordinated with it) means that He coordinated it after considering the coordination of its people upon it, because the command to reflect is dependent upon honor restricted to capability, not upon honor alone, since there is no obligation for the child. And he did not make it coordinated upon its people to preserve 'thumma' upon its original meaning; for the attachment of the command to reflect is not delayed by an interval from the mentioned capability. Indeed, the basis of obligation is the intellect as a faculty according to Shaykh al-Ashʿarī, and he did not interpret the word 'thumma' as mere gradation in ranks, since there is no reason for specification. His statement: He commanded them with that. His statement: (And the word 'thumma' upon its meaning, i.e., it is permissible to preserve it upon its original meaning based on the Ashʿarī school. The action and its effects. For we say: The derived (al-mustafād) in the second sense from the ranks of action also. I said: He intended by 'al-mustafād' that which they ruled that what comes after it by two ranks is ʿayn al-yaqīn and ḥaqq al-yaqīn derived in the first sense, not the second. For we do not concede that the simultaneous vision of intelligibles occurs before connection with the separate (intellect) and that which is judged to be the ultimate goal is the derived in the second sense. In summary, there is no conceivable rank in intellectual perfection itself higher than that all theoretical matters be observed actually in their true nature in a collective manner, whether it is said: this rank occurs before the other two ranks or after them, or that it is identical to one of them. And the superiority of the other two ranks over it, if conceded, is not by virtue of intellectual perfection itself, but by virtue of their inclusion of it along with another rank. So there is no problem.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's introductory statement about intellectual perfection. It begins by affirming that the ultimate abode of intellectual perfection is the Hereafter, though glimpses may occur in this world for souls freed from bodily attachments. The commentator then turns to the grammatical structure of the verse 'thumma amarahum' (then He commanded them), explaining that the command is coordinated with honor but conditioned on capability (ahlīyah). This is why there is no obligation for a child. The word 'thumma' (then) is preserved in its original sense implying temporal delay, not mere gradation, as per Ashʿarī doctrine. The discussion delves into the ranks of knowledge: ʿayn al-yaqīn (certainty by direct vision) and ḥaqq al-yaqīn (certainty by truth). The commentator refutes the idea that simultaneous vision of all intelligibles can be attained before separation from the body. The highest conceivable rank is the actual collective vision of all theoretical truths in their true nature, regardless of whether it occurs before or after the other ranks. Any superiority of the other ranks is not due to the essence of intellectual perfection but because they include additional aspects.

الكمال العلمي
الدار الآخرة
النفوس المجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think full intellectual perfection can be achieved in this world through philosophy or intellectual effort alone.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only glimpses are possible; full realization belongs to the Hereafter.

Checkpoint

A) The worldly abode
B) The Hereafter
C) Both equally
D) Only in this world

Key Takeaway
Intellectual perfection finds its complete realization in the Hereafter, with only glimpses in this world for the purified.

The Command to Reflect: Basis of Obligation
Grammatical and theological analysis of 'thumma amarahum'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari view that the command to reflect is dependent on capability and that the word 'thumma' preserves its literal meaning of temporal delay.

Core Idea
Obligation depends on capability; divine timing is real.

The commentator turns to a grammatical analysis of the verse 'thumma amarahum' to derive a theological point: the command to reflect comes after honoring humanity but is conditioned on capability. This explains why children are not obligated. The word 'thumma' is taken literally as temporal delay, reflecting Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the grammatical analysis
Read the Arabic passage where the commentator explains the coordination of 'thumma amarahum' with the act of honoring.
Pay attention to the phrase 'عطف على كرم'.
2
Understand why 'thumma' is not mere gradation
The commentator insists on preserving the original meaning of 'thumma' as temporal delay, not just logical gradation.
Contrast this with alternative interpretations.
3
Note the basis of obligation
According to al-Ash'ari, the basis is the intellect as a faculty (al-ʿaql bi-l-malakah), which gives capability at a certain time.
4
Connect to the broader theme
Understand how this grammatical point relates to the overall discussion of knowledge and perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المؤلف الكمالات العلمية ومستقره الدار الآخرة، وأما في الدار الدنيا فقد يرتجى لمحات منه للنفوس المجردة عن العلائق البشرية ) ثم أمرهم) عطف على كرم مع ما عطف عليه، قوله : ( عطف على كرم مع ما عطف عليه الخ ) يعني أنه عطف عليه بعد اعتبار عطف أهله عليه، لأن الأمر بالتفكر مترتب على التكريم المقيد بالأهلية، لا على التكريم فقط، إذ لا تكليف للصبي، ولم يجعله معطوفاً على أهله إبقاء لثم على معناها الأصلي؛ إذ ليس تعلق الأمر بالتفكر متأخراً بمهلة عن الأهلية المذكورة، فإن مناط التكليف هو العقل بالملكة عند الشيخ الأشعري، ولم يحمل كلمة ثم على مجرد التدرج في المراتب، إذ لا وجه لتخصيص، قوله : أمرهم بذلك . قوله : ( وكلمة ثم على معناها أي يجوز إبقاؤها على معناها الأصلي بناء على مذهب الأشاعرة . العمل وآثاره، لانا نقول المستفاد بالمعنى الثاني من مراتب العمل أيضاً قلت : أراد بالمستفاد الذي حكموا بأن ما بعده بمرتبتين عين اليقين وحق اليقين المستفاد بالمعنى الأول لا الثاني، إذ لا نسلم أن مشاهدة المعقولات دفعة يحصل قبل الاتصال بالمفارق والمحكوم عليه بأنه الغاية القصوى هو المستفاد بالمعنى الثاني، وبالجملة لا يتصور في نفس الكمال العلمي مرتبةً أعلى من أن تكون جميع النظريات على ما هي عليه مشاهدةً بالفعل على سبيل الاجتماع، سواء قيل: هذه المرتبة تحصل قبل المرتبتين الأخريين أو بعدهما، أو أنها عين إحداهما وأعلوية المرتبتين الأخريين منهما لو سلم فليس باعتبار نفس الكمال العلمي، بل باعتبار اشتمالهما عليهما وعلى مرتبة أخرى فلا إشكال .
The author's introduction: Intellectual perfections and their abode is the Hereafter. As for the worldly abode, glimpses of it might be hoped for by souls stripped of human attachments. Then He commanded them – it is coordinated with 'honored' and what is coordinated with it. His statement: (coordinated with 'honored' and what is coordinated with it) means that He coordinated it after considering the coordination of its people upon it, because the command to reflect is dependent upon honor restricted to capability, not upon honor alone, since there is no obligation for the child. And he did not make it coordinated upon its people to preserve 'thumma' upon its original meaning; for the attachment of the command to reflect is not delayed by an interval from the mentioned capability. Indeed, the basis of obligation is the intellect as a faculty according to Shaykh al-Ashʿarī, and he did not interpret the word 'thumma' as mere gradation in ranks, since there is no reason for specification. His statement: He commanded them with that. His statement: (And the word 'thumma' upon its meaning, i.e., it is permissible to preserve it upon its original meaning based on the Ashʿarī school. The action and its effects. For we say: The derived (al-mustafād) in the second sense from the ranks of action also. I said: He intended by 'al-mustafād' that which they ruled that what comes after it by two ranks is ʿayn al-yaqīn and ḥaqq al-yaqīn derived in the first sense, not the second. For we do not concede that the simultaneous vision of intelligibles occurs before connection with the separate (intellect) and that which is judged to be the ultimate goal is the derived in the second sense. In summary, there is no conceivable rank in intellectual perfection itself higher than that all theoretical matters be observed actually in their true nature in a collective manner, whether it is said: this rank occurs before the other two ranks or after them, or that it is identical to one of them. And the superiority of the other two ranks over it, if conceded, is not by virtue of intellectual perfection itself, but by virtue of their inclusion of it along with another rank. So there is no problem.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's introductory statement about intellectual perfection. It begins by affirming that the ultimate abode of intellectual perfection is the Hereafter, though glimpses may occur in this world for souls freed from bodily attachments. The commentator then turns to the grammatical structure of the verse 'thumma amarahum' (then He commanded them), explaining that the command is coordinated with honor but conditioned on capability (ahlīyah). This is why there is no obligation for a child. The word 'thumma' (then) is preserved in its original sense implying temporal delay, not mere gradation, as per Ashʿarī doctrine. The discussion delves into the ranks of knowledge: ʿayn al-yaqīn (certainty by direct vision) and ḥaqq al-yaqīn (certainty by truth). The commentator refutes the idea that simultaneous vision of all intelligibles can be attained before separation from the body. The highest conceivable rank is the actual collective vision of all theoretical truths in their true nature, regardless of whether it occurs before or after the other ranks. Any superiority of the other ranks is not due to the essence of intellectual perfection but because they include additional aspects.

الأهلية
التكليف
العقل بالملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the word 'thumma' in the Quran always implies mere logical order, not temporal sequence.
Correction: The Ash'ari position preserves the literal temporal meaning to emphasize the reality of divine commands unfolding in time.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is there no obligation for the child?
A) Because the child is not honored
B) Because the command is only for adults
C) Because obligation depends on capability
D) Because the word 'thumma' implies delay

Key Takeaway
The command to reflect is conditioned on capability, and the word 'thumma' in the verse preserves its literal temporal meaning.

?

Is there a truth you know but have not yet internalized because you keep postponing reflection? What will it take for you to stop delaying and confront it now?

The Highest Rank: Simultaneous Vision of All Intelligibles
Analysis of the highest rank of intellectual perfection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the highest conceivable rank of intellectual perfection as the simultaneous collective vision of all intelligibles in their true nature.

Core Idea
The ultimate intellectual perfection is the simultaneous vision of all truths.

The commentator addresses a potential confusion about the ranks of knowledge: ʿayn al-yaqīn and ḥaqq al-yaqīn. He argues that the highest rank is when all theoretical matters are observed actually in their true nature collectively, regardless of whether this occurs before or after the other ranks.

Objection and Answer on Common Intellectual Capacity
Responds to an objection about common intellectual capacity causing differentiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against common intellectual capacity as a basis for superiority is answered by distinguishing between the foundation of readiness and its variation in proximity and distance.

Core Idea
Common foundation, differing proximity determines virtue.

This passage presents an objection to the idea that a shared intellectual capacity can explain differences among individuals. The answer refines the concept: while the basic readiness is common, its degree of actualization varies, and that variation accounts for superiority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is short; pay attention to the objection and the answer.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
Find the part that begins with 'it was said' (qīla) – that is the objection. Then find the answer starting with 'And the answer is' (wa-ujība).
The objection questions how something common can cause differentiation.
3
Understand the key concepts
Focus on the terms 'asl al-isti‘dād' (foundation of readiness), 'qurb' (proximity), and 'bu‘d' (distance). The answer says that while the foundation is common, it varies in nearness to actualization, and virtue depends on that.
Proximity and distance are metaphors for degree of actualization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي استعداده لإدراك المعقولات قيل : الاستعداد لإدراك المعقولات لا يختلف في أفراد الإنسان فكيف يكون الأمر المشترك سببا لتفاضل بعض أفراد المشتركين على بعض؟ وأجيب بعد تسليم دلالة كلامه على أن كلاً مما ذكر في حيز من البيانية، سبب لتفاضل الأفراد بعضها على بعض، بل أصل الاستعداد وإن كان مشتركاً بين الجميع لكنه يختلف في الأفراد بحسب القرب والبعد والاختلاف في الفضيلة، إنما هو بحسب الاختلاف قرباً وبعداً .
His saying: (i.e., his readiness to perceive intelligibles) — it was said: 'Readiness to perceive intelligibles does not differ among individuals of mankind, so how can something common be a cause for the superiority of some individuals of the common group over others?' And the answer is, after conceding that his speech indicates that each of what was mentioned in the scope of the explanatory phrase is a cause for the superiority of individuals over others, [it is said] rather the foundation of readiness, although common to all, differs among individuals according to proximity and distance, and the difference in virtue is only according to difference in proximity and distance.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer from the commentary. The objection is: if the readiness to perceive intelligibles is common to all humans, how can it be the cause of superiority? The answer explains that while the foundation (asl) of readiness is common, it varies among individuals in terms of proximity and distance (i.e., closeness to actualization), and virtue depends on that difference.

Key Terms

معقولات
تفاضل

بعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If all humans share the same intellectual capacity, then they should all be equal in virtue.
Correction: The foundation of readiness is common, but its degree of actualization (proximity/distance) varies, leading to differences in virtue.

Checkpoint

C) By noting that the foundation of readiness varies in proximity and distance
D) By asserting that only acquired knowledge matters

Key Takeaway
The answer shows that common readiness does not entail equal actualization; variation in proximity accounts for differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objection in this lesson sounds reasonable: if everyone has the same potential, why are some superior? But the answer exposes an uncomfortable truth—your spiritual and intellectual virtues depend on your choices to draw near. When you look at your own life, are you content with being 'distant' in areas like patience, gratitude, or understanding? What would change if you truly believed that your current state is not fixed by your nature but is a reflection of your effort?

If the foundation of readiness is common but virtue is by proximity, then your current level of understanding is not an excuse but a responsibility. How would you respond if Allah asked you on the Day of Judgment: 'I gave you the same capacity as others, why did you not draw nearer?' Write a sincere answer as if you were standing before Him now.

The Certainty of Kalam's Proofs and Their Support by Revelation
Asserting that kalam's proofs are certain due to pure reason and reinforced by revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proofs of kalam are uniquely certain due to the agreement of pure reason and revelation, and how this distinguishes kalam from other sciences.

Core Idea
Kalam's proofs are certain because they combine pure reason with revelation, leaving no doubt.

This passage presents a bold claim: the proofs of kalam are the most reliable possible. It argues that this reliability stems from two sources: pure reason (free from imagination) and transmitted revelation (naql). Understanding this combination is key to appreciating why kalam is considered the supreme science of theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the author's full argument without trying to analyze it deeply yet.
The passage includes an objection and response, which is part of the argument.
2
Identify the two sources of certainty
Notice that the passage mentions two sources for the certainty of kalam's proofs: pure reason ('ṣarīḥ al-'aql') and transmitted evidence (naql). See how each contributes to reliability.
The term 'pure reason' means reason free from any taint of imagination (wahm).
3
Contrast with other sciences
The passage contrasts kalam with 'divine science' (ilāhī), where reason and revelation conflict. Understand why this conflict undermines their reliability.
This contrast highlights the unique position of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولاشك أنه إذا كان المعلوم أشرف كان العلم به أشرف مع أن موضوعه مقيد بحيثية تنبئ عن شرفه أيضاً (وغايته) أعني تلك السعادة المترتبة على الأمور الخمسة أشرف الغايات وأجداها نفعاً ودلائله يقينية يحكم بها بصحة مقدماتها، وحقية الصور العارضة لها صريح العقل بلا شائبة من الوهم ( وقد تأيدت ) تلك الدلائل ( بالنقل وهي شهادة العقل لها بصحتها مع تأيدها بالنقل هي الغاية في الوثاقة إذ لا يبقى شبهة في صحة الدليل الذي تطابق فيه العقل والنقل، قطعاً بخلاف دلائل العلم الإلهي فإن مخالفة النقل إياها شهادة عليها بأن أحكام عقولهم بها مأخوذة من أوهامهم، لا من صرائحها فلا وثوق بها أصلاً قوله: ( ودلائله يقينية إلخ) قيل عليه قد مر أن مسائل المخطئ من الكلام فكيف تكون دلائله يقينية وأجيب بالتخصيص؟ قوله: ( يحكم بها صريح العقل أي خالصه في الصحاح الصريح الخالص من كل شيء، وقد صرح بالضم صراحةً وصروحة فقوله: بلا شائبة من الوهم إشارة إلى أن المراد خالص العقل.
And there is no doubt that when the known is nobler, the knowledge of it is nobler, while its subject is restricted by a modality that indicates its honor also. And its goal — I mean that happiness resulting from the five things — is the noblest of goals and the most beneficial. And its proofs are certain, judged by the soundness of their premises and the correctness of the forms that occur to them by pure reason with no admixture of imagination. And those proofs have been strengthened by transmitted evidence. And it — i.e., the attestation of reason to its soundness together with its being strengthened by transmitted evidence — is the utmost in reliability, since no doubt remains in the validity of a proof in which reason and transmitted evidence agree, absolutely — unlike the proofs of divine science (ilāhī), for the opposition of transmitted evidence to them is a testimony that the judgments of their intellects regarding them are taken from their imaginations, not from their pure reason, so there is no reliance on them at all. His saying: 'and its proofs are certain, etc.' it was objected: it has already passed that the problems of the mistaken one are from kalam, so how can its proofs be certain? And he answered: by specification? His saying: 'judged by pure reason' i.e., the purest of it. In the Ṣiḥāḥ, 'ṣarīḥ' is the pure of everything, and it is said 'ṣaruḥa' with ḍamma, 'ṣarāḥatan' and 'ṣurūḥatan'. So his saying 'with no admixture of imagination' indicates that what is meant is pure reason.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a proof and defense of the certainty of kalam's proofs. It begins by stating that since the known (God) is the noblest, knowledge of it (kalam) is noblest, and the subject matter itself is qualified by a modality indicating its honor. The goal of kalam (happiness in both worlds) is the noblest and most beneficial. The proofs of kalam are certain: pure reason (without imagination) judges them, and they are also supported by transmitted evidence (naql). This dual support makes them the most reliable — no doubt remains. In contrast, the proofs of 'divine science' (likely philosophy or natural theology) are not supported by revelation; indeed revelation often opposes them, showing that their intellectual judgments stem from imagination, so they are unreliable. The passage then addresses an objection: it was previously said that the problems of the mistaken one (al-mukhti') are part of kalam (meaning some kalam arguments are erroneous), so how can all kalam proofs be certain? The answer: by specification — i.e., it is not all kalam proofs but those that are correctly reasoned and in agreement with revelation. The commentary on 'pure reason' clarifies that 'ṣarīḥ al-'aql' means the purest reason, free from any taint of imagination (wahm).

Key Terms
دلائله يقينية
صريح العقل
الوهم

The Theoretical Judgment and Its Necessity
Defines the theoretical judgment and argues for its necessity as a matter of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a theoretical judgment can be considered necessary when viewed as a matter of knowledge, and how it relates to essential accidents and intuitive knowledge.

Core Idea
The theoretical judgment is necessary because knowledge of essential attributes is intuitive.

In this passage, the author addresses a key question in epistemology: how can theoretical judgments, which require reasoning, be considered necessary? He explains that when we consider knowledge itself — specifically the affirmation of essential accidental properties — it becomes intuitive and thus necessary. This subtle distinction helps bridge the gap between theoretical and necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's argument that theoretical judgments should not be lumped with necessary ones, but then he refines the idea.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the terms 'theoretical judgment', 'necessary', 'essential accidents', and 'intuitive'. The commentary explains them.
3
Analyze the argument
The author states there is no basis for including theoretical judgments in necessary ones. Then he proposes that knowledge of essential accidents is intuitive, making it necessary in that context. How does this resolve the tension?
4
Reflect on the application
Consider why this distinction matters for the study of kalam. It shows that even theoretical knowledge has a firm foundation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم النظري فلا وجه لإدخالها في الضرورية، قلت : الظاهر في العلم إثبات العوارض الذاتية أي العلم بثبوتها وهي بهذا الاعتبار بديهية وبيان اللمية ليس من وظيفة العلم فهي ضرورية من حيث إنها مسألة العلم .
The theoretical judgment: there is no basis for including it in the necessary. I say: The apparent [view] regarding knowledge is the affirmation of essential accidental properties, i.e., knowledge of their existence, and in this respect it is intuitive, and the demonstration of the reason why is not a function of knowledge, so it is necessary insofar as it is a matter of knowledge.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the theoretical judgment (al-hukm al-nazari) and argues that it should not be classified as necessary (daruri). The author states that there is no justification for including theoretical judgments among necessary ones. He then explains that the apparent view concerning knowledge ('ilm) is that it involves establishing essential accidents (al-'awarid al-dhatiyya), meaning knowledge of their existence. In this respect, such knowledge is intuitive (badihi) and does not require a demonstration of cause (bayān al-limiyya), as that is not the function of knowledge itself. Therefore, the theoretical judgment is necessary when considered as a matter of knowledge, i.e., once it is known, it becomes self-evident in that specific sense.

Key Terms
حكم نظري
ضرورية
عوارض ذاتية
بديهية
بيان اللمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theoretical judgments are never necessary.
Correction: The passage shows that theoretical judgments, when considered as matters of knowledge (i.e., after being known), are necessary because knowledge of essential accidents is intuitive.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why can the theoretical judgment be considered necessary?
A) Because it is always self-evident
B) Because it is established by demonstration
C) Because as a matter of knowledge it is intuitive
D) Because it is part of the subject matter of kalam

Key Takeaway
A theoretical judgment, though requiring reasoning, becomes necessary when understood as intuitive knowledge of essential accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that knowledge of essential attributes is intuitive and necessary. Yet in your daily life, do you live as if these truths are theoretical and uncertain? What one step will you take today to move from reasoning about Allah's attributes to experiencing them as self-evident?
?
The author says 'the demonstration of the reason why is not a function of knowledge.' Are you seeking unnecessary proofs for matters that should already be certain? What change in your heart would occur if you stopped demanding explanations for His commands and simply submitted?
?
What essential accidental property of your own soul—such as your neediness or mortality—have you not yet affirmed as intuitive knowledge? How would your choices change if you truly knew it without doubt?

Knowledge as a Quality Necessitating Discrimination
Al-Jurjani defines knowledge and explains the first exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core definition of knowledge as a quality that necessitates discrimination, and how this excludes non-cognitive qualities and sensory perceptions.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a quality that makes its subject discriminate its object without possibility of error.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani presents the preferred definition of knowledge, emphasizing its nature as a quality that necessitates a discrimination that does not admit the opposite. He carefully distinguishes knowledge from other psychic and physical qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition components
Read the Arabic and translation of the definition. Identify the three key components: quality (صفة), locus (محل), and discrimination (تميز).
The definition is introduced as the seventh and chosen one.
2
Understand the first exclusion
Note that the term 'discrimination' excludes non-cognitive qualities like courage and blackness, because they give discrimination of the subject from others, but not of objects.
This shows that discrimination alone is not sufficient for knowledge.
3
See the exclusion of sensory perceptions
The definition further requires discrimination of objects among meanings (non-sensible), thus excluding external sense perceptions which discriminate concrete matters.
This will be further clarified later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - في تعريف مطلق العلم

السابع وهو المختار من تعريفاته لبراءته عما ذكر من الخلل في غيره وتناوله للتصور مع التصديق اليقيني ( إنه صفة ) أي أمر قائم بغيره (توجب) تلك الصفة (لمحلها ) وهو موصوفها (تميزاً) خرج به عن الحد ما عدا الإدراكات من الصفات النفسانية، كالشجاعة وغير النفسانية كالسواد مثلاً فإن هذه الصفات توجب لمحلها تميزاً عن غيرها، ضرورة أن الشجاع بشجاعته ممتاز عن الجبان وكذا الأسود بسواده متميز الأبيض، وأما الإدراكات فإنها توجب لمحالها تميزاً عن غيرها على قياس ما تقدم، وتوجب لها أيضاً تميزاً لمدركاتها عما عداها أي تجعلها بحيث تلاحظ مدركاتها وتميزها عما سواها بين المعاني أي ما ليس من الأعيان المحسوسة بالحواس الظاهرة، فيخرج به إدراكات هذه الحواس فإنها توجب تميزاً في الأمور العينية كما سيصرح به ( لا يحتمل النقيض ) أي لا يحتمل متعلق التمييز نقيض ذلك التمييز، وبهذا القيد خرج الظن والشك والوهم فإن متعلق التمييز الحاصل فيها يحتمل نقيضه بلا خفاء، وكذا خرج الجهل المركب لاحتمال أن يطلع في المستقبل صاحبه على ما في الواقع، فيزول عنه ما حكم به من الإيجاب أو السلب إلى نقيضه، وكذا خرج التقليد لأنه يزول بالتشكيك، ومحصله أن العلم صفة قائمة بمحل متعلقة بشيء توجب تلك الصفة إيجاباً عادياً كون محلها مميزاً للمتعلق تمييزاً لا يحتمل ذلك المتعلق نقيض ذلك التمييز، فلا بد من اعتبار المحل الذي هو العالم لأن التمييز
The Second Observatory: On the Definition of Absolute Knowledge. The seventh, which is the chosen one among its definitions because of its freedom from the defects mentioned in others and its inclusion of conception along with certain assent: that it is a quality, i.e., a thing subsisting in something else, which necessitates for its locus, which is its subject, discrimination. By this, what are other than cognitions among psychic qualities, like courage, and non-psychic ones, like blackness for example, are excluded from the definition; for these qualities necessitate for their locus a discrimination from others, necessarily because the courageous person by his courage is distinguished from the coward, and likewise the black by his blackness is distinguished from the white. As for cognitions, they necessitate for their loci discrimination from others analogously to what preceded, and they also necessitate for them a discrimination of their objects from what is other than them, i.e., they make them such that they observe their objects and distinguish them from what is other than them among meanings, i.e., what is not of sense-perceptible entities by the external senses. Thus, the perceptions of these senses are excluded by this, for they necessitate a discrimination in concrete matters, as will be explicitly stated. (It does not admit of the opposite), i.e., the object of the discrimination does not admit of the opposite of that discrimination. By this restriction, conjecture, doubt, and illusion are excluded, for the object of the discrimination occurring in them admits of its opposite without concealment. Likewise, compound ignorance is excluded because of the possibility that its possessor may in the future discover what is in reality, so what he judged of affirmation or negation passes away to its opposite. Likewise, imitation is excluded because it is removed by skepticism. The summary is that knowledge is a quality subsisting in a locus, related to something, which necessitates, as a customary exigency, that its locus be a discriminator of the object with a discrimination which that object does not admit of its opposite. So it is necessary to consider the locus, which is the knower, because discrimination...
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defines absolute knowledge (al-‘ilm) according to the preferred definition among several. It begins by stating that the seventh definition is chosen for its freedom from defects and for encompassing both conception (tasawwur) and certain assent (tasdiq yaqini). The definition states: knowledge is a quality (sifa) that necessitates for its locus (mahall)—which is its subject (mawsuf)—a discrimination (tamyiz). The author then explains what this excludes: non-cognitive qualities like courage and blackness, because they necessitate a discrimination of the subject from others, but they are not knowledge. Cognitions, however, necessitate both a discrimination of the subject from others and a discrimination of their objects from other objects. This discrimination is described as 'making it so that it observes its objects and distinguishes them from others among meanings (ma‘ani), i.e., non-sensible entities.' This excludes external sense perceptions, which discriminate concrete matters. The key restriction is that knowledge does not admit of the opposite (la yutammilu al-naqid): the object of the discrimination does not admit the opposite of that discrimination. This excludes conjecture (zann), doubt (shakk), and illusion (wahm), because their objects admit of the opposite. It also excludes compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) because its possessor may later discover the truth and lose his judgment. Imitation (taqlid) is excluded because it can be removed by skepticism. The summary reiterates that knowledge is a quality subsisting in a locus, related to something, which necessitates as a customary exigency that its locus be a discriminator of the object with a discrimination that the object does not admit of its opposite. The passage thus clarifies the precise boundaries of the concept of knowledge.

Key Terms
صفة
تميز
تصديق يقيني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any mental state that gives a clear distinction qualifies as knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows that even qualities like courage give a distinction, but they are not knowledge because they do not involve discrimination of objects among meanings.

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, which of the following is excluded because it gives only a discrimination of the subject from others, not of objects?
A) Certainty
B) Courage
C) Conception
D) Illusion

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is a quality that makes its subject discriminate its object intellectually, excluding non-cognitive and sensory discriminations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered whether your 'knowledge' of something is truly intellectual discrimination of its meaning, or merely a sensory familiarity? When was the last time you distinguished between these two in your own learning?
?
The definition excludes non-cognitive qualities like courage. Are there areas of your life where you mistake a personal quality (like bravery or kindness) for knowledge? How can you ensure that what you claim to know is actually based on intellectual discrimination?
?
This lesson distinguishes between sensory discrimination and intellectual discrimination. In your daily life, do you tend to rely more on sensory impressions or on abstract understanding? What change would it require to prioritize intellectual discrimination in your study of the Deen?

The Condition of Not Admitting the Opposite and Its Exclusions
Al-Jurjani applies the condition 'does not admit the opposite' to exclude five mental states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the condition that knowledge does not admit the opposite, and how it excludes conjecture, doubt, illusion, compound ignorance, and imitation.

Core Idea
Knowledge is irrevocably certain: its object cannot be imagined to be otherwise.

After defining knowledge as discrimination that does not admit the opposite, Al-Jurjani applies this condition to exclude several mental states that might be confused with knowledge. These include conjecture, doubt, illusion, compound ignorance, and imitation, each failing the test of irrevocable certainty.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition
Read the phrase 'لا يحتمل النقيض' and its explanation: the object of discrimination does not admit its opposite. This is the key condition that ensures certainty.
This condition is what distinguishes knowledge from mere opinion.
2
Identify the five excluded states
The passage lists five states: conjecture (ظن), doubt (شك), illusion (وهم), compound ignorance (جهل مركب), and imitation (تقليد). For each, note why its object admits the opposite.
Compound ignorance is excluded because the mistaken person may later discover the truth.
3
Review the summary definition
Read the summary sentence that encapsulates the full definition with the condition. This reinforces the complete concept of knowledge.
