Objection and Response on Conception's Opposite
Al-Sharh addresses an objection about conception's object and analyzes contradiction between concepts and judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection to conception having an opposite is resolved by distinguishing between conception by essence and conception by aspect.

Core Idea
Conception by essence excludes opposites; conception by aspect admits them.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a subtle objection: if conception only admits its own image, how can it have an opposite? The answer hinges on the distinction between conceiving a thing in its full reality (bi-l-kunh) and conceiving it under a particular description (bi-l-wajh). This distinction safeguards the definition of knowledge from the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objection: every conceived thing only admits its own image, so if conception has an opposite, its object cannot admit that opposite.
The objector assumes conception by essence.
2
Examine the answer
The answer distinguishes between conception by essence (bi-l-kunh) and conception by aspect (bi-l-wajh). Conception by essence does not admit opposites, but conception by aspect does, because the subject can be considered under different descriptions.
Example: human conceived as laughing can also be conceived as non-laughing.
3
Connect to the definition of knowledge
The definition of knowledge relies on the absence of an opposite for the object, which is valid when conception is by aspect.
This resolves the objection and preserves the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض أيضاً على ما ذكره الشارح بأن كل متصور لا يحتمل غير صورته الخاصة، فلو سلم إن للتصور نقيضاً فمتعلقه لا يحتمل نقيضه فلا معنى للبناء على عدم النقيض، وأجيب بأن هذا في المتصور بالكنه لا في المتصور بالوجه، فإنه لو فرض أن اللاضاحك بالفعل نقيض الضاحك بالفعل، فلا شك أن الإنسان المتصور بأحدهما يحتمل أن يتصور بالآخر على أن بناء شيء على شيء في الواقع لا ينافي وجود مبنى آخر له في التقدير، وبما ذكرنا من أن المتميز في التصديق هو الإثبات والنفي كما صرح به الشارح في الحواشي يندفع اعتراض الأستاذ بأن المراد من النقيض النقيض المصطلح كما يدل من
And an objection was also made against what the commentator mentioned: that every conceived thing does not admit other than its own particular image; if it were conceded that conception has an opposite, its relatum does not admit its opposite, so there is no meaning in basing it on the absence of the opposite. And it was answered that this is regarding the conceived thing by essence (bi-l-kunh), not by aspect (bi-l-wajh). For if it were assumed that the non-laughing actually is the opposite of the laughing actually, there is no doubt that the human conceived by one of them admits being conceived by the other, considering that building something on something in reality does not preclude another basis for it in estimation. And based on what we mentioned that what is distinguished in assent is affirmation and negation, as the commentator stated in his glosses, the objection of the master (al-ustādh) is repelled: that what is meant by opposite is the technical opposite, as indicated by
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection to the definition of knowledge. The objector argues: every conceived thing only admits its own specific image; even if we grant that conception has an opposite, the object of conception cannot admit that opposite, so it is meaningless to base the definition on the absence of an opposite for the object. The response distinguishes between two modes of conception: conception by essence (bi-l-kunh) and conception by aspect (bi-l-wajh). In conception by essence, the object is considered in its full quiddity, and indeed it cannot admit an opposite. But in conception by aspect, the object is considered under a particular attribute; for example, 'human' conceived as 'laughing' can be re-conceived as 'non-laughing' because the opposite 'non-laughing' refers to the same subject under a different description. This shows that the definition's reliance on the absence of an opposite is justified when conception is taken by aspect. The commentator then notes that this aligns with the view that what is distinguished in assent is affirmation and negation, thereby repelling another objection that the term 'opposite' might not be the technical logical opposite.

Key Terms
المتصور بالكنه
المتصور بالوجه
النقيض
التمييز
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The object of conception cannot have an opposite at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that conception by aspect can have an opposite; the object under a particular description admits its opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given in the passage, when does a conceived thing admit an opposite?
A) When it is conceived by essence
B) When it is conceived by aspect
C) When it is a simple concept
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The distinction between conception by essence and by aspect resolves the objection that conception's object cannot admit an opposite, preserving the definition of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own knowledge of Allah, do you tend to conceive of His attributes by essence or by aspect? How might a confusion between these two modes lead to a misunderstanding of divine unity? Commit to studying the attributes of Allah with this distinction in mind, and write down one attribute you will reflect on this week.
?
Think of a time when you rejected an Islamic teaching because it seemed contradictory to another belief you held. Could it be that you were conceiving both by essence rather than by aspect? Take a specific example and consider how the distinction from this lesson could have helped you reconcile them. What step will you take to revisit that topic?
?
This lesson shows that a minor confusion about the meaning of 'opposite' could undermine an entire definition. In your life, have you ever held a wrong belief because you assumed a term meant something else? Today, choose one Islamic concept you think you understand and search for its technical definition to ensure your conception is accurate.

The Problem of Contradiction in Perceptions
First investigation: No contradiction between perceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first investigation from al-Sharh: why the claim that affirmation and negation are contradictory is challenged by cases of simple ignorance and doubt.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation can both be absent, challenging their status as true contradictories.

Al-Sharh presents two outstanding problems (baḥthān) that arise from the earlier discussion. The first problem questions whether perceptions (idrākāt) can truly be contradictory. The author points to simple ignorance and doubt as cases where both affirmation and negation are absent, yet contradictories cannot both be absent. This forces us to reconsider the very meaning of contradiction in kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the first investigation once without analyzing. Then read its translation.
Notice the logical structure: observation, rule, question.
2
Identify the problem
The problem is that in simple ignorance and doubt, both affirmation and negation are absent. But for true contradictories, both cannot be absent.
This challenges the earlier claim that negation and affirmation are contradictories.
3
Understand the logical rule
Recall that in classical logic, two contradictory propositions cannot both be false; one must be true and the other false.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Ask yourself: if both can be absent, does that mean perception does not follow the same logical rules as external propositions?
This leads to deeper inquiry into the nature of mental judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي ها هنا بحثان الأول أنه لا تناقض بين الإدراكات الا يرى أن الإيجاب والسلب مرتفعان عند الجهل البسيط والشك، والمتناقضان لا يصح ارتفاعهما، فكيف يقال : إن النفي والإثبات متناقضان ؟ الثاني : أنه إن أريد بما به التمييز الذي جعل مجازاً عنه نفس الصفة لم يصح قوله صفة توجب تمييزاً، إذ الشيء لا يوجب نفسه، وإن اكتفى بالمغايرة الاعتبارية كان مخالفا لما نقل عنه في الحواشي من نقيض التمييز لا نقيض الصفة أو المتعلق وإن أريد أمر آخر يلزم تحقق أمور ثلاثة الصفة والتمييز، وشيء ثالث بينهما به التميز ولا يخفى بطلانه، اللهم إلا أن يجاب عن أصل الاعتراض بمنع كون الإيجاب والسلب من قبيل الكيفيات فتأمل .
There remain here two investigations: First, that there is no contradiction between perceptions; do you not see that affirmation and negation are absent in simple ignorance and doubt, yet two contradictories cannot both be absent, so how can it be said that negation and affirmation are contradictories? Second, if by 'that which distinguishes' (mā bihi al-tamyīz), which is used metonymically for it, the very attribute is intended, his statement 'an attribute that necessitates a distinction' is unsound, because a thing does not necessitate itself. And if one is satisfied with conceptual difference (al-mughāyara al-iʿtibāriyya), that contradicts what is transmitted from him in the glosses: that the opposite of the distinction, not the opposite of the attribute or the relatum, [is what matters]. And if something else is intended, then three things must exist: the attribute, the distinction, and a third thing between them by which the distinction occurs, whose unsoundness is not hidden. Unless one responds to the original objection by denying that affirmation and negation belong to the category of qualities (kayfiyyāt); so reflect.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage concludes the commentary with two outstanding problems (baḥthān). The first problem questions whether perception (idrāk) can truly be contradictory. The author notes that in simple ignorance and doubt, both affirmation and negation are absent—neither is present—but classical contradictory propositions cannot both be absent (they must have one true, one false). Therefore, how can we claim that negation and affirmation are contradictory when they can be co-absent? This challenges the standard logical rule that contradictory opposites cannot both be false. The second problem concerns the expression 'that which distinguishes' (mā bihi al-tamyīz). If this metonymically refers to the attribute itself (the mental image), then saying 'an attribute that necessitates a distinction' is problematic because a thing cannot necessitate itself (it would be circular). If we accept a merely conceptual distinction (al-mughāyara al-iʿtibāriyya) between the attribute and the distinction, that contradicts the commentator's own earlier gloss that 'the opposite of the distinction is what matters, not the opposite of the attribute or the relatum'. If we posit a third entity besides the attribute and the distinction, that would require three things, which is unacceptable. The author suggests a possible way out: to deny that affirmation and negation are in the category of quality (kayfiyya), which would change the logical analysis. This leaves the matter open for reflection.

Key Terms
الإدراكات
الجهل البسيط
الشك
المتناقضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That affirmation and negation are always contradictory logical opposites, even in mental perceptions.
Correction: The text shows that in states of simple ignorance and doubt, both can be absent, which would be impossible if they were true contradictories. Thus, their contradictory status is questioned.

Checkpoint
According to the first investigation, what problem is raised about the claim that negation and affirmation are contradictory?
A) They cannot both be true at the same time
B) They can both be absent in simple ignorance and doubt
C) They are not real opposites in the mind
D) The definition of contradiction does not apply to mental acts

Key Takeaway
The first investigation shows that the logical rule of contradiction does not apply straightforwardly to mental perceptions, as seen in simple ignorance and doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider an area of your life where you are in a state of simple ignorance or doubt about a religious truth. How does this passage challenge you to not merely be content with ignorance but to seek certainty? What step will you take today to move from ignorance to knowledge?
?
The author points out that in doubt, both affirmation and negation are absent. Think of a doubt you have about a matter of faith. Do you recognize that this doubt is not a third option but a lack of conviction? How will you resolve this doubt using the tools of reason and revelation?
?
This lesson shows that the law of contradiction does not apply to perceptions in the same way. How does this affect your understanding of theological disagreements? Can you think of a dispute where both sides might be operating from a state of ignorance rather than genuine contradiction? How does this insight call you to humility in debate?

The Nature of Distinction (Mā bihi al-Tamyīz)
Second investigation: The definition of 'that which distinguishes'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second investigation: the problem of defining 'that which distinguishes' and the various interpretations and their difficulties.

Core Idea
Defining 'that which distinguishes' leads to problems of self-reference, circularity, and regress.

The second investigation tackles the expression 'that which distinguishes' (mā bihi al-tamyīz) used in earlier definitions. The author examines three possible interpretations: taking it as the attribute itself, accepting a merely conceptual difference, or positing a third entity. Each has its own logical difficulties, forcing us to consider whether the category of quality (kayfiyya) applies to affirmation and negation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second investigation
Read the Arabic passage for the second investigation and its translation. Notice the three conditional statements.
2
Identify the three interpretations
The text considers three possibilities for interpreting 'that which distinguishes': (1) the attribute itself, (2) conceptual difference, (3) a third entity.
3
Understand the problem with each
For each interpretation, the author points out a specific logical flaw: circularity, contradiction with earlier gloss, or infinite regress.
4
Reflect on the proposed solution
The author suggests a possible way out: denying that affirmation and negation are qualities (kayfiyyāt). Consider why this might help.
This is left for the reader's reflection (فتأمل).

Concepts Are Always True; Error Is in Judgment
Defense against the objection that some concepts are non-conforming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conceptual knowledge is always conforming and why error is located only in judgment, not in the concept itself.

Core Idea
Concepts are always true to their meaning; error lies in judgment, not in conception.

A common objection to the claim that all conceptual knowledge is knowledge is that some concepts seem false. In this passage, the author shows that concepts themselves are never false—they only represent their meanings. The error occurs when the mind judges that a concept corresponds to a particular external object. This lesson clarifies that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The passage begins with an objection: if all concepts are knowledge, then non-conforming concepts would be knowledge too. Notice how the author sets up the problem.
2
Follow the response
The author denies that concepts can be non-conforming. Read the explanation and the example of seeing a phantom. Identify the key distinction: concept vs. judgment.
The example is crucial: the mental image of a human is a correct concept of 'human'; the error is in judging that it corresponds to the seen object.
3
Apply the conclusion
The author concludes that all concepts conform to their objects, whether existent or not. Reflect on why this matters for epistemology.
This shows that conceptual knowledge is always sound; error only enters with judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : فعلى هذا جميع التصورات علم مع أن بعضها غير مطابق، لأنا نقول : لا يوصف التصور بعدم المطابقة أصلاً ، فإنا إذا رأينا من بعيد شبحاً هو حجر مثلاً، وحصل منه في أذهاننا صورة إنسان فتلك الصورة صورة للإنسان، وعلم تصوري به والخطأ إنما هو في حكم العقل بأن هذه الصورة للشبح المرئي، فالتصورات كلها مطابقة لما تصورات له موجوداً كان أو معدوماً، ممكناً كان أو ممتنعاً وعدم المطابقة في أحكام العقل المقارنة لتلك التصورات فلا إشكال
It cannot be said: on this basis all concepts are knowledge even though some of them are non-conforming, for we say: the concept is not described by non-conformity at all. For if we see from a distance a phantom that is, for example, a stone, and from it there occurs in our minds an image of a human, then that image is an image of a human and a conceptual knowledge of it, and the error is only in the judgment of the mind that this image is of the seen phantom. Thus all concepts are conforming to that which they are concepts of, whether existent or non-existent, possible or impossible, and non-conformity is in the judgments of the mind that accompany those concepts, so there is no problem.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a defense against an objection. The objection claims that if conceptual knowledge is defined as something that does not admit negativity, then all concepts would be knowledge even though some are non-conforming. The author answers by explaining that concepts are never described as non-conforming in themselves. An example: when we see a phantom from afar that is actually a stone, the image in our mind is a human image; that image is a true concept of a human. The error is not in the concept but in the mind's judgment that this image corresponds to the seen object. Therefore all concepts are conforming to their objects (whether existent or non-existent, possible or impossible) and non-conformity only occurs in the accompanying judgments. Thus the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
تصور
مطابقة
حكم العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An erroneous mental image is a non-conforming concept.
Correction: The image itself is a correct concept of a universal; error is in the judgment applying it to a particular.

Checkpoint

A) In the conceptual image itself
B) In the judgment that the image corresponds to the seen object
C) In the senses
D) In the object itself

Key Takeaway
Concepts are always true to their objects; error arises only in the mind's judgment, not in the concept itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting an Objection: The Common Denominator Solution
The author refutes the objection about the mountain and stone/gold using a common subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that there is no probability of the opposite for the mountain by identifying a common subject that allows both stone and gold to be possible.

Core Idea
Probability of the opposite is preserved by taking the subject as a common denominator.

In this passage, the author confronts an objection that seems to challenge the possibility of opposite judgments. The objection claims that because a mountain is composed of stone, it cannot possibly be gold, eliminating any probability of the opposite. The author's response is both clever and foundational: he shifts the subject from the specific composition to a common denominator, such as 'the occupier of the place.' This move preserves the logical possibility of the opposite and shows a key method in kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: the objection is presented, then the response begins with 'قلنا'.
2
Identify the problem
The objection says that because the mountain is composed of stone, it cannot be gold, so there is no probability of the opposite.
3
Find the solution
The author uses 'قدر مشترك' (common denominator) like 'the occupier of the place' to allow both possibilities without contradiction.
4
Reflect on the method
Understand that this is a common kalam technique to avoid contradictions and maintain contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتركب منه الحجر لا يجوز أن يتركب منه الذهب، قلنا : نحن نعلم بالعادة أن الشاغل لذلك المكان المخصوص مثلاً حجر مع جواز أن يكون المختار قد أعدمه، وأوجد بدله ذهباً. قوله : (قلنا : نحن نعلم بالعادة إلخ ) يريد دفع ما يقال من أن ما تركب منه الجبل إذا كان مخالفاً في الحقيقة لا تركب منه الذهب، لم يكن هناك موضوع معين يصح أن يتوارد عليه هذان الوصفان المتنافيان فليس الحكم على الجبل بأحدهما محتملا لنقيضه نعم يمكن أن يعدم الجبل، ويوجد الذهب مكانه فيختلف الموضوع، فلا تنافي بين الحكمين فلا احتمال للنقيض، ووجه الدفع أنا نأخذ الموضوع ما هو قدر مشترك بينهما كالشاغل للمكان الفلاني.
The stone from which the mountain is composed cannot be composed of gold. We said: We know by custom that the occupier of that specific place, for example, is stone, while it is possible that the Free Agent may have annihilated it and brought gold in its place. His statement: (We said: We know by custom, etc.) intends to refute what is said: that if that from which the mountain is composed is different in reality, then gold is not composed from it, and there is no specific subject upon which these two contradictory descriptions can be applied; so the judgment on the mountain by one of them is not probable of its opposite. Rather, the mountain can be annihilated and gold brought in its place, so the subject differs; thus there is no contradiction between the two judgments, and no probability of the opposite. The refutation is that we take the subject as a common denominator between them, like the occupier of that place.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The objection argues that if the mountain is composed of stone, it cannot be gold because the subject is specific; thus there is no probability of the opposite. The answer (in 'قلنا') is that we know by custom that the occupier of the space is stone, but it is possible for God to replace it with gold. The commentary then explains that the refutation involves taking the subject as a common denominator (قدر مشترك) like 'the occupier of the place' rather than the specific mountain, so the opposite remains possible and the contradiction is avoided.

Key Terms
قدر مشترك
موضوع
النقيض
العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of the opposite refers to an essential impossibility.
Correction: The impossibility is only actual, not essential; the opposite remains logically possible through a change in the specific subject but with a common denominator.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the objection about the mountain being stone and gold?
A) By denying the possibility of the opposite
B) By taking the subject as the common denominator (the occupier of the space)
C) By asserting that the mountain can change its essence
D) By distinguishing between two kinds of probability

Key Takeaway
The probability of the opposite is preserved by identifying a common subject that can receive both descriptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an intellectual objection to a core belief, do you instinctively defend the specific formulation, or do you seek a broader common ground that preserves both reason and faith? The method here is to find a common denominator. Where in your own thinking can you apply this approach today?
?
The passage teaches that what is impossible by nature can be possible by divine power. Have you unconsciously limited God's ability in your own life by relying on 'custom' alone? Identify one area where you've said 'this cannot happen' and commit to rephrasing it as 'it is possible if God wills.' How would that change your du'a?
?
This refutation requires distinguishing between the subject's essence and its accidental descriptions. When you face a contradiction in your understanding of a religious text, do you assume one side must be false, or are you willing to find a deeper unity? Will you commit to approaching future theological challenges with the humility to seek a common denominator?

The First Division: Conception vs. Judgment
Defines conception as knowledge devoid of judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the primary division of knowledge is into conception (taṣawwur) and assent (taṣdīq), based solely on whether a judgment (ḥukm) is present.

Core Idea
The dividing line between conception and judgment is the presence or absence of a judgment.

Knowledge, in its basic sense of perception, is divided into two fundamental categories. The first and most important division is based on whether that perception includes a judgment—an affirmation or negation of a relation. This passage from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif lays out this division clearly, using examples from everyday language and logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of conception
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the condition 'if it is devoid of judgment' (khala ʿan al-ḥukm). Notice that this is the defining characteristic of conception.
2
Identify the types of known objects
The passage gives four categories of what can be known: (1) no relation, (2) qualifying relation, (3) constructive relation, (4) predicative relation. Observe that all are classified as conception if no judgment is made.
These categories show that conception covers a wide range, not just simple ideas.
3
Understand the example of doubt
The example of doubt about Zayd illustrates conception: you have the concepts of Zayd and standing, but you do not judge whether he is standing or not.
This shows that doubt is a state of conception, not assent.
4
Reflect on the debate
The commentary mentions a disagreement between al-Rāzī and al-Taftāzānī about whether 'devoid of judgment' is a condition or simply the absence of judgment. This deepens your understanding of the definition.
You are not required to resolve this debate, but it shows the scholarly precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث في أقسام العلم وفيه مقاصد ]
[ المقصد الأول أنه إن خلا عن الحكم ]
أي العلم بمعنى الإدراك مطلقاً ليتناول الظنيات أيضاً، أو بالمعنى المفسر بالحد المختار إن خلا عن (الحكم أي إيقاع النسبة، أو انتزاعها ( فتصور) سواء كان المعلوم مما لا نسبة فيه أصلاً، كالإنسان أو فيه نسبة تقييدية كالحيوان الناطق، أو إنشائية كقولك اضرب أو نسبة خبرية، لم يحكم بأحد طرفيها كما إذا شككت في زيد
قوله : ( ليتناول الظنيات أراد بالظن ها هنا ما يقابل اليقين كما سيجيء في مبحث تعريف النظر، فيشمل جيمع التصديقات الغير اليقينية .
قوله : ( أو بالمعنى المفسر) ومعنى الخلو وعدمه على تقدير كونه صفة ذات تعلق أن لا يوجب الحكم، ويوجبه وعلى تقدير كونه نفس التعلق أن لا يكون نفس الحكم، وأن يكون نفسه لأن التميز عبارة عن النفي والإثبات وهو الحكم.

قوله : (أو نسبة خبرية) أي نسبة مشعرة بنسبة ما خارجية .

The third observation on the divisions of knowledge, and it contains aims. [The first aim: that if it is devoid of judgment] i.e., knowledge in the sense of perception absolutely, so that it includes probabilistic things too, or in the sense interpreted by the chosen definition: if it is devoid of judgment, i.e., positing a relation or extracting it, then it is conception, whether the known thing has no relation at all, like 'human', or has a qualifying relation like 'rational animal', or a constructive relation like 'strike!', or a predicative relation, without judging one of its two sides, as when you doubt about Zayd. His saying: 'to include the probabilistic things' – he intends by 'probabilistic' here that which is opposite to certainty, as will come in the discussion on the definition of consideration, so it includes all non-certain assents. His saying: 'or by the interpreted meaning' – the meaning of being devoid or not, on the supposition that it is a quality with attachment, is that it does not necessitate the judgment, or does necessitate it; and on the supposition that it is the attachment itself, that it is not the judgment itself, and that it is itself, because distinction (tamyīz) is expression of negation and affirmation, which is judgment. His saying: 'if it is devoid of judgment, etc.' – if he intends by that that being devoid of judgment is considered in it, then it follows that what this category applies to is not considered in assent, since the conceptions of the terms considered in it are only called absolute conception, not conception restricted by absence of judgment, because when we conceive the terms we are heedless of judgment and its absence, as conscience witnesses. And if he intends that judgment is not considered in it, whether absence of judgment is considered or not, then it implies dividing a thing into itself and other. Al-Muḥaqqiq al-Rāzī chose the first, and al-ʿAllāma al-Taftāzānī chose the second, and both are unsound, and God knows best the secrets of His servants' speech. His saying: 'or constructive' – i.e., the relation that indicates an external relation. His saying: 'or predicative relation' – i.e., a relation indicating an external relation. His saying: 'to include the probabilistic things' – he did not address other than probabilistic of non-certain assents, like compound ignorance and others, despite absolute perception including them, because none of them is sought by consideration in that respect, as will come in the fifth observation of this station, and this suffices as a reason for not addressing them. His saying: 'if it is devoid of (judgment' – he intended by being devoid of judgment, on the supposition that knowledge is interpreted by the chosen definition, that it does not necessitate it. His saying: 'or predicative relation' – the application of 'predicative relation' to mere judgmental relation is unconventional, because it is possible that its counterpart is interrogative, and you are aware that he only applies it to the judgmental relation in what is other than constructs, and as for those which have it, they are already included in his saying 'or constructive', so there is no problem. His saying: 'as when you doubt, etc.' – in it is that he has excluded doubt from the chosen definition of knowledge, so how...
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage begins the third observation on the divisions of knowledge. It states the first aim: that if knowledge (in the sense of perception, whether absolute or defined by the chosen definition) is devoid of judgment (ḥukm), then it is called conception (taṣawwur). Judgment is defined as positing a relation (īqāʿ al-nisba) or extracting it. The known object may have no relation (like 'human'), a qualifying relation (like 'rational animal'), a constructive relation (like 'strike!'), or a predicative relation, without affirming or denying one side. The example given is doubt about Zayd. The commentary then explains that 'probabilistic things' (ẓanniyyāt) here means whatever is opposite to certainty, thus including all non-certain assents. It discusses the interpretation of 'devoid' depending on whether knowledge is a quality with attachment or the attachment itself. A key point is the debate: if 'devoid of judgment' is considered a condition, then the conception that is part of assent would not be true conception, because when we conceive terms we are heedless of judgment. If judgment is simply not considered, that leads to dividing a thing into itself and other. Al-Rāzī and al-Taftāzānī took different positions, both deemed unsound. Further comments clarify why only probabilistic things are mentioned (not compound ignorance) because they are not sought in valid consideration. The passage ends with a note that the chosen definition excludes doubt, and the argument continues on the next page.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division into conception and judgment is based on certainty (yaqīn) vs. doubt (shakk).
Correction: The division is based solely on the presence or absence of a judgment (ḥukm), not on certainty. Even a certain belief like 'the sky is blue' is assent because it contains a judgment. Doubt is an example of conception because no judgment is made.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the criterion for classifying something as conception (taṣawwur) rather than assent (taṣdīq)?
A) The absence of any relation in the known object.
B) The absence of judgment about the relation.
C) The presence of doubt.
D) The presence of a qualifying relation.

Key Takeaway
The first division of knowledge is between conception (no judgment) and assent (judgment present), regardless of the nature of the known object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you often hold beliefs without ever affirming or denying them—just drifting through concepts? How has that affected your understanding of truth?
?
Consider the difference between merely knowing about Allah (conception) and truly affirming His attributes with your heart (assent). Which do you find yourself doing most, and what step can you take today to move from conception to genuine assent?
?
This lesson shows that even doubt about a relation (e.g., 'Is Zayd standing?') is still conception because no judgment is made. Where in your life do you remain in doubt, refusing to judge? What would it cost you to make a judgment, and what would it cost you not to?

The Definition of Tasawwur: Knowledge Free from Judgment
Sanusi defines tasawwur and expands on its scope with glosses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tasawwur (conception) as knowledge that is free from judgment, the types of relations it can involve, and why it includes conjectural beliefs.

Core Idea
Conception is perception without judgment — the first division of knowledge.

Sanusi begins his discussion of the divisions of knowledge by defining tasawwur, the foundation of all conceptual understanding. He clarifies that 'knowledge' here means absolute perception, not limited to certainty, so that even uncertain beliefs qualify as conception. This passage lays the groundwork for distinguishing conception from assent (tasdiq).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the dense commentary style.
The passage includes many glosses (قوله) that clarify points.
2
Identify the core definition
Find where the author states that if knowledge is free from judgment, it is tasawwur. Note the key phrase 'خلا عن الحكم'.
3
Examine the types of relations
Look at the examples: human (no relation), rational animal (restrictive), strike! (constructive), and the informative relation. Understand how each fits the definition.
4
Understand the inclusion of zanniyyat
The author explains that 'knowledge' here includes conjectural beliefs (zanniyat) to cover all non-certain assents. Note the distinction between zann and yaqin.
5
Consider the nuances on 'free from judgment'
The glosses discuss two interpretations: whether 'free from judgment' is a condition of the conception or simply means judgment is absent. Razi and Taftazani differed, both deemed awkward.

قوله : ( أو نسبة خبرية ) أي نسبة مشعرة بنسبة ما خارجية

قوله : ( إن خلا عن الحكم ) أراد بالخلو عن الحكم على تقدير أن يفسر العلم بالحد المختار عدم إيجابه إياه

The third observation: on the divisions of knowledge, and it contains sections. The first section: that if [knowledge] is free from judgment, i.e., knowledge in the sense of perception absolutely, to include zanniyyat (conjectural beliefs) as well, or according to the meaning interpreted by the chosen definition, if it is free from judgment (i.e., the positing of a relation or its abstraction) then it is tasawwur (conception), whether the known is something that has no relation at all, like 'human', or has a restrictive relation like 'rational animal', or a constructive relation like 'strike!', or an informative relation, not judging one of its two sides, as when you doubt whether Zayd is standing, then all of these are knowledges free from the mentioned judgment. His saying: 'to include zanniyyat' — he intended by zann here what is opposite to yaqin (certainty), as will come in the investigation of the definition of nazar (speculation), so it includes all non-certain tasdiqat (assents). His saying: 'or according to the interpreted meaning' — and the meaning of being free [from judgment] and not being free, on the assumption that it is a quality that has attachment, is that it does not necessitate the judgment, and necessitates it; and on the assumption that it is the attachment itself, is that it is not the judgment itself, and that it is itself, because discrimination (tamyiz) is expression of negation and affirmation, and that is the judgment. His saying: 'if it is free from judgment etc.' — if he intended by it that being free from judgment is considered in it, then it would follow that what this division applies to is not considered in tasdiq, necessarily because the tasawwurat (conceptions) of the extremes considered in it are only validly called absolute conception, not the conception restricted by the absence of judgment, because when we conceive the extremes we are heedless of judgment and its absence, as conscience witnesses; and if he intended by it that judgment is not considered in it, whether the absence of judgment is considered or not, then the division of a thing into itself and other follows, and al-Muhaqqiq al-Razi chose the first, and al-Allama al-Taftazani chose the second, and both are awkward, and God knows best the secrets of His servants' speech. His saying: 'or constructive' — i.e., the relation that indicates an external relation. His saying: 'or informative relation' — i.e., a relation indicating some external relation. His saying: 'to include zanniyyat' — he did not address besides zanniyyat of the non-certain tasdiqat, like compound ignorance and others, despite absolute perception including them, because none of them is sought by speculation as such, as will come in the fifth observation of this stance, and this suffices as a reason for not addressing them. His saying: 'if it is free from judgment' — he intended by freedom from judgment, on the assumption that knowledge is interpreted by the chosen definition, the absence of necessitating it. His saying: 'or informative relation' — before, the application of 'informative relation' to mere hukmi relation is unconventional because it could be interrogative, and you are aware that he only applied it to the hukmi relation in what is other than constructions, and as for those that are [constructions], they are already included in his saying 'or constructive', so there is no objection. His saying: 'as when you doubt etc.' — there is in it that he has excluded doubt from the definition of the chosen knowledge, so how... standing, then all of these are knowledges free from the mentioned judgment.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the first part of the division of knowledge: tasawwur (conception) occurs when knowledge is free from judgment. The commentator explains that 'knowledge' here means absolute perception, not only certain knowledge, so as to include zanniyyat (conjectural beliefs). 'Free from judgment' means not involving the positing (إيقاع) or abstraction (انتزاع) of a relation. The known can be without relation (like 'human'), with a restrictive relation (like 'rational animal'), a constructive relation (like the imperative 'strike!'), or an informative relation (like a proposition); as long as one does not affirm or deny that relation (e.g., doubting whether Zayd is standing), it is conception. Several glosses clarify details: the first gloss explains that 'zann' here means non-certainty. The second gloss discusses two interpretations of 'free from judgment' depending on whether judgment is a quality or the very attachment. The third gloss examines an objection: if 'free from judgment' is taken as a condition, then the term 'tasawwur' would not apply to conceptions used in tasdiq (since they are not qualified by absence of judgment); two solutions are noted (al-Razi vs. al-Taftazani), both deemed awkward. The fourth and fifth glosses define 'constructive' and 'informative' relations. The sixth gloss explains why only zanniyyat are mentioned, not other non-certain tasdiqat like compound ignorance, because they are not sought by speculation. The seventh gloss repeats the meaning of 'free from judgment' under the chosen definition. The eighth gloss addresses the unusual use of 'informative relation' for non-propositional relations, noting no harm. The ninth gloss points out a contradiction: doubt was excluded from the definition of knowledge, but here it appears as an example of conception. The passage concludes that all such cases are knowledges free from judgment.

خلا عن الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that tasawwur includes only simple concepts without any relation, but the passage shows it also includes relations as long as no judgment is made.
Correction: Tasawwur can involve relations (restrictive, constructive, informative) as long as one does not affirm or deny any relation. The key is freedom from judgment, not absence of relation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of tasawwur?
A) Believing with certainty that the world is created.
B) Doubting whether Zayd is standing.
C) Affirming that fire is hot.
D) Denying that the sky is blue.

Key Takeaway
Tasawwur is knowledge perceived without any judgment, whether the known has a relation or not, and it includes uncertain beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that tasawwur includes cases like doubting whether Zayd is standing. How many of your own beliefs are actually mere conceptions you have never truly judged? Choose one area (creed, daily habit, opinion) where you will actively apply nazar (speculation) to move from conception to genuine assent, starting today.
?
The author notes that when we conceive the extremes (subject and predicate) in a proposition, we are often heedless of judgment. Can you identify that moment as a tasawwur you mistook for tasdiq? What would it take for you to seek certainty (yaqin) before acting on such matters?

The Three Views on Tasdiq: Definition and Debate
The commentator defines tasdiq and critiques the three main views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main positions on the nature of tasdiq (assent) and the commentator's preferred definition as perception of a judgment.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is not the judgment itself but the perception that a relation holds or not.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif introduces the definition of tasdiq, a key concept in Islamic epistemology. The commentator presents three views: tasdiq as judgment itself (early scholars), as perception accompanied by judgment (later scholars), and as a composite of judgment and conceptions (Imam al-Razi). The commentator then argues for his own view, based on introspection.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Let the author's rhythm come through.
2
Identify the three views
Find the three positions on tasdiq: early scholars (judgment itself), later scholars (perception accompanied by judgment), and Imam al-Razi (composite of judgment and conceptions).
Notice how each is introduced: 'لا نفس الحكم', 'الإدراك المقارن', 'المجموع المركب'.
3
Examine the commentator's own view
The commentator says: 'if judgment is made a perception... then knowledge that is a judgment (affirming/denying) is tasdiq.' This makes tasdiq a type of perception.
He bases this on introspection ('كما يشهد به رجوعك إلى وجدانك').
4
Understand why the composite view is rejected
The commentator implies that if tasdiq is the composite, the division would not be exhaustive. He argues that judgment itself should be a division of knowledge.
5
Review key terms
Ensure you understand: تصديق (tasdiq), حكم (judgment), إدراك مقارن (accompanied perception), مجموع مركب (composite).
These terms are essential for the subsequent glosses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يخل عن الحكم ( فتصديق ) والمتبادر من هذه العبارة أن التصديق هو الإدراك المقارن للحكم كما تقتضيه عبارة المتأخرين لا نفس الحكم كما هو مذهب الأوائل، ولا المجموع المركب منه ومن تصورات النسبة وطرفيها كما اختاره الإمام الرازي، ونحن نقول : إذا جعل الحكم إدراكاً كما يشهد به رجوعك إلى وجدانك، فالصواب أن يقال : العلم إن كان حكماً أي إدراكاً لأن النسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة فهو تصديق، وإلا فهو
قوله : ( والمتبادر من هذه العبارة ) فيه بحث لأن ذلك المعنى معنى خلا الموصول بالباء، أو إلى أو مع في التاج، يقال: خلا به وإليه ومعه بمعنى واحد ومصدره الخلوة وأما خلا الموصول بعن فمصدره الخلو المفسر بتهي شدن والمتبادر منه عدم الحصول فيه، فمعنى التقسيم إن خلا عن الحكم بأن لم يحصل فيه فتصور وإن لم يخل أي حصل فيه، فتصديق فيكون التصديق عبارة عن المجموع كما اختاره الإمام .
قوله : ( ولا) المجموع إلخ ) اعترض عليه بأنه لا يخرج مذهب الإمام لأنه يصدق عليه، أنه إدراك مقارن للحكم اللهم إلا أن يراد بالمقارنة اقتران المعروض بالعارض، فيخرج اقتران الكل بالجزء لكن لا ضرورة إلى ذلك وادعاء أنه متبادر من عبارة التقسيم والكلام مبني على هذا المتبادر قد لا يسلم بعد تسليم تبادر المقارنة لعدم الخلو . قوله : ( كما يشهد به الخ ) إذ لا يحصل لنا بعد تصور النسبة إلا إدراك أن النسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة وإذعانها . : قوله فالصواب الخ أي الصواب أن يجعل الحكم نفسه قسماً من العلم، إذ لو جعل معروضه، أو المجموع المركب منهما لم تكن القسمة حاصرة كذا نقل عنه، وهذا مبني على أن الحكم ليس داخلاً في التصور بالاتفاق وكيف يكون داخلاً فيه؟ وقد اتفقوا على اكتساب التصور من المعرف، والتصديق من الحجة . أدرجه ها هنا في التصور مع دخوله في العلم على ذلك التعريف كما سبق اللهم إلا أن يقال : الإخراج فيما سبق مبني على ما قاله الشارح في حواشي التجريد وكان الشك عندهم يعني عند المتكلمين حالة وراء التصور، والإدراج ها هنا على مبنى على مذهب الفلاسفة، والأقرب أن يقال : الذي أدرج في التصور في صورة الشك تصور ذات النسبة فلا مخالفة. قوله : ( ولا المجموع المركب إلخ ) اعترض عليه بأنه لا يخرج مذهب الإمام، لأنه يصدق عليه أنه إدراك مقارن للحكم والجواب أن المتبادر من المقارنة الخروج، فالكل لا يقارن الجزء بل بعض أجزائه . قوله : (إذا جعل الحكم إدراكاً أما إذا جعل الحكم موجباً للإدراك لا نفسه، كما هو على الحد المختار لا يتأتى هذا القول، كما لا يتأتى على القول بفعليته
(And otherwise) i.e., if it is not free from judgment (then it is tasdiq). And what is immediately understood from this expression is that tasdiq is perception accompanied by judgment, as the expression of the later scholars requires, not the judgment itself as is the doctrine of the early scholars, nor the composite consisting of it and the conceptions of the relation and its two extremes as chosen by Imam al-Razi. And we say: if judgment is made a perception, as is witnessed by your returning to your conscience, then the correct thing is to say: knowledge, if it is a judgment, i.e., a perception that the relation occurs or does not occur, then it is tasdiq, and otherwise it is [tasawwur]. His saying: 'and what is immediately understood from this expression' — there is investigation in it, because that meaning is the meaning of khala connected with bi, or ila, or ma'a in al-Taj; it is said: khala bihi, ilayhi, and ma'ahu with one meaning, and its verbal noun is al-khalwa. As for khala connected with 'an, its verbal noun is al-khuluww interpreted as 'tahī shudan' (becoming empty), and what is immediately understood from it is non-occurrence in it. So the meaning of the division is: if it is free from judgment, i.e., no judgment occurs in it, then tasawwur; and if not free, i.e., it occurs in it, then tasdiq, and thus tasdiq would be an expression for the composite as Imam [al-Razi] chose. His saying: 'and not the composite etc.' — it was objected that it does not exclude Imam's doctrine, because it is true of it that it is perception accompanied by judgment, unless what is meant by 'accompaniment' is the conjunction of the subject with the accident, thus the conjunction of the whole with the part is excluded, but there is no need for that, and claiming that it is immediately understood from the expression of the division, and the discourse is built on this immediate understanding, may not be granted after granting the immediate understanding of accompaniment due to lack of freedom. His saying: 'as is witnessed by etc.' — for after conceiving the relation, we only obtain a perception that the relation occurs or does not occur, and an acknowledgment of it. His saying: 'so the correct thing etc.' — i.e., the correct thing is to make the judgment itself a division of knowledge, because if one makes it its subject or the composite of the two, the division would not be exhaustive, as is transmitted from him. This is based on the agreed fact that judgment is not included in tasawwur; and how could it be included in it? They have agreed that tasawwur is acquired from definition, and tasdiq from proof. He included it here in tasawwur while it is included in knowledge according to that definition, as before, unless it is said: the exclusion earlier is based on what the commentator said in the glosses on al-Tajrid, and doubt according to them, i.e., the mutakallimun, is a state beyond tasawwur, and the inclusion here is based on the doctrine of the philosophers, and the closer is that what is included in tasawwur in the case of doubt is the conception of the essence of the relation, so there is no contradiction. His saying: 'and not the composite etc.' — it was objected that it does not exclude Imam's doctrine, because it is true of it that it is perception accompanied by judgment, and the answer is that what is immediately understood from accompaniment is being separate, so the whole does not accompany the part, but rather some of its parts. His saying: 'if judgment is made a perception' — but if judgment is made a cause of perception, not itself, as is on the chosen definition, this statement cannot obtain, just as it cannot obtain on the doctrine of its being an act.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage completes the division of knowledge by defining tasdiq (assent) as that which is not free from judgment. The commentator begins by explaining the apparent meaning of the division: tasdiq is perception accompanied by judgment (al-idrak al-muqarin li'l-hukm), which is the view of later scholars, as opposed to the early scholars who said it is the judgment itself, and to Imam al-Razi who said it is a composite of judgment and conceptions. The commentator then presents his own preferred view: if we consider judgment itself to be a perception (as conscience testifies), then knowledge is either a judgment (affirming or denying a relation) — which is tasdiq — or not. The first gloss investigates the language of 'khala 'an' versus 'khala bi' etc., concluding that the phrase 'khala 'an al-hukm' implies non-occurrence of judgment, so tasdiq is precisely the occurrence of judgment; this would align with al-Razi's composite view. The second gloss objects that calling tasdiq 'perception accompanied by judgment' does not exclude al-Razi's composite, unless 'accompaniment' means something specific. The third gloss defends the commentator's view that judgment itself is a perception, based on introspection. The fourth gloss argues that making judgment itself a division ensures exhaustive division. The fifth gloss addresses an apparent contradiction: doubt was previously excluded from knowledge, but here it seems included in tasawwur; the resolution is that doubt for the mutakallimun is a state beyond tasawwur, but the commentator here follows the philosophers who include it. The sixth gloss repeats the objection about al-Razi's composite and answers that 'accompaniment' implies separation, so the whole composite does not accompany the judgment. The final gloss notes that the commentator's position that judgment is a perception only holds if judgment is not considered a cause or an act.

Key Terms
تصديق
حكم
إدراك مقارن
مجموع مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think tasdiq is simply the judgment itself, independent of perception.
Correction: The passage shows that tasdiq is the perception of the judgment, i.e., the awareness that a relation holds or not.

Checkpoint
Which view does the commentator attribute to the early scholars?
A) Tasdiq is perception accompanied by judgment.
B) Tasdiq is the judgment itself.
C) Tasdiq is a composite of judgment and conceptions.
D) Tasdiq is a perception.

Key Takeaway
Tasdiq is defined as knowledge that is a judgment, i.e., a perception that a relation occurs or does not occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own heart, do you find that your belief in Allah is a mere judgment in your mind or a living perception accompanied by conviction? Which view does your experience align with, and what does that reveal about your tasdiq?
?
The commentator asks you to return to your conscience to verify that judgment is a perception. When was the last time you honestly examined your inner experience of faith? If you haven't done so, what holds you back from this introspection?
?
If tasdiq is the perception that a relation holds, then your tasdiq about the truth of Islam should feel like direct perception. Does it feel that way to you? If not, what change must you make to transform your knowledge into true tasdiq?

The Language of 'Khala' and Resolving the Doubt Issue
The commentator refines the definition by analyzing the word 'khala' and addressing objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the linguistic analysis of 'khala' and the nature of doubt affect the definition of tasdiq.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'khala' determines whether tasdiq includes the composite of judgment and conception.

The commentator's glosses delve deeper into the linguistic and philosophical implications of the definition. He examines the word 'khala' used in the division, showing that it can mean 'absence of judgment' which would align with al-Razi's composite view. He also addresses an apparent contradiction: doubt was previously excluded from knowledge, yet here it seems to fall under tasawwur. The resolution involves distinguishing between the mutakallimun and philosophers on the nature of doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the first gloss on 'khala'
The commentator investigates the word 'khala' and its different prepositions. When followed by 'an', it implies non-occurrence of judgment, making tasdiq the composite (al-Razi's view).
Note the distinction between 'khala bihi' and 'khala 'an'.
2
Understand the objection about al-Razi's composite
It is objected that the earlier definition of tasdiq as 'perception accompanied by judgment' still includes al-Razi's composite. The answer is that 'accompaniment' implies separation, so the whole does not accompany the part.
3
Examine the introspection argument
The commentator defends that judgment is a perception because after conceiving the relation, we only obtain a perception that it occurs or not.
This supports making judgment itself a division of knowledge.
4
Resolve the doubt issue
An apparent contradiction: doubt was excluded from knowledge earlier, but here it is included in tasawwur. The resolution is that for mutakallimun, doubt is beyond tasawwur, but the commentator here follows the philosophers who include it.
The closer view is that what is included in tasawwur in case of doubt is the conception of the essence of the relation.
5
Review the conditional nature of the commentator's view
The commentator's position that judgment is a perception only holds if judgment is not considered a cause or an act.

?

?

?

Tasdiq as Judgment in Perception: Three Doctrinal Positions
Al-Iji defines tasdiq and presents three doctrinal positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different definitions of tasdiq (assent) held by early scholars, later scholars, and Imam al-Razi, and how al-Iji resolves the debate.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is not a composite; it is the perception that a relation holds.

In this passage, al-Iji clarifies the crucial distinction between tasawwur and tasdiq. After stating that knowledge empty of judgment is tasawwur, he turns to the nature of tasdiq itself. Here he presents three competing definitions from the tradition and offers his own resolution. This is foundational for understanding how Islamic theology builds upon simple conceptions to reach judgments about Allah and the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysing. Then read the literal translation. Note the problematic word 'قائم' which is likely a corruption.
The intended reading is probably 'تصور' (tasawwur).
2
Identify the three positions
Find the three definitions of tasdiq: (1) later scholars: perception accompanied by judgment, (2) early scholars: judgment itself, (3) al-Razi: composite of judgment and conceptions of relation and its extremes.
These are presented in contrast to the apparent meaning.
3
Understand al-Iji's resolution
Al-Iji redefines 'judgment' as a perception (based on introspection). This makes tasdiq simply a judgment (i.e., a perception of a relation), not a composite.
This resolves the disagreement by taking a position close to the early scholars but with a refined understanding of judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : أنه إن خلا عن الحكم قائم، فإن هذه كلها علوم خالية عن الحكم المذكور ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يخل عن الحكم ( فتصديق ) والمتبادر من هذه العبارة أن التصديق هو الإدراك المقارن للحكم كما تقتضيه عبارة المتأخرين لا نفس الحكم كما هو مذهب الأوائل، ولا المجموع المركب منه ومن تصورات النسبة وطرفيها كما اختاره الإمام الرازي، ونحن نقول : إذا جعل الحكم إدراكاً كما يشهد به رجوعك إلى وجدانك، فالصواب أن يقال : العلم إن كان حكماً أي إدراكاً لأن النسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة فهو تصديق، وإلا فهو
The third observation - the first objective: That if it is empty of judgment [qā'im] / standing?, then these are all sciences empty of the mentioned judgment. (Otherwise) i.e., if it is not empty of judgment, then it is tasdiq. The apparent meaning of this expression is that tasdiq is the perception accompanied by judgment, as required by the phraseology of the later scholars, not judgment itself as is the doctrine of the earlier scholars, nor the composite consisting of it and the conceptions of the relation and its two extremes as chosen by Imam al-Razi. And we say: If judgment is made perception, as your recourse to your inner consciousness testifies, then the correct view is to say: knowledge, if it is a judgment, i.e., a perception that the relation is actually occurring or not occurring, then it is tasdiq; otherwise it is [tasawwur].
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage from the matn divides knowledge into tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent). It begins by stating the condition: if knowledge is empty of judgment, it is conception (the text has "qā'im" which is likely an OCR error for "tasawwur"). Otherwise, it is tasdiq. The passage then clarifies that the apparent meaning of the word "tasdiq" in this division is perception accompanied by judgment, which is the position of later theologians. This is contrasted with two other views: the earlier scholars held that tasdiq is judgment itself, while Imam al-Razi held it is a composite of judgment and the conceptions of the relation and its two terms. The author then gives his own view: if judgment is understood as a perception (as introspection confirms), then knowledge is either a judgment (which is tasdiq) or not (which is tasawwur). This resolves the disagreement by identifying tasdiq with the perceptual judgment itself, not a composite.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
حكم (hukm)
الإدراك (al-idrak)
النسبة (al-nisba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tasdiq is always a composite of judgment and conception of the relation.
Correction: According to al-Iji, if judgment is itself a perception, then tasdiq is simply that perception, not a composite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the apparent meaning of 'tasdiq' in the division of knowledge?
A) Judgment itself
B) Perception accompanied by judgment
C) The composite of judgment and conceptions
D) A mere conception

Key Takeaway
Tasdiq is best understood as a judgment that is a perception of a relation, not a composite of separate elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Quran or a hadith, do you typically assume your understanding is a composite of separate elements, or do you experience it as a direct perception of truth? Which approach brings you closer to certainty, and why?
?
Al-Iji appeals to 'your recourse to your inner consciousness' as evidence that judgment is a perception. Have you ever examined your own inner experience when you affirm or deny a statement? What does your introspection reveal about the nature of your judgments?
?
If tasdiq is ultimately a simple perception of a relation, does that challenge your habit of breaking down beliefs into pieces? How might you train yourself to see your faith as a unified perception rather than a collection of arguments?

Division When Judgment is an Act: Bare and Composite Conception
Al-Iji offers an alternative division when judgment is considered an act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative division of knowledge into bare conception (tasawwur sādhij) and conception with assent (tasawwur ma'ahu tasdiq) when judgment is considered an act.

Core Idea
When judgment is an act, all knowledge is conception — either bare or with assent.

This lesson presents a refined classification of knowledge that arises when we view judgment not as a mental content but as an action. Al-Iji shows that certain expressions for judgment imply action, leading to a different division: instead of two separate kinds (conception and assent), we get two types of conception. This subtle shift has implications for how we understand the path to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Note the conditional structure: 'if empty of judgment -> tasawwur; if judgment is an act -> new division.'
Pay attention to the shift in perspective.
2
Identify the five action-words
The passage lists five expressions that suggest judgment is an act: isnād, ījāb, īqā', salb, and intizā'. Understand each term briefly.
These words describe actions like affirming, negating, or abstracting.
3
Grasp the new division
Instead of the usual tasawwur/tasdiq division, Al-Iji says the correct division here is into bare tasawwur and tasawwur with tasdiq. That is, tasdiq is not a separate kind but a composite conception.
This division is attributed to authoritative books, likely Ibn Sina.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Since all knowledge is tasawwur, theoretical parts have a single acquisitive path. Consider how this differs from the standard view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : أنه إن خلا عن الحكم تصور، فيكون لكل من قسمي العلم طريق موصل يخصه، وإن جعل فعلاً كما توهمه العبارات التي يعبر بها عنه من الإسناد والإيجاب والإيقاع والسلب والانتزاع فالصواب أن يقسم العلم إلى تصور ساذج وتصور معه تصديق كما ورد في بعض الكتب المعتبرة، فللعلم حينئذ وهو التصور مطلقاً طريق خاص كاسب، لما هو نظري منه
The third observation - the first objective: That if it is empty of judgment, it is tasawwur; then for each of the two divisions of knowledge there is a specific path leading to it. And if it is made an act, as the expressions used to express it suggest, such as attribution, affirmation, execution, negation, and abstraction, then the correct view is to divide knowledge into bare tasawwur and tasawwur with tasdiq, as mentioned in some authoritative books. For knowledge then, which is absolute tasawwur, has a specific acquisitive path for what is theoretical among it.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the second division of knowledge, which applies when judgment is considered an act (fi'l) rather than a perception. The matn notes that certain expressions like 'isnād' (attribution), 'ījāb' (affirmation), 'īqā'' (execution), 'salb' (negation), and 'intizā'' (abstraction) suggest a notion of action. Under this understanding, the correct classification is into 'tasawwur sādhij' (bare conception) and 'tasawwur ma'ahu tasdiq' (conception with assent), i.e., tasdiq is not a separate kind but a conception accompanied by judgment. This division is attributed to some authoritative books, likely referring to Ibn Sina. The consequence is that all knowledge, being absolute conception, has a single acquisitive method for its theoretical parts.

Misconception: The division of knowledge is always into tasawwur and tasdiq, regardless of how judgment is viewed.
Correction: If judgment is considered an act, the correct division is into bare conception and conception with assent, not two separate kinds.

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge depends on how we conceptualize judgment; when judgment is an act, knowledge is divided into bare conception and conception with assent.

Tasawwur and Tasdiq Are Distinct in Essence
Distinguishes tasawwur and tasdiq as essentially distinct types of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) are considered two essentially distinct types of knowledge, grounded in inner intuition and the property of truth-aptness.

Core Idea
Tasawwur and tasdiq differ in essence, not merely by addition of judgment.

The commentator begins by rejecting the notion that tasdiq is simply tasawwur plus judgment. Instead, he argues that these are two fundamentally different kinds of knowledge, distinguishable by direct inner experience and by the presence or absence of truth-value. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding the epistemology of Islamic logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the commentator's critical stance against the composition view.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the statement that tasawwur and tasdiq are 'two types distinct in essence'. Note how this is presented as a positive affirmation.
3
Understand the inner intuition argument
The commentator gives an example: conceive a relation with doubt, then judge it. Reflect on how your own mind experiences these two states differently.
This experiential difference is meant to be persuasive.
4
Reflect on the concomitant property
Note that tasdiq admits truth and falsehood, while tasawwur does not. Ask yourself why this property is a natural distinguishing feature.
This property is a well-known concomitant (لازم مشهور).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : أنه إن خلا عن الحكم ولاعارضه المسمى بالحكم والتصديق طريق خاص آخر، وأما جعل التصديق قسماً من العلم مع تركبه من الحكم، وغيره فلا وجه له فعلاً كان الحكم، أو إدراكاً ( وهما ) أي التصور والتصديق ( نوعان متمايزان بالذات ) أي بالماهية فإنك إذا تصورت نسبة أمر إلى آخر، وشككت فيها فقد علمت ذينك الأمرين، والنسبة بينهما قطعاً فلك في هذه الحالة نوع من العلم، ثم إذا زال عنك الشك، وحكمت بأحد طرفي النسبة فقد علمت تلك النسبة نوعاً آخر من العلم، ممتازاً عن الأول بحقيقته وجدانا ( وباعتبار اللازم المشهور وهو احتمال الصدق والكذب ) في التصديق ( وعدمه ) في التصور . المعتبرة، كالشفاء، والنجاة وإن أوله المحقق الرازي بأن المراد أن العلم التصوري يحصل على وجهين، وليس مراد الشيخ التقسيم بناء على أن الحكم عنده إدراك فبطل الحصر. قوله : ( فلا وجه له إلخ) أما إذا كان فعلاً فلان المركب من الفعل والإدراك، لا يكون إدراكاً وأما إذا كان إدراكاً فلبطلان الحصر، وأيضاً على التقديرين لا فائدة لتركيب الحكم مع غيره، لأنه وحده ممتاز عما عداه بطريق كاسب كذا نقل عنه
The third observation – the first objective: That if it is devoid of judgment and its opposite called judgment and assent has a special other path. And as for making assent a division of knowledge while it is composed of judgment and something else, there is no justification for it, whether judgment is an act or a perception. (And they are) i.e., conception and assent (two types distinct in essence) i.e., in quiddity, for if you conceive a relation of one thing to another and doubt it, you have certainly known those two things and the relation between them, so you have in this state a type of knowledge. Then when doubt leaves you and you judge one of the two sides of the relation, you have known that relation as another type of knowledge, distinct from the first in its reality by inner intuition. (And by consideration of the well-known concomitant, which is the possibility of truth and falsehood) in assent (and its absence) in conception. The considered ones, like al-Shifa' and al-Najat, although al-Muhaqqiq al-Razi interpreted it as meaning that conceptual knowledge occurs in two ways, and the Master (al-Sayyid) did not intend division based on judgment being a perception for him, so the exclusivity is invalid. His statement: (there is no justification for it, etc.) If it is an act, then the composite of act and perception is not a perception; and if it is a perception, then due to the invalidity of exclusivity. Also, in both cases, there is no benefit in combining judgment with something else, because it alone is distinguished from what is other than it by a method of acquisition, as has been transmitted from him.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument establishing that tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) are two types of knowledge distinct in essence. The commentator begins by stating that the claim that assent is a division of knowledge composed of judgment and something else has no justification, whether judgment is considered an act or a perception. He then explains the distinction: when you conceive a relation between two things but doubt it, you have a type of knowledge (tasawwur). When you then judge one side of the relation, you have another type (tasdiq), distinct in its reality as experienced by inner intuition. This distinction is further supported by the concomitant property: tasdiq admits truth and falsehood, while tasawwur does not. The commentator then discusses al-Razi's interpretation that the Master's intent was not a strict division but that conceptual knowledge occurs in two ways. He concludes by refuting the composition view: if judgment is an act, combining it with perception does not yield perception; if judgment is a perception, the exclusivity of the division vanishes. In both cases, adding judgment to something else is unnecessary because judgment alone is distinguished by its method of acquisition.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
حكم (judgment)
علم (knowledge)
ذات (essence)
ماهية (quiddity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tasdiq is just tasawwur plus a judgment.
Correction: They are essentially distinct types of knowledge, not merely additive. The passage argues they differ in essence and can be distinguished by inner intuition and truth-aptness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for distinguishing tasawwur and tasdiq?
A) Their subject matter
B) Their essence (dhat)
C) Their composition from judgment
D) Their relation to truth

Key Takeaway
Tasawwur and tasdiq are two essentially distinct types of knowledge, distinguishable by inner intuition and the presence of truth-aptness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage says the distinction is known by inner intuition. Can you recall a moment when you passed from doubting a religious truth to affirming it? Describe that shift: what did you know before and after?
