Objections to the Essence-Distinction of Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Objections to the essence-distinction of tasawwur and tasdiq are discussed and answered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main objections to the claim that tasawwur and tasdiq are distinct in essence, and how scholars have responded to them.

Core Idea
The essence-distinction of tasawwur and tasdiq survives objections rooted in accidental distinctions.

This passage presents several objections that challenge the essential distinction between tasawwur and tasdiq. These objections include claims that the distinction is accidental, that the division lacks exclusivity, and that inner intuition is insufficient. The commentator provides responses drawing on earlier scholars like al-Razi. Working through these arguments sharpens your understanding of the foundational division of knowledge in Islamic logic and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main objections
Read the passage and list the objections raised against the essence-distinction. They include: the distinction being external, the lack of exclusivity, and the reliance on inner intuition.
Notice how each objection is introduced with 'قد يمنع' (may be denied).
2
Examine the responses
For each objection, find the response given. The first response reinterprets 'by essence' as 'by itself'. The second response from al-Razi denies exclusivity. The third response redefines tasawwur and tasdiq in terms of mental occurrence.
The responses are layered; not all are equally accepted.
3
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the responses aim to preserve the idea that tasawwur and tasdiq are distinct in essence, not merely by accident. The passage ends with a note that inner intuition may not convince a determined opponent.
This shows the dialectical nature of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي بالماهية لا يخفى أن تمايزهما بالماهية لا يصح على تقسيم المتن، بناء على حمله على مذهب المتأخرين لأن التمايز بين القسمين حينئذ يكون بأمر خارج، وهو المقارنة بالحكم ،وعدمه وما ذكره الشارح إنما يفيد تمايز العارض والمعروض لا تمايز القسمين، فالتوجيه حمل قوله بالذات على معنى بنفسه . الخاص بطريق الاستخدام إذ المراد حينئذ ورود تقسيم قسم من العلم إليهما، والكلام محمول على التنظير دون التمثيل، واعلم أن هذا الجواب مبني على ما ذكره الرازي في شرح المطالع من أن مراد الشيخ بما ذكره ليس الحصر، بل إن العلم يقع على أحد الوجهين، ووقوعه على الوجه الثالث لا ينافيه، وقد يوجه كلام الشيخ بأن الحكم باعتبار ذاته يسمى تصديقاً وحكماً، وباعتبار حصوله في الذهن تصوراً فمراده بتصوره معه تصديق نفس الحكم، وإطلاق المعية بالنظر إلى المغايرة : الاعتبارية، وبه يظهر أنه يمكن رد قولهم العلم إما تصور ساذج أو تصور معه حكم إلى هذا المعنى، فعلى هذا يرجع تقسيم الشيخ إلى التقسيم المختار، ويتم الحصر لكنه خلاف المتبادر . قوله : ( فلا وجه له فعلاً كان الحكم أو إدراكاً) قال رحمه الله : أما إذا كان فعلاً فلان المركب من الإدراك، والفعل لا يكون إدراكاً ،وعلماً، وأما إذا كان إدراكاً فلبطلان الحصر، وأيضاً على التقديرين لا فائدة لاعتبار تركيب الحكم مع غيره لأنه وحده يمتاز عما عداه بطريقي كاسب له هذا وقد يمنع بطلان الحصر بالتزام دخول الحكم في التصور الساذج المقابل للتصديق فتأمل . قوله: (متمايزان بالذات قد يمنع ذلك ويدعي أن التمايز ليس إلا بالعوارض، وأما الوجدان فربما لم يقنع به الخصم. قوله : ( نوعان آخر من العلم) قد يمنع ذلك يجواز أن يكون الامتياز بالهوية، أو بالعوارض كما سيأتي مثله في مباحث العلم والوجدان في مثله ليس ينفع للجاحد
His statement: (i.e., in quiddity) it is not hidden that their distinction in quiddity is not valid according to the division of the text, based on interpreting it according to the later scholars, because the distinction between the two divisions then would be by an external matter, which is being accompanied by judgment or its absence. What the commentator mentioned only benefits the distinction of the accident and the subject, not the distinction of the two divisions. So the direction is to interpret his statement 'in essence' as meaning 'by itself' in the way of using the term, because the intent then is that a division of knowledge occurs into these two, and the statement is carried as an illustration not an example. Know that this answer is based on what al-Razi mentioned in Sharh al-Matali' that the Master's intent in what he mentioned is not exclusivity, but rather knowledge occurs in one of two ways, and its occurrence in a third way does not contradict it. The Master's statement may be directed such that judgment, considered in itself, is called tasdiq and hukm, and considered as occurring in the mind is tasawwur, so his intent by 'tasawwur with it' is the tasawwur of the judgment itself, and the expression of accompaniment is according to notional difference. By this it appears that it is possible to refute their statement 'knowledge is either bare conception or conception with judgment' to this meaning. Thus the Master's division returns to the chosen division, and exclusivity is achieved, but it is contrary to what is apparent. His statement: (So there is no justification for it, whether judgment is an act or a perception) – he, may Allah have mercy on him, said: If it is an act, then the composite of perception and act is not a perception and knowledge; and if it is a perception, then due to the invalidity of exclusivity. Also, in both cases, there is no benefit in considering the composition of judgment with something else, because it alone is distinguished from what is other than it by two methods of acquisition for it. And this may prevent the invalidity of exclusivity by committing to the entry of judgment into the bare conception that corresponds to tasdiq, so reflect. His statement: (distinct in essence) – this may be denied, and it may be claimed that the distinction is only by accidents, and as for inner intuition, perhaps the opponent is not convinced by it. His statement: (another type of knowledge) – this may be denied by the possibility that the distinction is by identity or by accidents, as will come later in the discussions of knowledge. And inner intuition in such cases does not benefit against the denier.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage addresses objections to the claim that tasawwur and tasdiq are distinct in essence. The commentator begins by noting a problem: if we interpret the text according to later scholars, the distinction between the two divisions would be by an external matter (accompaniment by judgment or its absence), not by essence. The commentator's earlier statement only distinguishes accident from subject, not the two types themselves. He then offers a solution: interpret 'by essence' as 'by itself' using the method of usage, meaning that the division of knowledge into these two is illustrative. This answer is based on al-Razi's view that the Master did not intend exclusivity but that knowledge occurs in one of two ways, and a third way does not contradict. Then he proposes another interpretation: judgment considered in itself is called tasdiq, but considered as occurring in the mind it is called tasawwur, so 'conception with it' means the conception of the judgment itself. This refutes the claim that knowledge is either bare conception or conception with judgment. The passage then repeats the earlier argument that composition is invalid. Finally, it mentions that the claim of distinctness in essence can be denied, with the opponent arguing that the distinction is only by accidents and that inner intuition does not convince the denier.

Key Terms
ذات (dhat)
عارض (accident)
معروض (subject)
تمايز (distinction)
وجدان (inner intuition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinction between tasawwur and tasdiq is merely accidental, not essential.
Correction: The passage defends the essential distinction by reinterpreting 'by essence' and providing alternative models. While objections exist, the dominant view maintains an essential difference.

Checkpoint
What objection is raised against the claim that tasawwur and tasdiq are distinct in essence?
A) They are identical
B) The distinction is merely accidental
C) The opponent does not accept inner intuition
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The essence-distinction of tasawwur and tasdiq is defended through multiple interpretative strategies, despite objections that it is only accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you merely form a mental concept of the words, or do you actively affirm the truths they convey? If there is a gap between conception and judgment in your heart, what practical step can you take today to transform your knowledge into heartfelt conviction?

The passage shows that even scholars debated whether the distinction between conception and assent is essential. Have you ever examined the nature of your own faith—is it merely a concept in your mind, or is it a judgment that shapes your choices? What would change if you treated your understanding of Allah as a tasdiq that demands action?
?
The commentator notes that inner intuition may not convince a determined opponent. Are you ever content with intuitive certainty about your beliefs, or have you sought intellectual grounding that can withstand challenges? If someone denied the fundamentals of your faith, would your current understanding of tasawwur and tasdiq help you articulate why belief is more than just a concept?

Defining Necessary Knowledge and the First Objection
Definition of necessary knowledge and first objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi Abu Bakr's definition of necessary knowledge and the first objection that it can be removed by opposites.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge clings to the soul, but sleep can loosen its grip.

Al-Qadi Abu Bakr defines necessary knowledge as that which the creature cannot escape. However, this definition faces an immediate challenge: if necessary knowledge can be lost, is it truly inescapable? This lesson presents the definition and the first objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage defining necessary knowledge. Notice the key phrase 'لا يجد المخلوق إلى الانفكاك عنه سبيلا'.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that necessary knowledge can vanish due to sleep, heedlessness, etc. Consider what this means for the definition's claim of inescapability.
3
Contemplate the scope
Think about whether the definition meant absolute inescapability or inescapability after occurrence. The commentator discusses this later.
This leads to the second objection.

Source Text

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثاني : العلم الحادث ينقسم إلى ضروري ومكتسب ( العلم الحادث ) قيده بالحدوث ليخرج عنه علمه تعالى فإنه قديم ولا يوصف بضرورة ولا كسب ) ينقسم إلى ضروري ومكتسب فالضروري قال القاضي ) أبو بكر في تفسيره (هو) العلم الذي يلزم نفس المخلوق لزوماً لا يجد) المخلوق (إلى الانفكاك عنه سبيلا ) كالعلم بجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات ( وأورد عليه جواز زواله ) أي زوال العلم الضروري بعد حصوله ( بأضداده كالنوم والغفلة و أورد أيضاً (أنه قد يفقد العلم الضروري لعدم مقتضيه كما يفقد (قبل الحس) أي شاملاً . قوله : ( ولا يوصف أي عند المتكلمين ولذا أخذوا في تعريفهما المخلوق، وأما عند المنطقيين فداخل في الضروري لعدم توقفه على نظر، ولذا جعل المحقق الدواني المقسم خلط لهما، ومن بین الاصطلاحين وقع في ورطة الحيرة فقال الضروري معتبر في مفهومه عما من شأن جنسه أن يكون حاصلا بالنظر والعلم القديم ليس كذلك، وهذا مع عدم دليل على هذا الاعتبار إنما يتم لو كان علم الواجب مخالفاً بالجنس لعلم الممكن، أما لو كان مخالفاً بالنوع فلا . قوله : ( إلى (ضروري) قال الآمدي : الضروري يطلق على ما أكره عليه، وعلى ما تدعو الحاجة إليه دعاء قوياً كالأكل في المخمصة وعلى ما سلب فيه الاختيار على الفعل، والترك كحركة المرتعش . وإطلاق الضروري على العلم بهذا الاعتبار الأخير فهو الذي لا قدرة للمخلوق على تحصيله. قوله : ( وأورد عليه إلخ) لا يخفى عليك أن خلاصة الإيراد إبطال جامعية التعريف، وهو حاصل بزوال العلوم الضرورية بطريان الأضداد، سواء أريد بالانفكاك الانفكاك مطلقاً، أو الانفكاك بعد الحصول وإن قوله : وإنه قد يفقد لا يفيد إلا بطلان جامعيته على تقدير إرادة الانفكاك مطلقاً، فهو تكثير لمواد النقض، وليس إيراداً آخر فقوله : وأورد أيضاً تقدير مخل لأنه عطف على أورد وإن اللائق تقديم قوله : وإنه قد يفقد على قوله جواز زواله ليصير حاصله، أنه لا يمكن إرادة الانفكاك مطلقاً، ولا إرادة الانفكاك بعد الحصول إذ لا فائدة بعد إبطال إرادة الانفكاك بعد الحصول في إبطال إرادة الانفكاك مطلقاً، إلا أن يقال: إنه قدمه لأن المتبادر من هو الفقدان بعد الحصول فإبطال إرادته أهم . قوله : ( وإنه قد يفقد إلخ ) فإذا حصل بعد فقدانه لا يصدق عليه أنه علم لا نجد سبيلا إلى الانفكاك . قوله ولا يوصف بضرورة ولا كسب فإن قلت : عدم التوقف على النظر والكسب يشمل علمه تعالى فاختصاص الضروري بالعلم الحادث محل نظر قلت : التقابل بين الضروري والنظري تقابل العدم والملكة والاستعداد المعتبر فيه قد يكون بحسب الجنس، كعدم البصر بالنسبة إلى العقرب على ما سيأتي تحقيقه، وعدم النظر من هذا القبيل فلا يشمل علمه تعالى إذ لا تجانس بينه وبين علمنا على أن كلاً منهما لا يخلو عن إيهام الحدوث ولذا لا يوصف علم الله تعالى بهما .
The third observation – the second objective: Temporal knowledge is divided into necessary and acquired. (Temporal knowledge) he restricted it by temporality to exclude His knowledge, exalted be He, for it is eternal and is not described as necessary or acquired. It is divided into necessary and acquired. The necessary – al-Qadi Abu Bakr said in his interpretation: It is the knowledge that clings to the soul of the creature with a clinging such that the creature finds no way to free himself from it, like knowledge of the permissibility of permissible things and the impossibility of impossible things. And an objection was raised against it: the possibility of its removal, i.e., removal of necessary knowledge after its occurrence, by its opposites like sleep and heedlessness. And also an objection was raised: that necessary knowledge may be absent due to the absence of its cause, as it is absent before sense-perception, i.e., inclusive. His statement: (and it is not described) i.e., according to the theologians, and therefore they included in their definition of them the creature. As for the logicians, it is included in the necessary because it does not depend on reflection. Therefore al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani made the divided term a mixture between the two terminologies, and the one between the two fell into a perplexity and said: The necessary is considered in its concept as being of the kind that normally occurs by reflection, and eternal knowledge is not like that. And this, without evidence for this consideration, is only valid if the knowledge of the Necessary is generically different from possible knowledge, but if it is specifically different, then no. His statement: (to necessary) – al-Amidi said: 'Necessary' is applied to that which one is compelled to do, and to that which need strongly calls for, like eating in extreme hunger, and to that in which choice is removed from action and avoidance, like the movement of a tremulous person. And the application of 'necessary' to knowledge in this last consideration is that which the creature has no power to acquire. His statement: (and an objection was raised against it, etc.) It is not hidden from you that the gist of the objection is to invalidate the comprehensiveness of the definition, and this is attained by the removal of necessary knowledge through the occurrence of opposites, whether by 'freeing' is meant absolute freeing or freeing after occurrence. And his statement: 'and that it may be absent' only benefits the invalidity of its comprehensiveness on the assumption of intending absolute freeing, so it is a multiplication of counterexamples, not another objection. So his statement 'and also an objection was raised' is a false estimation because it is conjoined with 'was raised', and it would be appropriate to present his statement 'and that it may be absent' before his statement 'the possibility of its removal' so that the result is: it is impossible to intend absolute freeing, nor freeing after occurrence, since after invalidating the intention of freeing after occurrence there is no benefit in invalidating the intention of absolute freeing. Unless it is said: he presented it because what is apparent is loss after occurrence, so invalidating that intention is more important. His statement: (and that it may be absent, etc.) So if it occurs after its loss, it does not apply to it that it is knowledge from which we find no way to free ourselves. His statement: (and it is not described as necessary or acquired) – if you say: the lack of dependence on reflection and acquisition includes His knowledge, so the specification of the necessary to temporal knowledge is subject to consideration. I say: The contrast between necessary and theoretical is a contrast of absence and possession, and the disposition considered in it may be generic, like the absence of sight in relation to the scorpion, as will be investigated later. And the absence of reflection is of this kind, so it does not include His knowledge, exalted be He, since there is no homogeneity between it and our knowledge. Moreover, each of them is not free from implying temporality, and therefore the knowledge of Allah, exalted be He, is not described by them.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) and presents objections to the definition. It begins the second section on the division of temporal knowledge into necessary and acquired. The definition is from al-Qadi Abu Bakr: necessary knowledge is that which clings to the soul of the creature such that he cannot free himself from it, like knowledge of the possible and impossible. Two objections are raised: first, such knowledge can be lost through opposites like sleep; second, it can be absent before its cause exists, like before sense-perception. The commentator then discusses these objections, explaining that the first objection invalidates the definition's comprehensiveness if 'freeing' is interpreted absolutely or after occurrence. He notes that the second objection is just a different form of the first. He also addresses the issue of whether necessary knowledge applies only to temporal knowledge, arguing that the contrast between necessary and theoretical is a contrast of absence and possession, and since God's knowledge is not homogeneous with ours, it is not included. Finally, he notes that both necessary and acquired imply temporality, so God's knowledge is not described by them.

Key Terms
علم ضروري (necessary knowledge)
انفكاك (freeing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge is absolutely permanent and cannot be lost.
Correction: The passage shows that necessary knowledge can be removed by opposites like sleep and heedlessness.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi Abu Bakr, necessary knowledge is defined as:
A) Knowledge that results from reflection
B) Knowledge that the creature cannot free himself from
C) Knowledge that is eternal
D) Knowledge that is acquired

Key Takeaway
Necessary knowledge is inescapable by nature, but can be temporarily lost due to its opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are overtaken by sleep or heedlessness, are you still accountable for the truths you know necessarily? What does the temporary loss of such knowledge mean for your responsibility to remain mindful of Allah?
?
If you know with certainty that something is wrong (e.g., lying), yet you still do it, does that mean you did not truly know? How can you bridge the gap between your necessary knowledge and your actions?
?
The objection warns that necessary knowledge can be removed by its opposites. What are the 'opposites' in your own life—distractions, desires, fatigue—that threaten to steal your certainty in fundamental truths? What practical steps will you take to guard against them?

Second Objection and the Nature of God's Knowledge
Second objection and discussion on God's knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the definition of necessary knowledge and why God's knowledge is not classified as necessary or acquired.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge can also be absent before its cause, and God's knowledge is beyond such categories.

The definition of necessary knowledge faces a second challenge: it can be absent before its cause exists. Additionally, this lesson explores why theologians do not describe God's knowledge as necessary or acquired, contrasting it with our temporal knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the passage about necessary knowledge being absent due to absence of its cause, e.g., before sense-perception.
2
Understand the commentary
The commentator explains that this is not a separate objection but a variation of the first. Consider why.
3
Examine the issue of God's knowledge
Read why God's knowledge is not described as necessary or acquired. The commentator discusses the contrast between necessary and theoretical.

?

?
When you rely on your acquired knowledge, do you ever reflect on its dependence on causes and its potential absence? How can this awareness lead you to trust more in God's eternal knowledge?
?
If God's knowledge is so different from yours that the categories of necessary and acquired do not apply, how should that reshape your understanding of His omniscience and your own intellectual limits?

The Objection of Necessity After Acquisition
Objection and answer about acquired knowledge becoming necessary after its occurrence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of acquired knowledge is defended against the objection that it becomes necessary after its occurrence by distinguishing temporal from absolute inability to separate.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge can be avoided before it occurs, unlike necessary knowledge.

This passage presents a subtle objection that could collapse the distinction between necessary and acquired knowledge. The defender sharpens the definition by showing that the criterion of 'impossibility of separation' must be understood absolutely, not merely after the knowledge has been obtained. Understanding this nuance is key to appreciating the precision of kalam epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic and translation without overthinking. Let the objection and answer stand out.
Notice the structure: division, objection, answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find the part where the objector argues that theoretical knowledge becomes necessary after acquisition. Understand why this would be a problem for the definition.
The objector uses the phrase 'فكذا النظري بعد حصوله'.
3
Grasp the answer's key distinction
The answer distinguishes between 'before acquisition' and 'after acquisition'. The definition requires absolute inability to separate, which only necessary knowledge has.
The answer uses 'لا يلزم من عدم القدرة على الانفكاك عن النظري بعد حصوله عدم القدرة على الانفكاك عنه مطلقاً'.
4
Apply the distinction
Think of an example: you can choose not to reflect on a topic (so separation is possible before), but once you know it, you cannot un-know it (separation impossible after). The definition concerns the former.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم الحادث ينقسم إلى ضروري ومكتسب. به امتناع الانفكاك المقدور فيكون آخر كلامه تفسيراً لأوله. فإن قيل : فكذا النظري بعد حصوله أي هو أيضاً غير مقدور انفكاكه، إذ لا قدرة للمخلوق على الانفكاك عنه بعد حصوله، فيدخل في حد الضروري، والفاء في قوله فكذا، للإشعار بترتب هذا السؤال على الجواب عن السؤال الأول : قلنا : لا يلزم من عدم القدرة على الانفكاك عن النظري بعد حصوله عدم القدرة على الانفكاك عنه مطلقاً والمذكور في التعريف هو عدم القدرة على الانفكاك مطلقاً، وذلك إنما يوجد في الضروري، وأما النظري فمقدور انفكاكه قبل حصوله، بأن يترك النظر فيه.
Contingent knowledge is divided into necessary and acquired. By it, the impossibility of separation is predestined, so the end of his speech is an explanation of its beginning. For if it is said: Likewise, theoretical knowledge after its occurrence, i.e., it also is not within power to separate from it, for the creature has no power to separate from it after its occurrence, so it enters into the definition of necessary. And the 'fa' in his saying 'likewise' indicates the ordering of this question upon the answer to the first question. We say: It does not follow from the lack of power to separate from theoretical knowledge after its occurrence the lack of power to separate from it absolutely. And what is mentioned in the definition is the lack of power to separate absolutely, and that is only found in necessary. As for theoretical, its separation is within power before its occurrence, by abandoning reflection upon it.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer concerning the definition of acquired knowledge. The commentator begins by stating the division of contingent knowledge into necessary and acquired. The phrase 'by it, the impossibility of separation is predestined' explains the defining characteristic. Then the objector argues: if theoretical knowledge, after it is obtained, cannot be separated from by the creature, then it would fall under the definition of necessary knowledge, which requires absolute lack of power to separate. The answer clarifies that the definition considers absolute lack of power to separate, which applies only to necessary knowledge. Theoretical knowledge, however, is within power to separate from before its occurrence by simply not engaging in the reflection that leads to it. Thus, the objection fails because it conflates the time after acquisition with the absolute condition.

Key Terms
ضروري
مكتسب
انفكاك
قدرة
نظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If after obtaining theoretical knowledge one cannot separate from it, then it must be necessary knowledge.
Correction: The definition of necessary knowledge requires absolute inability to separate, not just after occurrence. Acquired knowledge can be avoided before it is obtained.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector claim that theoretical knowledge would become necessary?
A) Because it is not within power to acquire.
B) Because after it is obtained, one cannot separate from it.
C) Because it is based on reflection.
D) Because it is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The definition of acquired knowledge depends on the possibility of separation before acquisition, not after.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector in this lesson confuses a temporary inability with an absolute one. Are there beliefs you hold that you have never questioned simply because you have always had them? What would it mean for you to 'abandon reflection' on an important matter of faith—could that be a form of neglect?
?
This passage distinguishes between before and after acquisition. When you learn something new about your Creator, do you treat that knowledge as a one-time fact, or as an ongoing responsibility to reflect? How can you keep the door open for deeper understanding even after you 'know' something?
?
Consider a time when you felt you could not change a certain belief or habit of thought. Was that feeling of impossibility based on the reality of the situation, or on your own unwillingness to examine it? What would it take for you to 'separate' from a wrong assumption before it becomes entrenched?

Sensory Knowledge and Whether It Is Within Power
Objection that sensory knowledge is not within power, with reply distinguishing acquisition from separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument about whether sensory knowledge is within human power, and how the distinction between sensation and knowledge resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Sensory knowledge is acquired, not necessary, because we can choose to abandon sensation.

In this passage, al-Sanusi addresses a key objection to the classification of sensory knowledge as acquired (muktasab). The objector claims that if sensory knowledge depends on unknown external factors, then it cannot be within our power, but the ability to abandon sensation might imply otherwise. The careful distinction between the power to sense and the resulting knowledge forms the core of the reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the objector's two-part argument
Notice that the objector first argues that sensory knowledge is not within power to acquire because it depends on non-powerful matters. Then he adds that separation from it by abandoning sensation is within power.
3
Analyze the reply's distinction
The reply concedes that separation from sensation is within power, but denies that this implies separation from knowledge is within power. Distinguish between the act of sensing and the resulting knowledge.
4
Consider the final objection about judging the unknown
The passage ends with an objection: how can we judge that those matters are not within power if we do not know them? Reflect on this epistemological puzzle.
5
Summarize the conclusion
Review the structure: initial claim, objection, reply, explanation of phrase, and final objection. Conclude that sensory knowledge is acquired because its means (sensation) is within power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك كالمحسوسات بالحواس الظاهرة، فإنها لا تحصل بمجرد الإحساس المقدور لنا، بل تتوقف على أمور غير مقدورة لا نعلم ما هي. بأن العلم بالحسيات غير مقدور التحصيل لتوقفه على أشياء غير مقدورة، ومقدور الانفكاك بترك الإحساس الذي هو مقدور الانفكاك لأنا لا نسلم أن الانفكاك عنه مقدور، لأنه يستلزم مقدورية ترك الانفكاك الذي هو التحصيل. وقد اعترفت بأنه غير مقدور نعم الانفكاك عن الإحساس مقدور، وهو لا يستلزم مقدورية الانفكاك عن العلم. قوله : ( لا تحصل بمجرد الإحساس) وإلا لما عرض الغلط. قوله : (بل تتوقف الخ) إذا تحققت تلك الأمور مع الإحساس حصل العلم، وإلا فلا وتلك الأمور غير مقدورة لنا لأن القدرة لا تتعلق إلا بالمعلوم، وتلك الأمور غيره معلومة، فقوله : لا نعلم جملة مستأنفة بيان لكون تلك الأمور غير مقدورة، وليس صفة لأمور على ما وهم، ثم ما هي. اعترض بأنه إذا لم تكن معلومة كيف يحكم على غير المعلوم بأنها غير مقدورة؟.
And that like sense-percepts by the external senses, for they are not obtained by mere sensation that is within our power, but depend on non-powerful matters we do not know what they are. That sensory knowledge is not within power to acquire due to its dependence on non-powerful things, and separation is within power by abandoning sensation which is within power. For we do not concede that separation from it is within power, because it entails the ability to leave the acquisition which is acquisition. And you have admitted that it is not within power. Yes, separation from sensation is within power, and it does not entail the ability to separate from knowledge. His saying: (It is not obtained by mere sensation) otherwise error would occur. His saying: (But it depends etc.) when those matters occur with sensation, knowledge occurs; otherwise not. And those matters are not within our power because power attaches only to the known, and those matters are not known. So his saying 'we do not know' is a parenthetical sentence explaining that those matters are not within power, not a description of matters, as was imagined. Then, 'what are they?' He objected: if they are not known, how does one judge something unknown as not within power?
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage discusses an objection concerning sensory knowledge. It begins by stating that sensory percepts are not obtained by mere sensation alone but depend on unknown non-powerful causes. The objector argues that if sensory knowledge is not within power to acquire due to this dependence, then separation from it by abandoning sensation should be within power. But the opponent denies that separation from knowledge itself is within power because that would entail ability to leave the acquisition, which is admitted to be impossible. The reply distinguishes: separation from sensation is within power, but that does not imply ability to separate from the resulting knowledge. Then the commentator explains the phrase 'we do not know' as a parenthetical explanation that those matters are not within power, not a description. Finally, an objection is raised: how can one judge something unknown as not within power? This passage thus defends the classification of sensory knowledge as acquired despite its reliance on external factors.

مكتسب
إحساس
قدرة
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory knowledge is necessary because it depends on external factors beyond our control.
Correction: While it depends on external factors, the ability to abandon sensation shows that the knowledge is not necessary but acquired.

Checkpoint
Why is sensory knowledge considered not within power to acquire?
A) Because it is obtained by mere sensation.
B) Because it depends on non-powerful matters.
C) Because it is necessary.
D) Because it is infinite.

Key Takeaway
Sensory knowledge is classified as acquired because, although its full acquisition is not within our power, we have power over the means (sensation) that leads to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perceive something beautiful or painful, you may attribute the experience to your own senses. But this lesson shows that sensation alone is insufficient—unknown factors beyond your control must conspire. How does this realization confront your tendency to take credit for your own awareness? Will you now pause to acknowledge the One who orchestrates those unseen factors before you react?
?
Consider a time you tried to 'unsee' or 'unhear' something—perhaps a disturbing image or news. You could close your eyes or plug your ears, but the knowledge remained. Does this not prove that once knowledge is acquired, you are powerless to separate from it? How does this truth compel you to guard what you expose your senses to, knowing you cannot undo the knowledge that results?
?
The objector in this passage assumed that if you can control the means (sensation), you can control the end (knowledge). Are there areas of your spiritual life where you make the same mistake—thinking that performing an outward action guarantees an inward state? What is one specific assumption you need to surrender today, trusting that only Allah gives the increase?

Examples of Necessary Knowledge and Definition of Badihi
Al-Iji gives three categories of necessary knowledge and defines badihi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of necessary knowledge illustrated by al-Iji and the precise definition of badihi as that which is known by the intellect alone without any external aid.

Core Idea
Badihi knowledge needs nothing but the mind's attention—no senses, no reasoning.

Al-Iji now enriches our understanding of necessary knowledge by giving concrete examples from internal sensation, everyday certainties, and logical first principles. He then defines 'badihi' (self-evident) as a particular type of necessary knowledge that the mind grasps without any assistance. This lesson will help you see the distinction between what we know automatically and what we know through effort.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic source and the English translation to get a feel for the argument. Notice the examples al-Iji lists for necessary knowledge.
The passage uses a compact style; the translation will help clarify.
2
Identify the three types of examples
Al-Iji gives three groups: internal sensations (pleasure/pain), customary knowledge (mountains are stable), and necessary truths (non-contradiction). Make sure you see how each differs from the others.
The third group has no cause—it is always present in the mind.
3
Understand the definition of badihi
The key phrase: 'badihi is what mere intellect affirms without assistance of sense or anything else.' Note that it applies to both concepts and judgments.
Badihi is a subset of the necessary—more specific.
4
Check your understanding with the quiz
The checkpoint question asks what makes badihi more specific. Use the definition and the examples to answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومتى حضلت وكيف حصلت كما سنذكره بخلاف النظريات فإنها تحصل بمجرد النظر المقدور لنا، وكالمحسوسات بالحواس الباطنة مثل علم الإنسان بلذته وألمه وكالعلم بالأمور العادية مثل علمنا بأن الجبال المعهودة لنا ثابتة، والبحار غير غائرة، وكالعلم بالأمور التي لا سبب لها ولا يجد الإنسان نفسه خالية عنها، مثل علمنا بأن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان ( والبديهي ما يثبته مجرد العقل) أي يثبته التفاته غير استعانة بحس ، أو غيره تصوراً كان أو تصديقاً فهو أخص من الضروري، وقد يطلق مرادفاً له ) والكسبي يقابل الضروري) فهو العلم المقدور. قوله : ( وكالعلم بالأمور التي إلخ ) أي العلم البديهي، وإذا لم يكن له سبب صدق أنه ليس تحصيله مقدوراً لنا إذ لا معنى لكون العلم مقدوراً إلا كون سببه وطريقه مقدوراً فإن قلت أليس ذلك العلم حاصلا لنا الالتفات المقدور لنا، فيكون مقدوراً قلت : الالتفات قدر مشترك بمجرد بين جميع العلوم، فليس ذلك سبباً له بل لخصوصية الأطراف مدخل فيه، ومعني كون مجرد الالتفات كافياً فيه أنه لا احتياج فيه إلى سبب آخر، لا أنه سبب تام كما أشار إليه الشارح بقوله : من غير استعانة بحس أو غيره أي من الأسباب . قوله : ( فهو أخص من الضروري لأنه الذي لا يكون تحصيله مقدوراً بأن لا يكون له سبب مقدور يدور معه وجوده وعدمه، وذلك بأن لا يكون له سبب يدور معه، وهو البديهي أو يكون له سبب يدور معه لكن لا يكون مقدوراً كالحسيات والتجربيات والعاديات، وغير ذلك فاستقم فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام .
And when it is present and how it is obtained as we will mention, unlike theoretical matters, for they are obtained by mere theoretical reflection which is in our power. And like sensations by the internal senses, such as man's knowledge of his pleasure and pain, and like knowledge of customary matters, such as our knowledge that the familiar mountains are stable and the seas are not perforated, and like knowledge of matters that have no cause and man does not find himself free from them, such as our knowledge that negation and affirmation do not combine and are not both absent. (And the self-evident is what mere intellect affirms) i.e., it affirms it by its attention without assistance of sense or anything else, whether it is a concept or an assertion, so it is more specific than the necessary, and sometimes it is used synonymously with it. (And the acquired is opposite to the necessary) so it is knowledge that is in one's power. His statement: '(And like knowledge of matters that...)' i.e., self-evident knowledge. When it has no cause, it is true that its attainment is not in our power, for the meaning of knowledge being in our power is only that its cause and path are in our power. If you say: is not that knowledge attained by our attention which is in our power, so it would be in our power? I say: attention is a common factor among all sciences, so it is not a cause for it, rather the specificity of the terms has an entry in it. And the meaning of mere attention being sufficient for it is that there is no need for another cause, not that it is a complete cause, as the commentator pointed out by his statement: 'without assistance of sense or anything else' from the causes. His statement: '(so it is more specific than the necessary)' because the necessary is that whose attainment is not in our power, either by having no cause that co-varies with its existence and non-existence (and that is the self-evident) or by having a cause that co-varies but is not in our power, like sensations, experiments, and customary matters, and others. So be straight, for feet have slipped therein.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of necessary knowledge by providing additional examples: internal sensations (pleasure and pain), customary knowledge (stability of mountains, non-perforation of seas), and necessary truths (law of non-contradiction). It then defines the self-evident (badihi) as what the mere intellect affirms without aid of sense or any other cause, whether conceptual or assertive. The commentary clarifies that badihi is more specific (akhass) than necessary (daruri) because necessary includes both badihi (which has no cause at all) and other types like sensations and customary knowledge which have causes but those causes are not in our power. The passage also explains that mere intellectual attention is not a sufficient cause for badihi; it is merely a common factor, and the specificity of the terms plays a role. The commentator warns against confusion on this point.

Misconception: Some think that 'badihi' means any knowledge that comes naturally, even if it involves the senses or reasoning.
Correction: The passage clarifies that badihi is exclusively what the intellect affirms without any assistance from senses or other causes.

Checkpoint
According to al-Iji, what is the defining characteristic of badihi knowledge?
A) It is obtained through sensory perception.
B) It is known by mere intellect without any external aid.
C) It requires careful reasoning and theoretical reflection.
D) It is always about external objects.

Key Takeaway
Badihi knowledge is what the mind grasps directly by itself, without any help from senses or reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time when you experienced intense pleasure or pain. Did you need any intellectual reasoning to know that you were feeling it? That immediate certainty is a type of necessary knowledge. How often do you trust that kind of direct knowledge from your heart or conscience about spiritual matters? Is there a truth about your relationship with Allah that you know with that same certainty but you avoid acting on it? Commit today to acting on one such truth.
?
The passage says that customary knowledge (like the stability of mountains) is considered necessary because of repeated experience. Are there spiritual habits or acts of worship that you have done so often that they have become certain for you? Do they still feel like that, or have they become routine? Reflect on one practice that you can restore to its original certainty today.
?
The law of non-contradiction is presented as a truth that has no cause and is always present in the mind. Is there a contradiction in your life between what you claim to believe and how you live? Identify one such contradiction and commit to resolving it today.

Badihi vs Daruri: The Role of Attention and Causality
Al-Iji answers an objection about attention and clarifies why badihi is more specific.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why badihi is more specific than daruri and how the concept of 'attention' relates to causation in self-evident knowledge.

Core Idea
Badihi has no cause at all; daruri includes knowledge with a cause not in our power.

The previous lesson defined badihi as what the intellect knows without aid. Now al-Iji addresses a subtle objection: if badihi requires attention (which is in our power), doesn't that make it acquired? He explains that attention is merely a common factor, not a complete cause. This clarifies why badihi is a subset of the necessary, not the acquired. This lesson deepens your grasp of how these categories work in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the extended passage and translation
This passage is denser. Read it carefully, following the objection and the response about attention being insufficient as a cause.
Look for the phrase 'ليس ذلك سبباً له' – that's the key point.
2
Follow the objection and reply
The objector says: attention is in our power, and badihi is attained by attention, so it would be acquired. Al-Iji replies that attention is not the cause; it's just a prerequisite shared by all knowledge.
The specificity of the terms (the content itself) plays the role, not attention.
3
Understand the akhass relation
Badihi is more specific because it has no cause. Daruri includes both badihi (no cause) and other necessary knowledge that has a cause but that cause is not in our power (like sensations).
This is the final distinction.
4
Heed the warning
Al-Iji ends with 'so be straight, for feet have slipped'. This indicates that the distinction is subtle and many have erred.
Reflect on why confusion might arise.

?

Will you now approach empirical knowledge with more humility, acknowledging its dependence on causes outside your will?
?

Could it actually be caused by something you hadn't noticed (like repetition, emotion, or external influence)? Identify one such belief and trace whether it has a cause. Will you investigate it today?
?
Attention is necessary for all knowledge, but it is not a sufficient cause. You cannot will yourself to know something truly—you must also have the right object and conditions. Where in your life have you tried to produce certainty by sheer mental effort, ignoring the objective grounds? This lesson forces you to recognize that knowledge is not entirely in your power. Will you now ask yourself: Am I seeking truth or just the feeling of knowing? What would change if you admitted that some truths are simply given, not achieved?

Definition of Acquired Knowledge and the Problem of Power
Al-Iji defines acquired knowledge and addresses objections about its relation to power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of acquired knowledge (muktasab) and the key objections regarding its relation to divine power and human power, including the role of customary causality.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge is in our power, but its exact relation to divine power remains a delicate theological puzzle.

In this passage, al-Iji defines acquired knowledge as that which is in our power (maqdur). He then addresses a series of objections concerning how power (qudra) relates to necessary knowledge and acquired knowledge, clarifying the role of customary causality and the limits of human power. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion on the nature of theoretical knowledge and its distinction from the necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of acquired knowledge
Start by reading the opening lines where al-Iji defines acquired knowledge as 'knowledge that is in one's power'. Notice that he immediately distinguishes it from necessary knowledge.
The definition is straightforward but leads to a complex debate.
2
Follow the objection about attention
The first objection argues that even necessary knowledge involves attention (iltifat), which is in our power. Al-Iji responds by distinguishing attention as a common factor, not a sufficient cause.
This shows that the definition hinges on what 'being in one's power' means causally.
3
Understand the interplay of power and necessary knowledge
The text then explains that necessary knowledge has no cause in our power, or its cause is not in our power, while acquired knowledge does. The subsequent objection challenges this, and the response clarifies that power is considered independently by custom.
This is the heart of the problem: how to preserve the contingency of acquired knowledge without undermining God's sole creative power.
4
Analyze the final objection and response
The final objection points out that many necessary sciences also depend on mere attention (which is in our power), so they would be acquired—thus contradicting the equivalence of theoretical and acquired. Al-Iji leaves this as an open issue to be resolved later.
This shows the dialectical nature of kalam: objections drive further clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكسبي يقابل الضروري) فهو العلم المقدور. قوله : ( وكالعلم بالأمور التي إلخ ) أي العلم البديهي، وإذا لم يكن له سبب صدق أنه ليس تحصيله مقدوراً لنا إذ لا معنى لكون العلم مقدوراً إلا كون سببه وطريقه مقدوراً فإن قلت أليس ذلك العلم حاصلا لنا الالتفات المقدور لنا، فيكون مقدوراً قلت : الالتفات قدر مشترك بمجرد بين جميع العلوم، فليس ذلك سبباً له بل لخصوصية الأطراف مدخل فيه، ومعني كون مجرد الالتفات كافياً فيه أنه لا احتياج فيه إلى سبب آخر، لا أنه سبب تام كما أشار إليه الشارح بقوله : من غير استعانة بحس أو غيره أي من الأسباب . قوله : ( فهو أخص من الضروري لأنه الذي لا يكون تحصيله مقدوراً بأن لا يكون له سبب مقدور يدور معه وجوده وعدمه، وذلك بأن لا يكون له سبب يدور معه، وهو البديهي أو يكون له سبب يدور معه لكن لا يكون مقدوراً كالحسيات والتجربيات والعاديات، وغير ذلك فاستقم فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام . قوله : بخلاف) النظريات إلخ يرد عليه أن اعتبار القدرة على التحصيل فيها إن كان بطريق المدخلية في الجملة عادة كما هو الظاهر، يلزم أن تكون العلوم الضرورية التي تتوقف على قدرتنا في الجملة، كما تتوقف على التجربة والإحساس غير ضرورية، فإنه مما فيه مدخل لقدرة المخلوق وإن كان على وجه الكفاية والاستقلال، فهو خلاف المذهب فإن قلت : نختار أن القدرة قد اعتبرت على وجه الاستقلال عادة بمعنى أن الكسبي يتوقف على مجرد قدرتنا عادة والضروري ليس كذلك، بل يتوقف على أمور خارجة عنها، وإن توقف عليها أيضاً في الجملة، قلت : إن الكسبيات كما تتوقف على قدرتنا تتوقف على أشياء ضرورية كالمبادي الضرورية مثلاً على أن عدم العلم بتلك الأشياء بخصوصها لا يستلزم العلم بالعدم، فلا نسلم أن العلم بالكسبيات إنما يحصل بمجرد قدرة المخلوق، وأيضاً كثير من العلوم الضرورية يحصل بمجرد التفاتنا المقدور لنا كما يدل عليه تفسيره الآتي للبديهي فيلزم أن يكون ذلك من الكسبيات والتزامه ينافي ما سيجيء من أن النظري والكسبي متساويان صدقاً، اللهم إلا أن يمنع بمجرد الالتفات ويؤول ما سيذكره بما سنذكره .
(And the acquired is opposite to the necessary) so it is knowledge that is in one's power. His statement: '(And like knowledge of matters that...)' i.e., self-evident knowledge. When it has no cause, it is true that its attainment is not in our power, for the meaning of knowledge being in our power is only that its cause and path are in our power. If you say: is not that knowledge attained by our attention which is in our power, so it would be in our power? I say: attention is a common factor among all sciences, so it is not a cause for it, rather the specificity of the terms has an entry in it. And the meaning of mere attention being sufficient for it is that there is no need for another cause, not that it is a complete cause, as the commentator pointed out by his statement: 'without assistance of sense or anything else' from the causes. His statement: '(so it is more specific than the necessary)' because the necessary is that whose attainment is not in our power, either by having no cause that co-varies with its existence and non-existence (and that is the self-evident) or by having a cause that co-varies but is not in our power, like sensations, experiments, and customary matters, and others. So be straight, for feet have slipped therein. His statement: '(unlike theoretical matters...)' an objection occurs that if the consideration of power over attainment is by way of entry in general customarily, as is apparent, then it would follow that the necessary sciences that depend on our power in general, as they depend on experiment and sensation, would be non-necessary, for they involve entry of the creature's power, even if it is in a sufficient and independent manner, that is contrary to the school. For if you say: we choose that power is considered in an independent manner customarily, meaning the acquired depends on our mere power customarily, while the necessary is not like that; rather it depends on things outside it, even if it also depends on them in general. I say: acquired sciences, as they depend on our power, also depend on necessary things like necessary principles, for example, and not knowing those things in their particularity does not imply knowledge of non-existence, so we do not concede that acquired knowledge is obtained only by the mere power of the creature. Also, many necessary sciences are obtained by our mere attention which is in our power, as his later interpretation of the self-evident indicates, so it would follow that they are acquired, and adhering to that contradicts what will come that theoretical and acquired are equivalent in extension, unless one denies that it is by mere attention and interprets what he will mention as we will mention.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines acquired knowledge (muktasab) as knowledge that is in our power (maqdur). It then addresses a key objection: if power is considered in a general customary way, then necessary knowledge (which also partly depends on our power, like experiment and sensation) would become non-necessary. The response clarifies that power in acquired knowledge is independent and sufficient by custom, while necessary knowledge depends on external things beyond our power. However, the objector further argues that acquired knowledge also depends on necessary principles (which are not in our power), so it is not solely by our power. Also, many necessary sciences are obtained by mere attention (which is in our power), so they would be acquired, contradicting the equivalence of theoretical and acquired. The passage leaves this as an issue to be resolved.

Key Terms
كسبي
مقدور
نظري
قدرة
استقلال
عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Acquired knowledge is solely by human power without any dependence on God or necessary principles.
Correction: The passage shows that acquired knowledge depends on necessary principles (al-mabadi' al-daruriyya) which are not in our power, so it is not solely by human power. Power is considered by custom, but divine causality remains foundational.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection raised against defining acquired knowledge as that obtained by mere power?
A) The evidence is contradictory.
B) Acquired knowledge also depends on necessary principles not in our power.
C) There is no such thing as acquired knowledge.
D) Necessary knowledge is also obtained by power.

Key Takeaway
Acquired knowledge is in our power by custom but still depends on necessary principles, making its relation to divine power a subtle theological issue requiring careful interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your acquired knowledge of Allah depends not only on your effort but also on necessary principles that are given to you. Does that realization make you more humble or more reliant on your own intellect? How will you seek knowledge from now on—with pride in your reasoning or with a sense of dependence?
?
The passage admits a tension: some necessary knowledge seems to come from attention (in your power), which would make it acquired. Does it trouble you that even the most basic certainties might rest on a fragile distinction? What does that say about the certainty of your faith?
?
If power alone does not guarantee knowledge, then your knowledge of the truth is not entirely your doing. Will you thank Allah for every truth you grasp, or will you still feel that you earned it? How does this change your daily engagement with learning?

Badihi: Knowledge Established by Mere Reason
Al-Jurjani defines badihi and its relation to daruri.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of badihi (self-evident) and its relationship to daruri (necessary) as more specific, including the distinction based on causality and power.

Core Idea
Badihi is a subset of necessary knowledge distinguished by having no cause within power.

Al-Jurjani begins by defining a key epistemological term: the self-evident (badihi). This passage clarifies how badihi relates to necessary knowledge (daruri) and warns against common confusions. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how knowledge is classified in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the precise wording: 'mere reason' and 'without assistance of a sense'. Understand that badihi can be either a concept or a judgment.
The term 'badihi' is often translated as self-evident or axiomatic.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement that badihi is more specific than daruri. Note that the passage says they are sometimes used synonymously, but technically badihi is a subset.
3
Understand the reasoning
The passage explains why badihi is more specific: necessary knowledge includes things not within power. Two cases: (1) no cause at all (badihi) and (2) a cause not within power (sensory, experimental, customary). This shows badihi is a subset.
The key distinction is whether knowledge is within human power to acquire or not.
4
Reflect on the warning
The author ends with a caution: 'So be upright, for feet have slipped in this.' Understand that many scholars have erred in distinguishing these terms.
This emphasizes the importance of precise terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبديهي ما يثبته مجرد العقل) أي يثبته التفاته غير استعانة بحس ، أو غيره تصوراً كان أو تصديقاً فهو أخص من الضروري، وقد يطلق مرادفاً له .
قوله : ( فهو أخص من الضروري لأنه الذي لا يكون تحصيله مقدوراً بأن لا يكون له سبب مقدور يدور معه وجوده وعدمه، وذلك بأن لا يكون له سبب يدور معه، وهو البديهي أو يكون له سبب يدور معه لكن لا يكون مقدوراً كالحسيات والتجربيات والعاديات، وغير ذلك فاستقم فإنه قد زل فيه أقدام .
And the self-evident (badihi) is that which mere reason establishes—that is, its attention establishes it without assistance of a sense or other [means], whether it is a concept or an assent. So it is more specific than the necessary (daruri), and sometimes it is used synonymously with it. His statement: (so it is more specific than the necessary) because that whose acquisition is not within power—that is, it has no cause within power upon which its existence and non-existence depend—that is either because it has no cause that it depends on, and that is the badihi, or because it has a cause that it depends on but that cause is not within one's power, such as sensory, experimental, and customary things, and other than that. So be upright, for feet have slipped in this.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines the self-evident (badihi) and clarifies its relationship to necessary knowledge (daruri). The author states that badihi is what the intellect establishes by mere attention, without relying on any sense or external means, whether it is a concept or an assent. He then notes that badihi is more specific than daruri, though sometimes used interchangeably. The commentary explains why it is more specific: necessary knowledge includes both knowledge that is not within human power (like badihi) and knowledge that has a cause but that cause is not within power (like sensory or customary knowledge). The core idea is that badihi is a subset of daruri distinguished by its lack of any cause beyond rational attention. The author warns that mistakes have been made on this point.

Key Terms
بديهي (badihi)
ضروري (daruri)
مقدور (maqdur)
سبب (sabab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All necessary knowledge is self-evident (badihi).
Correction: Necessary knowledge includes both self-evident knowledge (no cause) and knowledge that has a cause but not within power (e.g., sensory). Badihi is only the former.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is badihi more specific than daruri?
A) Because badihi is always a concept.
B) Because badihi has no cause at all that its existence depends on, while daruri may have a non-power cause.
C) Because badihi is acquired by effort.
D) Because daruri is always within human power.

Key Takeaway
Badihi is a specific type of necessary knowledge that relies solely on reason without any cause within human power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any belief you hold about God or existence that you consider self-evident, but upon reflection might depend on external factors like upbringing or sense experience? How would you test it against the definition of badihi given in this lesson?
?
The author warns that 'feet have slipped' on this distinction. Have you ever confidently asserted something as obvious without checking whether it meets the criteria of pure rational necessity? What would it cost you to examine that claim today?
?
If badihi knowledge requires no cause within your power, what does that imply about the foundational truths of your faith? Are you willing to accept that some of your certainties may not be badihi but still necessary? How would you differentiate?

Defining Kasbi: Knowledge Attainable by Created Power
Al-Jurjani defines kasbi and clarifies the purpose of its restriction to created power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of kasbi (acquired knowledge) and why the phrase 'by the created power' is essential to distinguish it from other types of knowledge.

Core Idea
Kasbi is knowledge we can earn, but only through our own created power.

In this passage, al-Jurjani sharpens the definition of acquired knowledge (kasbi) and explains a crucial restriction. He wants to make sure we do not confuse kasbi with necessary knowledge or with divine knowledge. Pay attention to how he uses two different reasons for the same phrase.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Let the author's wording sink in.
Notice the repeated phrase 'بالقدرة الحادثة'.
2
Understand the first reason: excluding daruri
Al-Jurjani says the phrase 'by the created power' excludes necessary knowledge (daruri) because necessary knowledge is attainable in us by eternal power, not created power.
This distinguishes kasbi from innate knowledge.
3
Understand the second nuance: not about qadim
The same phrase is not needed to exclude eternal knowledge, because eternal knowledge is already excluded by 'whose acquisition is within power' — it is not acquired at all.
This shows precision: the restriction has a specific target.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكسبي يقابل الضروري) فهو العلم المقدور تحصيله بالقدرة الحادثة .
قوله : (بالقدرة الحادثة) هذا القيد لإخراج العلم الضروري لأنه مقدور التحصيل فينا بالقدرة القديمة .
قوله : ( بالقدرة الحادثة) هذا القيد لزيادة التوضيح لا للاحتراز عن العلم القديم، لخروجه بالمقدور تحصيله سواء أريد بالعلم القديم الصفة القديمة، أو تعلقاتها أما على الأول فلانها بطريق الإيجاب، كما سيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى، وأما على الثاني فلما صرحوا به من وجوب تعلقها بكل ما يجوز تعلقها به.
And the acquired (kasbi) is opposite to the necessary (daruri); it is knowledge whose acquisition is within power by the created power (al-qudrat al-haditha). His statement: (by the created power) this restriction is to exclude necessary knowledge, because it is within power of acquisition in us by the eternal power. His statement: (by the created power) this restriction is for additional clarification, not to exclude eternal knowledge, because it is excluded by 'whose acquisition is within power'—whether by eternal knowledge is meant the eternal attribute or its relations; as for the first, because it is by way of causation (ijab), as will come, Allah willing; and as for the second, because they have stated the necessity of its relation to everything it is possible to relate to.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines acquired knowledge (kasbi) as the opposite of necessary knowledge (daruri). Kasbi is knowledge that can be attained through human power, specifically created power (al-qudrat al-haditha). The commentary explains the purpose of the phrase 'by the created power': it excludes necessary knowledge, which is attained in us by divine eternal power, not by our own. The author then adds a second clarification: the restriction is not to exclude eternal knowledge (al-'ilm al-qadim), because eternal knowledge is already excluded by the phrase 'whose acquisition is within power'—since eternal knowledge is not acquired at all. This holds whether eternal knowledge means God's attribute or its objects, as God's knowledge is necessary and not subject to acquisition.

Key Terms
كسبي (kasbi)
ضروري (daruri)
قدرة حادثة (qudra haditha)
قدرة قديمة (qudra qadima)
علم قديم ('ilm qadim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge within our power is kasbi.
Correction: Kasbi is limited to knowledge attained by created power; necessary knowledge (like self-evident or sensory) is also within our power in a sense, but it is attained by divine power, not created power.

Checkpoint
Why is the phrase 'by the created power' added in the definition of kasbi?
A) To indicate that kasbi is impossible without divine power.
B) To exclude necessary knowledge, which is attained by eternal power.
C) To show that all knowledge is within our power.
D) To include knowledge of the past.

Key Takeaway
The definition of kasbi explicitly limits it to knowledge attainable by created power, thereby excluding necessary knowledge (from God) and eternal knowledge (God's own).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you attribute your acquired knowledge to your own efforts, forgetting that even the power to acquire it is created by Allah? Reflect on a time you felt proud of your learning. Did you acknowledge that your ability to learn is a gift from Him?
?
The text distinguishes between knowledge attained by created power and knowledge granted directly by God (like necessary knowledge). Consider your daily life: in what areas do you rely on your own 'created power' versus recognizing Allah's direct provision? What would change if you saw all your knowledge as either a gift or a trust?
?
The phrase 'by the created power' is for clarification, not exclusion. How often do you misinterpret the intentions behind divine commands or definitions, adding restrictions where there are none? This week, when you learn a new ruling or concept, ask yourself: Am I adding unnecessary boundaries that Allah did not place?

Defining Nazari: Entailment, Not Causation
Al-Jurjani defines nazari via entailment and rejects causation and generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school defines theoretical knowledge (nazari) as what correct reasoning habitually entails, not causes.

Core Idea
Theoretical knowledge is entailed by reasoning, not caused by it.

In this passage, al-Jurjani gives the Ash'ari definition of theoretical knowledge (nazari) by carefully distinguishing it from two rejected alternatives. He explains why saying 'caused by reasoning' or 'occurs after reasoning' would either commit to Mu'tazili causation or include necessary knowledge that isn't theoretical. The key is that nazari is 'entailed' by correct reasoning, following the Ash'ari principle of habitual sequence (ada).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Start by reading the Arabic phrase defining nazari as 'what correct reasoning entails'. Notice the technical term tadammun (entailment).
The definition is attributed to al-Qadi and explained by al-Amidi.
2
Understand the entailment
Al-Amidi explains that entailment means that without impediments, correct reasoning is never separate from this knowledge, yet without causation or generation.
The Ash'ari school rejects causation (ijab) and generation (tawlid).
3
See what is rejected
The author rejects two alternative definitions: 'what reasoning causes' and 'what occurs after reasoning'. Understand why each is problematic.
4
Examine the exclusions
Pay attention to how the phrases 'would not be separate' and 'does not occur except with it' exclude certain types of knowledge from the definition.
These exclusions refine the definition to only theoretical knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النظري فهو ما يتضمنه النظر الصحيح ) هذه عبارة القاضي قال : الآمدي معنى تضمنه له أنهما بحال لو قدر انتفاء الآفات، وأضداد العلم لم ينفك النظر الصحيح عنه بلا إيجاب وتوليد مع أنه لا يحصل إلا معه ( ولم نقل ما يوجبه ) النظر الصحيح كما قاله بعضهم (إذ ليس إيجاب النظر للعلم ( مذهبنا ) بل حصوله عقيبه بطريق العادة عندنا ولم نقل أيضا (ما يحصل عقيبه)  ليدخل في الحد ) حينئذ (بعض الضروريات ( أعني ما يحصل من الضروريات عقيب النظر الصحيح، كالعلم بما يحدث به من الألم واللذة والفرح والغم، ونحو ذلك.
قوله : (لم ينفك النظر الصحيح عنه خرج به العلم بما يحدث به من الألم واللذة والفرح فإنه ينفك النظر الصحيح عنه .
قوله : (مع أنه لا يحصل إلا معه متعلق بلم ينفك أي مع عدم انفكاك النظر عنه يكون مختصاً حصوله بالنظر خرج به العلم بالعلم بالشيء الحاصل عقيب النظر، لكنه لا اختصاص له بالنظر لكونه تابعاً للعلم بالشيء، سواء كان العلم بالشيء حاصلاً بالنظر أو بدونه.
And as for the theoretical (nazari), it is that which correct reasoning (al-nazr al-sahih) entails (yatadammanahu). This is the expression of al-Qadi. Al-Amidi said: The meaning of its entailing it is that they are in such a state that, if we were to suppose the removal of impediments and opposites of knowledge, correct reasoning would not be separate from it without causation (ijab) and generation (tawlid), while it does not occur except with it. (And we did not say 'what it causes') as some said, because causation of reasoning for knowledge is not our school; rather, its occurrence after it in the way of habit (adah) is our view. And we did not say 'what occurs after it' because then some necessary knowledge would enter the definition, namely what occurs from necessary knowledge after correct reasoning, such as knowledge of what happens to one of pain, pleasure, joy, grief, and the like. His statement: (correct reasoning would not be separate from it) excludes knowledge of what happens to one of pain, pleasure, joy, for correct reasoning is separate from it. His statement: (while it does not occur except with it) is dependent on 'would not be separate', i.e., with the non-separation of reasoning from it, its occurrence is specific to reasoning; this excludes knowledge of the knowledge of a thing that occurs after reasoning, for it has no specificity to reasoning because it is dependent on knowledge of the thing, whether that knowledge of the thing occurs by reasoning or without it.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage defines theoretical knowledge (nazari) and clarifies its relationship with correct reasoning (al-nazr al-sahih). The author adopts the definition that nazari is 'what correct reasoning entails', citing al-Qadi and explaining through al-Amidi that entailment means that under normal conditions (without impediments), correct reasoning is never separate from this knowledge, yet without causing or generating it; the knowledge only occurs with reasoning. The author then explains two things they did not say: First, they did not say 'what reasoning causes' (yujib), because the Ash'ari school rejects causation (ijab) and instead holds that knowledge habitually follows reasoning (ada). Second, they did not say 'what occurs after it' (yahsul 'aqibahu), because that would include some necessary knowledge that occurs after reasoning (like knowing one's own pain from a cause), which is not theoretical. The commentary elaborates that the phrase 'would not be separate' excludes knowledge that can occur without reasoning (e.g., feeling pain from a cause other than reasoning). The phrase 'does not occur except with it' further specifies that the knowledge is unique to reasoning, excluding knowledge of one's own knowledge, which can occur without reasoning.

Key Terms
نظري
نظر صحيح
تضمن
إيجاب
عادة
توليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theoretical knowledge is caused by reasoning (causation/ijab).
Correction: The Ash'ari school holds that knowledge habitually follows reasoning without causation; nazari is entailed, not caused.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the phrase 'what reasoning causes' in defining nazari?
A) Because reasoning is not the only way to get knowledge.
B) Because causation is not the Ash'ari school's doctrine; they hold that knowledge habitually follows reasoning.
C) Because reasoning sometimes fails.
D) Because all knowledge is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari definition of nazari is that it is what correct reasoning habitually entails, not caused by it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your logical reasoning directly causes certainty in your heart? How does the Ash'ari distinction between causation and habitual sequence challenge your reliance on your own reasoning?
?
When you seek knowledge through study, do you feel you 'produce' it? What would change if you saw it as a gift from Allah that merely habitually follows your effort?
?
Are there areas where you treat your reasoning as a necessary cause rather than a context for Allah's creation of knowledge? What would it look like to depend on Allah while still using reasoning diligently?

Tasawwur and Tasdiq as Necessarily Known by Wijdan
Commentary on the third objective: tasawwur and tasdiq are partly necessary by wijdan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how direct inner experience (wijdan) serves as a foundation for necessary knowledge and under what conditions it constitutes a valid proof in debate.

Core Idea
Direct inner experience is the bedrock of knowledge, but it only proves what is universally shared.

This passage tackles a foundational issue in epistemology: how do we know that some of our concepts and judgments are necessary? The commentator argues that we can rely on direct inner experience (wijdan) that all rational people share. But then he addresses a crucial objection: if someone denies that experience, can we still use it as a proof? The answer depends on who the denier is.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the commentator uses a very compressed style with embedded objections.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the statement that tasawwur and tasdiq are partly necessary by wijdan. Understand that 'necessary' here means self-evident through inner experience.
The claim is the starting point for the entire discussion.
3
Understand the condition for wijdan as proof
The passage explains that wijdan is a proof (hujja) only when it is known to be shared by all rational persons. This is why the text says 'every rational person'.
Wijdan that is not universally shared cannot be used as evidence against others.
4
Examine the objection and response
The objection is that wijdan is not a proof against others. The commentator responds by distinguishing two types of deniers: the obstinate and the ignorant. Each requires a different approach.
This shows that wijdan is sometimes accepted and sometimes rejected depending on the opponent.
