المقصد الثالث : أن كلاً من التصور والتصديق بعضه ضروري بالوجدان) فإن كل عاقل يجد من نفسه أن له تصورات وتصديقات ضرورية. قوله: (غير مقدور بن) لكونهما فعل الغير. قوله: (لاحتياجها إلخ) فلا يكون مقدوراً للمخلوق لأن المراد منه أن يكون مقدوراً للكل، أو الأكثر والتصفية ليس مقدوراً إلا بالنسبة إلى الأقل الذي يفي مزاجه بالمجاهدات الشاقة فاندفع ما قيل أن الاحتياج المذكور يقتضي صعوبة الحصول لا تعذره ليخرج عن المقدورية. قوله: (ولا معنى لكون العلم إلخ إذ لا قدرة عليه إلا باعتبار التحصيل بسبب من الأسباب. قوله: (فإن كل عاقل إلخ) أشار بذكر كل إلى أن الوجدان المذكور معلوم الاشتراك بين الكل، فيكون حجة بخلاف الوجدان الذي لا يقطع باشتراكه فإنه لا يكون حجة على الغير إلا بعد إثبات الاشتراك. قوله: (لاحتياجها إلى مجاهدات إلخ قد يناقش فيه بأن الاحتياج إلى ما ذكره يقتضي صعوبة الحصول لا تعذره، ليخرج عن المقدورية ولهذا جعلوا القدرة مبدأ لمعني في الحيوان به، يمكن أن يصدر عنه أفعال شاقة بل التوجه التام المستتبع للإلهام استتباعاً عادياً، كاستتباع النظر للنتيجة على ما هو طريق حكماء الهند طريق مقدور أيضاً، وسيأتي تتمة لهذا الكلام. قوله: (فإن كل علم مقدور لنا يتضمنه النظر (الصحيح مبني على ما أشرنا إليه من أن التوقف على الأمور الغير المقدورة معتبر في البديهيات اعتباره في الحسيات. قوله: (ضروري بالوجدان إن قلت: الوجدان ليس بحجة على الغير، قلت: المنكر إما معاند يجحد الحق مع عرفانه، فيعرض عنه لأن المكابرة تسد باب المناظرة، وإما جاهل بمعنى ما أنكره، فيفهم معناه ليرجع إلى وجدانه ويعود عن إنكاره كذا ذكره الشارح في حواشي شرح المختصر، وبهذا يعلم أن التشبث بالوجدان تارة يسمع في باب المناظرة وأخرى يرد بأنه ليس بحجة على الغير وكان السر في ذلك أن الأحكام متفاوتة جلاء، وخفاء فيدور عليه القبول
The third objective: that each of tasawwur (conception) and tasdiq (assent) is partly necessary (daruri) by wijdan (direct experience). For every rational person finds in himself that he has necessary conceptions and assents. His statement: (not attainable [؟]) because they are acts of another. His statement: (because of requiring, etc.)—so it is not attainable for the creature, because what is intended is that it be attainable for all or most, and purification is not attainable except for the few whose temperament allows the difficult struggles. Thus the objection that the mentioned requirement implies difficulty of obtaining, not impossibility so as to exclude it from being attainable, is repelled. His statement: (and there is no meaning for knowledge, etc.)—for there is no power over it except by way of attainment through some means. His statement: (for every rational person, etc.)—he indicated by mentioning 'every' that the mentioned wijdan is known to be shared by all, so it serves as a proof, unlike wijdan that is not known to be shared, for it is not a proof against others except after establishing its sharedness. His statement: (because of requiring struggles, etc.) may be objected to by saying that the requirement for what he mentioned implies difficulty, not impossibility, so it does not exclude it from being attainable; for this reason they made power a principle for an animal by which it can produce difficult actions. Rather, complete attention that habitually results in inspiration, just as reasoning results in the conclusion, according to the way of the sages of India, is also an attainable way. A completion of this discussion will come. His statement: (for every knowledge attainable for us is entailed by correct reasoning) is based on what we indicated, that dependence on non-attainable matters is considered in self-evident matters just as it is considered in sensory matters. His statement: (necessary by wijdan). If you say: wijdan is not a proof against others, I say: the denier is either an obstinate person who denies the truth while knowing it, so one turns away from him because stubbornness closes the door of debate; or he is ignorant of the meaning of what he denies, so he is made to understand its meaning so that he may return to his own wijdan and retract his denial. This is what the commentator mentioned in the glosses on Sharh al-Mukhtasar. By this it is known that clinging to wijdan is sometimes accepted in the arena of debate and sometimes rejected on the grounds that it is not a proof against others. The secret behind that is that judgments vary in clarity and obscurity, and acceptance revolves around that.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objective (al-maqsad al-thalith) from the original text: that both conceptions (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat) are partly necessary by wijdan (direct inner experience). The commentator explains that every rational person finds in themselves necessary conceptions and assents—these are self-evident by introspection. The commentary then addresses a series of points: (1) The statement 'غير مقدور لنا' (not attainable for us) regarding inspiration and teaching is because they are acts of another (God or the Prophet). (2) The objection that purification requires difficult struggles but that implies only difficulty, not impossibility, is rebutted by noting that the intended meaning of 'attainable' is that it is attainable for the majority, not for the exceptional few. (3) The commentator clarifies that wijdan (direct experience) is a proof only when it is known to be shared by all rational persons. Therefore, the text mentions 'every rational person' to indicate commonality. (4) An objection is raised that the requirement of struggles implies difficulty, not impossibility, so purification could still be considered a way to knowledge. The commentator responds that even if difficult, it is not a standard attainable way for most, but some (like the Indian sages) consider intense attention leading to inspiration as a valid method. (5) The statement that every attainable knowledge is entailed by correct reasoning is based on the principle that dependence on non-attainable matters is acceptable in self-evident matters just as it is in sensory matters. (6) Finally, the passage addresses the objection that wijdan is not a proof against others. The response distinguishes two types of deniers: the obstinate who knowingly reject the truth—they are to be turned away from because stubbornness prevents dialogue; and the ignorant who do not understand the meaning—they must be taught the meaning so they can see it in their own wijdan and withdraw their denial. This shows that wijdan is sometimes accepted as a proof and sometimes rejected, depending on the clarity of the judgment.

Misconception: One might think that wijdan is always a valid proof in debate.
Correction: The passage explains that wijdan is valid only when the experience is universally shared, and even then it may be rejected if the opponent is obstinate or ignorant.

Checkpoint

A) When the other person is not rational.
B) When the experience is not known to be shared by all rational persons.
C) When the experience is about sensory matters.
D) When the other person has already denied it.

Key Takeaway
Direct inner experience (wijdan) is a valid source of knowledge only when it is universally shared, and its use in debate depends on whether the opponent is obstinate or ignorant.

Proof that Some Conceptions and Assents Are Necessary by Intuition
Proof that some conceptions and assents are necessary by intuition to avoid circularity or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some conceptions and assents must be intuitive (ḍarūrī) and not acquired through reflection, based on the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
If all knowledge were acquired through reflection, acquisition would be impossible.

In this lesson, we study a foundational proof in Islamic logic and epistemology. The author argues that not all conceptions and assents can be theoretical (naẓarī); some must be necessary (ḍarūrī) and intuitive. Otherwise, we would face an impossible chain of dependence, either circular or infinite. This passage presents the core argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Note the technical terms: تصور, تصديق, ضروري, نظري.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that some conceptions and assents are necessary by intuition, i.e., obtained without power or reflection.
This is the thesis to be proved.
3
Understand the conditional argument
The author argues that if it were not for some being necessary, circularity or infinite regress would result. Trace the logic: every conception/assent would be theoretical, leading to dependence on another, and so on.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress
Both are invalid (bāṭil) and impossible (mumtaniʿ), preventing acquisition. Since they are impossible, what depends on them is also impossible.
The conclusion is that the assumption that all are theoretical is false.
5
Conclude the necessity of intuitive knowledge
Therefore, some conceptions and assents must be necessary by intuition. This refutes the possibility that all knowledge is acquired through reflection.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : التصور والتصديق بعضه ضروري بالوجدان. نفسه أن بعض تصوراته وكذا بعض تصديقاته حاصل له بلا قدرة منه ولا نظر فيه. وإذ لولاه أي لولا أن بعضاً من كل منهما ضروري لزم الدور أو التسلسل، إذ حينئذ يكون كل واحد من التصور، وكذا كل واحد من التصديق نظرياً، فإذا حاولنا تحصيل شيء منهما كان ذلك التحصيل مستنداً إلى تصور أو تصديق آخر، وهو أيضاً نظري مستند إلى غيره من التصورات أو التصديقات، فإما أن يدور الاستناد في مرتبة من المراتب أو يتسلسل إلى ما لا يتناهى، وهما يمنعان الاكتساب لأنهما باطلان ممتنعان، فما يتوقف عليهما كان باطلاً ممتنعاً، وحينئذ يلزم أن لا يكون شيء من التصور والتصديق حاصلاً لنا، وهو باطل قطعاً.
The third observatory - the third purpose: Conception and assent, some of each is necessary by intuition. It itself that some of its conceptions and likewise some of its assents are obtained for it without its power and without reflection therein. And if not for that, i.e., if not for some of each being necessary, circularity or infinite regress would be necessary. For then every conception and every assent would be theoretical. So if we attempt to acquire something from them, that acquisition would be dependent on another conception or assent, which is also theoretical, dependent on others of conceptions or assents. Then either the dependence circles in a certain rank or regresses infinitely. And both prevent acquisition because they are invalid and impossible. So what depends on them is invalid and impossible. Then it follows that nothing of conception and assent is obtained for us, which is absolutely false.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a proof that some conceptions and assents must be necessary (ḍarūrī) and intuitive (bi-al-wijdān), not theoretical (naẓarī). The author begins by stating the claim: some conceptions and assents are obtained by a person without power or reflection. He then provides a conditional argument: if it were not for some being necessary, circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) would occur. The reasoning is that if every conception and every assent were theoretical, then when trying to acquire any of them, that acquisition would depend on another theoretical item, which itself depends on another, leading either to circularity (where the dependence loops back to the starting point) or to an infinite chain. Both circularity and infinite regress are impossible—they prevent acquisition (iktisāb) because they involve the impossible. Therefore, anything that depends on them is impossible. Consequently, if all were theoretical, nothing would be acquired at all, which is false by fact. Thus, some must be intuitive. The conclusion is that the supposition of all being theoretical leads to a falsehood, so it is rejected.

تصديق
ضروري
نظري
وجدان
دور

اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary knowledge is innate without any cause.
Correction: Here, necessary means it is not acquired through reflection, but it is still obtained (ḥāṣil) without one's power — it does not imply absence of any cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that some conceptions and assents must be necessary by intuition?
A) Because all knowledge is theoretical.
B) To avoid circularity or infinite regress in acquisition.
C) Because the Qur'an commands it.
D) Because it is self-evident to all.

Key Takeaway
Some conceptions and assents must be necessary by intuition; otherwise, all knowledge acquisition would be impossible due to circularity or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one belief you hold about Allah that you consider certain. Can you trace how you came to hold it? Does it ultimately rest on something you accepted without reason? Are you willing to admit that your certainty might be grounded in an intuition you cannot prove?
?
You rely on your intellect to gain knowledge. But if every single piece of knowledge required proof from another proof, you would never know anything. What is one truth you accept without proof? How does acknowledging that change your humility before your Creator?
?
The proof shows that without intuitive knowledge, acquisition is impossible. Reflect on your own journey: have you ever prided yourself on your reasoning, forgetting that the very tools of reasoning are gifts you did not earn? What would you lose if you had to defend every belief from scratch?

Objection and Answer: The Proof Itself Is Theoretical
Objection and answer about the proof being theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends his proof that not all knowledge is theoretical by distinguishing between knowledge under a hypothesis and knowledge in actuality.

Core Idea
A proof can be valid in reality even if assumed theoretical in a hypothesis.

In this passage, the author anticipates an objection to his earlier argument that not all knowledge can be theoretical. The objector claims that the proof itself would then be theoretical, leading to circularity or infinite regress. The response clarifies a crucial distinction: the premises of the proof are known in actuality, not merely assumed within the opponent's theory. This saves the argument from being self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's claim: if all knowledge is theoretical, then the proof against that is also theoretical, causing a regress.
The objection is a classic tu quoque argument.
2
Identify the problem
The objection leads to circularity or infinite regress if the proof is theoretical and requires another proof.
3
Study the answer's distinction
The author distinguishes between knowledge on the assumption (that all is theoretical) and knowledge in actuality. The premises are known in actuality, so the assumption is false.
Look for the phrases 'لا في نفس الأمر' and 'بل هو معلوم لنا في نفس الأمر'.
4
See how the objection is rebutted
Because the premises are actually known, the proof does not depend on the false assumption. The objection only applies if the premises were unknown under that assumption, but they are known in reality.
The proof is valid for those who admit the premises as known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال إذ فرض الكل نظري فهذا الذي ذكرته من لزوم الدور أو التسلسل وكونهما مانعين من الاكتساب ومفضيين إلى أن لا يكون شيء من الإدراكات حاصلاً لنا أيضاً نظري على ذلك التقدير وحينئذ يمتنع إثباته لأن إثباته إنما يكون بنظري آخر فيلزم الدور أو التسلسل لما ذكرتم بعينه والحاصل أن دليلكم على بطلان كون الكل نظرياً ليس يتم بجميع مقدماته لأن كونه تاماً كذلك يستلزم المحال المذكور. ولأنا نقول ما ذكرناه في دليلنا من التصورات والتصديقات نظري غير معلوم على ذلك التقدير لا في نفس الأمر بل هو معلوم لنا في نفس الأمر فيبطل ذلك التقدير لاستلزامه خلاف الواقع أعني كون تلك القضايا معلومة في نفس الأمر. والحق أن هذا الدليل الذي ذكرناه حجة قائمة على من اعترف بالمعلومات أي اعترف بأن تلك القضايا المذكورة في الدليل معلومة في نفس الأمر وزعم أنها كسبية على ذلك التقدير ولا تكون معلومة عليه فكيف يجوز التمسك بها في إبطال ذلك التقدير.
It cannot be said: Since the assumption is that all is theoretical, then this which you mentioned of the necessity of circularity or infinite regress and that both prevent acquisition and lead to nothing of perceptions being obtained for us is also theoretical on that assumption, and then its proof is impossible because its proof can only be by another theoretical, so circularity or infinite regress follows as you mentioned. The upshot is that your proof of the invalidity of all being theoretical is not complete in all its premises because its being complete thus necessitates the mentioned impossibility. And we say: What we mentioned in our proof of conceptions and assents is theoretical and not known on that assumption, but in itself it is known to us in actuality. So that assumption is invalid because it necessitates contradiction of reality, namely that those propositions are known in actuality. And the truth is that this proof we mentioned is a valid argument against one who admits the known things, i.e., admits that those propositions mentioned in the proof are known in actuality, and claims that they are acquired on that assumption while they are not known thereby. So how can they be used to invalidate that assumption?
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that if all knowledge were theoretical (naẓarī), then the proof itself (which argues against that) would also be theoretical. Therefore, proving that proof would require another theoretical proof, leading to circularity or infinite regress. Thus the proof fails. The author answers by distinguishing between knowledge on the assumption and knowledge in reality. The premises used in the proof (like the impossibility of circularity and regress) are not known on the assumption that all is theoretical; rather, they are known in actuality (fī nafs al-amr). Therefore, the assumption that all is theoretical contradicts reality, since these premises are actually known. The proof is valid only for someone who admits that these premises are known in reality, even if they claim they are acquired. If the opponent denies their knowledge, the argument cannot be used. Thus the proof stands for those who accept the premises as known.

اعترف بالمعلومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof that not all knowledge is theoretical is self-defeating because it itself is a theoretical argument.
Correction: The proof's premises are known in actuality, not merely assumed within the hypothesis. Therefore, it is not self-defeating; the objection incorrectly assumes the premises are only known on the assumption.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that the proof itself is theoretical?
A) He admits the proof is theoretical and offers another proof.
B) He says the premises are known in reality, not on the assumption of full theory.
C) He argues that circularity is acceptable.
D) He denies the objection entirely.

Key Takeaway
The author's argument against all knowledge being theoretical is saved from circularity by the distinction that its premises are known in actuality, not merely within the assumption it refutes.

Reflect on whether your hesitation might be a contradiction of what you already know to be true. What step can you take this week to act on a truth you have been treating as theoretical?
?
The author argues that some propositions are known in actuality and cannot be denied without contradiction. Is there a truth about your own sin or need for Allah that you are treating as theoretical when it is actually undeniable? The First Weak School: All Knowledge is Necessary
Al-Ījī presents the first weak school and its two factions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first weak school on knowledge, its two factions, and why the dispute is merely verbal for one faction.

Core Idea
Some scholars said all knowledge is necessary, but the real issue is what 'necessary' means.

In this passage, al-Ījī introduces the first of four weak schools regarding the nature of knowledge. This school claims that all knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī), a view attributed to Imām al-Rāzī and some Ash'ari companions. The author then divides the proponents into two factions based on whether they accept dependence on reflection. Let's see what they argued and how the Ash'aris responded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the school
Read the opening lines to understand the claim that all knowledge is necessary. Who held this view?
2
Note the two factions
The passage divides the proponents into two groups. One accepts that some knowledge depends on reflection; the other denies it. Understand the difference.
The first faction leads to a merely verbal dispute.
3
Understand the verbal dispute
The first faction's concession allows Ash'aris to say the dispute is just about terminology. Learn how Ash'aris define 'acquired' (kasbī) to reconcile.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : المذاهب الضعيفة في هذه المسألة ( في بعض مذاهب ضعيفة في هذه المسألة (وهي أي تلك المذاهب بتأويل ) الطرائق ( أربع ) المذهب (الأول) أن الكل ضروري وبه قال ناس) من أصحابنا ( وهو قول الإمام الرازي) وذلك لعدم حصول شيء منه بقدرتنا إذ لا تأثير لها عندنا ( وهؤلاء فرقتان فرقة تسلم توقفه أي توقف بعض من الكل أو توقف العلم ( على النظر فيكون النزاع معهم في مجرد التسمية) بلا مخالفة معنوية لأنا نسلم أن ليس لقدرتنا تأثير في حصول شيء منه، لكنا تعني بالكسبي المقدور لنا ما تتعلق به القدرة الحادثة كسباً ويحصل عقيب النظر عادة لا ما تؤثر فيه قدرتنا حقيقة، قال الإمام الرازي في المحصل العلوم كلها ضرورية لأنها إما ضرورية ابتداء، أو لازمة عنها لزوماً ضرورياً فإنه إن بقي احتمال عدم اللزوم، ولو على أبعد الوجوه لم يكن علماً، وإذا كانت كذلك كانت بأسرها ضرورية وقال ناقده: أراد بالضروري معنى اليقيني دون البديهي المستغني عن النظر، وقد سمى كل اليقينيات ضرورياً موافقة لقول أبي الحسن الأشعري ( وفرقة تمنع ذلك أي توقفه على النظر ( وهؤلاء إن أرادوا بعدم توقفه ( أنه ( أي العلم ( لا يتوقف على النظر وجوبا)، إذ ليس بينهما ارتباط عقلي يوجب ذلك (بل) يتوقف عليه ( عادةً أو ( أرادوا به ( أن العلم) الحاصل بعد النظر ( غير واقع به ) أي بالنظر ( أو ) غير واقع ( بقدرتنا ) على وجه التأثير (بل بخلق الله تعالى ( فينا عقيب النظر بطريق جريان العادة ) فهو مذهب أهل الحق من الأشاعرة )، واحترز بذلك عما اختاره الإمام الرازي في المحصل من القول بوجوب العلم من النظر، لا على سبيل التوليد، وقد نسب هذا القول إلى القاضي وإمام الحرمين، فإنهما قالا : باستلزام النظر للعلم وجوباً من غير أن يكون النظر علة، أو مولداً ( وإن أرادوا بعدم توقفه عليه ) أنه لا يتوقف عليه أصلاً) أي لا تأثيراً ولا وجوباً ولا عادة ( فهو مكابرة ) ومخالفة لما يجده كل عاقل من أن علمه بالمسائل المختلف فيها يتوقف على نظره فيها .
The Fourth Objective: The weak schools in this matter. (There are some weak schools in this matter, and they are, by interpreting 'the ways,' four.) The first school: that all [knowledge] is necessary (ḍarūrī), and a group of our companions held this, and it is the saying of Imām al-Rāzī, due to the fact that nothing of it is acquired by our power, since there is no effect [of our power] according to us. And these are two groups: a group that concedes its dependence – i.e., the dependence of some of the whole or the dependence of knowledge – on reflection (naẓar), so the dispute with them is merely over terminology, without any essential disagreement, for we concede that our power has no effect in attaining any of it, but we mean by 'acquired' (kasbī) that which is within our capacity what the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) relates to as an acquisition (kashan) and occurs after reflection habitually, not that our power actually effects it. Imām al-Rāzī said in al-Muḥaṣṣal: 'All sciences are necessary, because they are either necessary initially or necessarily consequent upon that which is necessary; for if there remains any possibility of non-necessity, even in the most remote way, it would not be knowledge. And since they are thus, they are all necessary.' And his critic said: 'By necessary he meant [the sense of] certain (yaqīnī), not the self-evident (badīhī) that dispenses with reflection, and he called all certainties necessary, in agreement with the saying of Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī.' And [there is] a group that denies that – i.e., its dependence on reflection. And these, if they intend by its non-dependence that knowledge is not necessarily dependent on reflection, since there is no rational connection between them that necessitates that, but it is dependent on it habitually, or they intend that the knowledge that occurs after reflection is not brought about by reflection, nor by our power in the way of effect, but by the creation of Allah in us after reflection by way of the course of habit – then it is the doctrine of the people of truth, the Ash'aris. And he guarded himself by that against what Imām al-Rāzī chose in al-Muḥaṣṣal of the saying that knowledge is necessitated by reflection, not by way of generation (tawlīd). And this saying was attributed to al-Qāḍī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn, for they said: 'Reflection necessitates knowledge necessarily, without reflection being a cause or generator.' And if they intend by its non-dependence that it does not depend on it at all – i.e., neither effectively, nor necessarily, nor habitually – then it is stubbornness and opposition to what every rational person finds, that his knowledge of matters of dispute depends on his reflection upon them.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four weak schools (madhaahib da'eefa) regarding the epistemological status of knowledge: whether it is necessary (ḍarūrī) or acquired (kasbī). The author introduces the Fourth Objective of the Third Observation, which specifically examines weak opinions on this issue. The passage begins by stating that there are four such schools, interpreting 'ways' (ṭarā'iq) to make the number feminine. The first school holds that all knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī), a position attributed to Imam al-Razi and some Ash'ari companions, based on the premise that human power (qudra) has no real effect in producing knowledge. The author then divides proponents of this school into two factions. The first faction concedes that some knowledge depends on reflection (naẓar), making the dispute merely verbal: the Ash'aris concede that our power does not truly effect knowledge, but they define 'acquired' (kasbī) as that which is conventionally attributed to human capacity through acquisition (kash) and occurs habitually after reflection, not that human power causes it. The second faction denies any dependence on reflection. For them, three possible interpretations are considered: (1) denial of necessary (wujūbī) dependence, accepting only habitual dependence – this aligns with the Ash'ari position that knowledge is created by Allah after reflection by habit; (2) denial of any effective or causal role for reflection or human power, affirming that Allah creates knowledge after reflection as a matter of habit – this is the true Ash'ari doctrine; (3) absolute denial of any dependence whatsoever – this is dismissed as stubbornness (mukābara) and contrary to common experience. The passage also notes that Imam al-Razi's view in al-Muḥaṣṣal equated 'necessary' with 'certain' (yaqīnī), not with 'self-evident' (badīhī), following Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. This clarification resolves the apparent conflict. The author further distinguishes the Ash'ari position from the generative (tawlīd) theory, which holds that reflection necessarily produces knowledge as a direct effect, and from the views of al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn who held necessitation without causation or generation. The core argument is that knowledge is either initially necessary or necessarily follows from premises, but the Ash'aris maintain that the 'necessity' of acquired knowledge is habitual, not intrinsic or causal.

كسبي
مذهب
فرقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'necessary' (ḍarūrī) here means self-evident without need for reflection.
Correction: In this context, 'necessary' can also mean certain (yaqīnī), as explained later. The first faction accepts dependence on reflection, so they use 'necessary' in a broader sense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the dispute with the first faction merely verbal?
A) Because they deny any dependence on reflection
B) Because both sides agree that human power has no effect in producing knowledge
C) Because they reject the Ash'ari definition of kasb
D) Because they hold that all knowledge is self-evident

Key Takeaway
The first weak school claims all knowledge is necessary, but its first faction accepts dependence on reflection, reducing the disagreement to a verbal one about the definition of 'acquired'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study, do you ever tell yourself 'knowledge is only from God' to avoid the effort of deep reflection? What does this lesson’s mention that even the first school concedes dependence on reflection (naẓar) demand from you? Commit to one practical step to engage your mind actively in your next study session.
?
The passage shows that the dispute over 'necessary' vs 'acquired' knowledge is merely terminological for one group. Yet many debates today become heated over labels. Examine your own heart: do you ever cling to a label (like 'Salafi' or 'Ash'ari') to feel certain rather than seeking truth? What would it look like for you to prioritize substance over terminology?
?
If the Ash'aris define 'acquired' knowledge as occurring 'habitually after reflection, not that our power actually effects it,' how does that redefine your responsibility in seeking knowledge? You are not the ultimate cause, but you are commanded to reflect. What specific habit will you change to ensure you habitually engage in reflection (naẓar) after learning?

The Second Faction and the Ash'ari Position
Al-Ījī discusses the second faction and the Ash'ari response, including the meanings of 'dependence' and the distinction from tawlīd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second group's denial of dependence on reflection and the nuanced Ash'ari position that knowledge is created by Allah habitually after reflection.

Core Idea
Knowledge depends on reflection habitually, not necessarily.

The second faction of the first weak school denies that knowledge depends on reflection at all. The author examines three possible interpretations of this denial. The correct Ash'ari position is that knowledge is created by Allah after reflection by way of habit, not by necessitation or generation. This lesson also clarifies Imām al-Rāzī's usage of 'necessary' as certain (yaqīnī), and distinguishes the Ash'ari view from the theory of tawlīd (generation). Let's delve into these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second faction's denial
The second group denies that knowledge depends on reflection. But what do they mean by 'dependence'? The author lists three possibilities: necessary, habitual, or none.
2
Identify the correct Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris hold that knowledge is not necessarily dependent on reflection, but it is habitually dependent. Allah creates knowledge after reflection as a matter of habit, not by necessity.
This is the 'second type' of denial.
3
Examine Imām al-Rāzī's view
Imām al-Rāzī said all knowledge is necessary, but his critic clarified he meant 'certain' (yaqīnī), not self-evident. He was following Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī's usage.
4
Distinguish from tawlīd and other views
The Ash'ari position is contrasted with the view that reflection necessarily produces knowledge (attributed to al-Qāḍī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn), which involves necessitation without generation.

Misconception & Correction

A) Stubbornness

?

The Second Weak School: Conceptualization is Not Acquired
Al-Jurjani states the second school's denial of acquired conceptualization and contrasts it with affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of the second weak school that all conceptualization is necessary and cannot be acquired through theoretical reflection, unlike affirmation.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge comes from reflection—some concepts are inborn and necessary.

In this lesson, we examine a second weak opinion (al-madhhab al-thānī) that denies any conceptual knowledge can be gained through theoretical reflection (naẓar). Instead, all conceptualizations are necessary (ḍarūrī) and occur without acquisition. This view sharply distinguishes between conceptualization (taṣawwur) and affirmation (taṣdīq), where the latter can be either necessary or acquired. Imam al-Razi adopted this school and gave two reasons, which we will explore in subsequent lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the author's style. Then read the English translation carefully.
Notice the contrast between tasawwur and tasdiq.
2
Identify the school's claim
Find where the author states that conceptualization cannot be acquired and is all necessary.
The phrase 'بل كل ما يحصل منه كان ضروريا' is key.
3
Understand the contrast with affirmation
Notice that affirmation (tasdiq) is said to divide into necessary and acquired, unlike conceptualization.
This highlights the uniqueness of the school's position.
4
Prepare for the reasons
The passage ends by mentioning that al-Razi gave two reasons. Reflect on what might follow.
These reasons are taken up in the next lesson.

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع: المذاهب الضعيفة في هذه المسألة
١١٠
(المذهب الثاني : في هذه المسألة ( إن التصور لا يكتسب ) بالنظر بل كل ما يحصل منه كان ضروريا حاصلا بلا اكتساب ،ونظر بخلاف التصديق فإنه ينقسم إلى ضروري ومكتسب ( وبه قال الإمام الرازي)، واختاره في كتبه ( لوجهين أحدهما أن المطلوب) التصوري (إما مشعور به مطلقاً ( فلا يطلب ( لحصوله بناء على أن تحصيل الحاصل محال بالضرورة ) أو (لا يكون مشعوراً به أصلاً ، فلا يطلب أيضاً لأن المغفول عنه ) بالكلية، وهو المسمى بالمجهول المطلق ( لا يمكن توجه النفس) بالطلب (نحوه) بالضرورة أيضاً ( وأجيب) عن هذا الوجه ( بأن الحصر) أي حصر المطلوب التصوري فيما هو مشعور به من جميع الوجوه، أو غير مشعور به أصلاً ( ممنوع لجواز أن يكون معلوماً ومشعوراً به من وجه دون وجه آخر، ولم يتبين بما ذكره أن هذا القسم يمتنع طلبه ( فعاد) الإمام ( وقال : الوجه المعلوم معلوم مطلقاً، والوجه المجهول مجهول مطلقاً فلا يمكن طلب شيء منهما ) لما مر من امتناع تحصيل الحاصل وامتناع توجه النفس نحو المغفول عنه بالكلية ( والجواب ) هذا الوجه بعد استيفاء الأقسام الثلاثة أن يقال : ( لا نسلم أن الوجه المجهول مجهول مطلقاً أي جميع الوجوه ) فإن المجهول مطلقاً ما لم يتصور ذاته ) بكنهه
قوله: (بخلاف التصديق إلخ لأن ما يتعلق به التصديق أعني النسبة أمر واحد معلوم
تصوراً مجهول تصديقاً ، فلا تجري الشبهة المذكورة فيه، وكذا الثانية وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( إن المطلوب التصوري) ملخصه أنه لو كان المطلوب التصوري مكتسباً لما امتنع طلبه والتالي ،باطل أما الملازمة فظاهرة وأما بطلان التالي فلان المطلوب التصوري، إما مشعور به أو غير مشعور به وكل منهما يمتنع ،طلبه فالمطلوب التصوري يمتنع طلبه، وبما حررنا يندفع ما قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يحصل شيء فيه بطريق أن يترتب أشياء يري أنه هل يؤدي إلى شيء أم لا ؟ فيتفق أن يؤدي إلى تصور مخصوص .
قوله : (بل كل ما يحصل منه إلخ) قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يحصل شيء منه ؟ بطريق أن يترتب أشياء يرى أنه هل يؤدي إلى شيء أم لا، فيتفق أن يؤدي إلى تصور مخصوص، نعم يمكن الإلزام لمن يقول بالطلب .
قوله : ( إن المطلوب التصوري إما مشعور به إلخ) قيل عليه : إنه منقوض باكتساب التصديق مع جريان الدليل فيه أجيب بأن ما يتعلق به التصديق كالقضية، أو النسبة معلوم بحسب التصور، فلا يمتنع التوجه إليه مجهول بحسب التصديق، فلا يمتنع طلب حصوله وهذا بخلاف التصور، فإن ما يكون مجهولاً بحسب التصور يكون مجهولاً مطلقاً إذ لا علم قبل التصور، وحاصله أن متعلق التصديق يجوز أن يتعلق به قبل التصديق علم هو التصور بخلاف
متعلق التصور .
The third observation - the fourth aim: The weak schools in this issue. 110. (The second school: In this issue, that conceptualization (taṣawwur) cannot be acquired by reflection, but rather everything that is obtained from it is necessary, obtained without acquisition and reflection, unlike affirmation (taṣdīq), for it is divided into necessary and acquired. And Imam al-Razi held this, and he chose it in his books, for two reasons. One of them: the sought conceptual thing is either completely perceived (mashʿūr bihi muṭlaqan), in which case it is not sought because its attainment is impossible (since acquiring what is already present is impossible by necessity); or it is not perceived at all, in which case it is also not sought because what is completely neglected (al-maghfūl ʿanhu bi-l-kulliyyah), which is called the absolute unknown (al-majhūl al-muṭlaq), cannot be directed by the soul towards it by necessity. And it was answered regarding this reason: that the exclusive division—i.e., confining the sought conceptual thing to what is perceived in all respects or not perceived at all—is denied, because it is possible for it to be known and perceived in one respect but not in another, and he did not show that seeking this division is impossible. So the Imam returned and said: The known aspect is known absolutely, and the unknown aspect is unknown absolutely, so seeking either of them is impossible, due to the aforementioned impossibility of acquiring what is present and the impossibility of the soul's direction towards what is completely neglected. And the answer to this reason after completing the three divisions is to say: We do not accept that the unknown aspect is unknown absolutely, i.e., in all respects, for the absolutely unknown is that whose essence has not been conceptualized in its reality. His statement: (unlike affirmation, etc.) because what affirmation relates to, namely the proposition or relation, is known conceptually but unknown in affirmation, so the aforementioned doubt does not apply to it; and similarly the second reason, and it is clear. His statement: (Indeed, the sought conceptual thing) — its summary: if the sought conceptual thing were acquired, its seeking would not be impossible; but the consequent is false. As for the implication, it is clear; as for the falsity of the consequent: the sought conceptual thing is either perceived or not perceived, and both are impossible to seek, so the sought conceptual thing is impossible to seek. And by what we have written, the objection is refuted: why is it not possible for something to be obtained from it by arranging things to see whether it leads to something or not, and it happens to lead to a specific conceptualization? His statement: (but everything that is obtained from it, etc.) It was said: Why is it not possible for something to be obtained from it by arranging things to see whether it leads to something or not, and it happens to lead to a specific conceptualization? Yes, it is possible to compel one who asserts the possibility of seeking. His statement: (Indeed, the sought conceptual thing is either perceived, etc.) — an objection was raised against it: that it is invalidated by the acquisition of affirmation, since the same argument applies to it. It was answered: what affirmation relates to, like the proposition or relation, is known in terms of conceptualization, so directing the soul towards it is not impossible, but it is unknown in terms of affirmation, so seeking its attainment is not impossible. This is unlike conceptualization, for what is unknown in terms of conceptualization is absolutely unknown, since there is no knowledge prior to conceptualization. The summary: that the object of affirmation can be preceded by the knowledge of conceptualization, unlike the object of conceptualization.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the second weak school (al-madhhab al-thānī) regarding the issue of whether conceptualization (taṣawwur) can be acquired through theoretical reflection (naẓar). The school denies that conceptualization can be acquired; rather, all conceptualizations are necessary (ḍarūrī), occurring without acquisition. This is unlike affirmation (taṣdīq), which can be either necessary or acquired. Imam al-Razi adopted this position with two reasons. 

First reason: The object of conceptual seeking (al-maṭlūb al-taṣawwurī) is either completely perceived (mashʿūr bihi muṭlaqan) or not perceived at all. If completely perceived, seeking it is impossible because one cannot acquire what is already present (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). If not perceived at all, it is the absolute unknown (al-majhūl al-muṭlaq), and the soul cannot direct itself toward what is completely neglected. Therefore, conceptualization cannot be sought, so it cannot be acquired. 

The refutation of this first reason: The dichotomy (ḥaṣr) is denied (mamnūʿ) because it is possible for the sought object to be known and perceived in one respect but not in another. The argument does not prove that seeking this partially unknown object is impossible. 

Imam al-Razi then refined his argument: The known aspect is known absolutely, and the unknown aspect is unknown absolutely, so seeking either of them is impossible. 

The refutation of this second reason: We do not accept that the unknown aspect is unknown absolutely, because the absolutely unknown is only that whose essence has not been conceptualized in its reality (bi-kunhihi). In cases of partial knowledge, the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown; the soul can direct itself toward the known aspect while seeking the unknown aspect. 

The passage also includes clarifications: Unlike conceptualization, affirmation (taṣdīq) can be acquired because its object (the proposition or relation) is already known through conceptualization; therefore, the mind can direct itself to the known subject to seek the unknown truth value. The objection that the same reasoning applies to affirmation is thus refuted. The text also addresses a possible objection: one might ask why conceptualization cannot occur accidentally through arranging premises to see what results; the answer is that such accidental occurrence is possible, but it does not constitute intentional seeking. The passage concludes that the object of affirmation can be preceded by conceptual knowledge, unlike the object of conceptualization itself.

Key Terms
التصور
التصديق
الاكتساب
ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all knowledge, including conceptualization, can be acquired through reasoning.
Correction: This school argues that basic concepts like the meaning of 'existence' are not acquired; they are innate and necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the claim of the second weak school regarding conceptualization (tasawwur)?
A) Conceptualization can be acquired through reflection like affirmation.
B) Conceptualization is all necessary and cannot be acquired.
C) Conceptualization is all acquired through reflection.
D) Conceptualization and affirmation are both only necessary.

Key Takeaway
The second weak school asserts that all conceptualizations are necessary, unlike affirmations which can be either necessary or acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that conceptualization (tasawwur) is not acquired but necessary—it comes to you without effort. Have you ever taken for granted the simple concepts you have, like your understanding of existence or unity? Consider that even your ability to think about these matters is a gift from Allah, not a product of your own intellect. How will you thank Him for the very ability to know?
?
This lesson contrasts conceptualization with affirmation (tasdiq), which can be acquired through reflection. Think about your own beliefs about Allah, the Prophet, or the afterlife. Did they come through diligent reflection, or have you inherited them without thought? Pause and examine: which of your affirmations are truly your own through reflective effort, and which are passively held? What will you do to turn a passive belief into an acquired, firm conviction?
?
The second school maintains that all conceptualizations are necessary and not acquired. Does this challenge the modern emphasis on self-made knowledge and individual effort? In a world that prizes autonomy, how does it feel to be told that your most foundational concepts are not your own doing? Will you accept this dependency with humility, or resist it? Commit to a small practice that reminds you of this dependency, such as beginning each study session with a prayer for understanding.

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Al-Razi's First Reason and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani gives al-Razi's first reason and the refutation challenging the exclusive division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's first argument against acquiring conceptualization based on the dichotomy of the sought object, and its refutation that this dichotomy is incomplete.

Core Idea
You cannot seek what you already know, and you cannot seek what you are completely ignorant of—but something in between is possible.

Al-Razi provided two reasons for the second weak school. The first reason argues that the object of conceptual seeking (al-maṭlūb al-taṣawwurī) must be either completely perceived or not perceived at all; both cases make acquisition impossible. The refutation, however, denies this exclusive division, because an object can be known in one respect and unknown in another, making seeking possible. This lesson unpacks this back-and-forth.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's first reason
Read the Arabic text for the first reason and its translation. Note the two cases: completely perceived or not at all.
This is a classic dilemma.
2
Understand the refutation's point
The refutation says the dichotomy (حصـر) is forbidden (ممنوع) because there is a middle case: known from one angle but unknown from another.
This is a key logical move.
3
See how the refutation undermines the argument
If the sought object can be partially known, then the soul can direct itself toward the known aspect while seeking the unknown aspect.
This shows the argument is not conclusive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the only two options are full knowledge or total ignorance, making seeking impossible.
Correction: The refutation shows that partial knowledge—knowing something from one aspect but not another—is possible, making seeking feasible.

Checkpoint
Why did the refutation reject al-Razi's first argument?
A) Because the argument assumed the impossibility of acquiring what is already present.
B) Because the argument incorrectly claimed that the soul cannot direct itself toward the absolute unknown.
C) Because the argument's exclusive division (fully known vs. fully unknown) is incomplete; there is a middle state of partial knowledge.
D) Because the argument confused conceptualization with affirmation.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first reason fails because it ignores the possibility of partial knowledge, which allows for seeking what is partly known and partly unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a new concept in aqeedah, do you assume you either fully understand it or are completely ignorant? How does al-Razi's mistake challenge your approach to learning?
?
What is one concept of faith that you partially understand but have not pursued further? What holds you back from seeking full comprehension with the awareness that partial knowledge is a valid starting point?
?
Al-Razi's refutation shows that you can seek what is partly known. In your spiritual life, what is something you are content with only partially knowing? Is that contentment a result of humility or negligence?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Al-Razi's Refined Reason and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents al-Razi's refined reason and the final refutation clarifying absolute unknowability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's refined argument distinguishing between the known and unknown aspects, and the refutation that the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown when there is partial knowledge.

Core Idea
Even if an object has two aspects, the unknown side is still absolutely unknown—unless partial knowledge makes it not absolutely unknown.

After the first refutation, al-Razi refined his argument: in a partially known object, the known aspect is known absolutely, and the unknown aspect is unknown absolutely, so seeking either is impossible. The response argues that the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown unless one has no concept of it at all. Since in partial knowledge one has a concept of the whole, the unknown aspect is not truly absolute. This lesson unpacks this precise exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's refined reason
Read the Arabic where al-Razi says the known aspect is known absolutely and the unknown is unknown absolutely.
This attempts to overcome the partial knowledge objection.
2
Understand the refutation's point
The refutation denies that the unknown aspect is unknown absolutely; absolute unknowability means no conceptualization of the essence at all.
Partial knowledge implies some conceptualization, so the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown.
3
See the distinction between partial and complete ignorance
Even if one does not know the specific essence, one has a generic concept; thus, the soul can still seek the specific.
This completes the refutation of al-Razi's position.

قوله: (بخلاف التصديق إلخ لأن ما يتعلق به التصديق أعني النسبة أمر واحد معلوم
تصوراً مجهول تصديقاً ، فلا تجري الشبهة المذكورة فيه، وكذا الثانية وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( إن المطلوب التصوري) ملخصه أنه لو كان المطلوب التصوري مكتسباً لما امتنع طلبه والتالي ،باطل أما الملازمة فظاهرة وأما بطلان التالي فلان المطلوب التصوري، إما مشعور به أو غير مشعور به وكل منهما يمتنع ،طلبه فالمطلوب التصوري يمتنع طلبه، وبما حررنا يندفع ما قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يحصل شيء فيه بطريق أن يترتب أشياء يري أنه هل يؤدي إلى شيء أم لا ؟ فيتفق أن يؤدي إلى تصور مخصوص .
قوله : (بل كل ما يحصل منه إلخ) قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يحصل شيء منه ؟ بطريق أن يترتب أشياء يرى أنه هل يؤدي إلى شيء أم لا، فيتفق أن يؤدي إلى تصور مخصوص، نعم يمكن الإلزام لمن يقول بالطلب .
قوله : ( إن المطلوب التصوري إما مشعور به إلخ) قيل عليه : إنه منقوض باكتساب التصديق مع جريان الدليل فيه أجيب بأن ما يتعلق به التصديق كالقضية، أو النسبة معلوم بحسب التصور، فلا يمتنع التوجه إليه مجهول بحسب التصديق، فلا يمتنع طلب حصوله وهذا بخلاف التصور، فإن ما يكون مجهولاً بحسب التصور يكون مجهولاً مطلقاً إذ لا علم قبل التصور، وحاصله أن متعلق التصديق يجوز أن يتعلق به قبل التصديق علم هو التصور بخلاف
متعلق التصور .
The third observation - the fourth aim: The weak schools in this issue. 110. (The second school: In this issue, that conceptualization (taṣawwur) cannot be acquired by reflection, but rather everything that is obtained from it is necessary, obtained without acquisition and reflection, unlike affirmation (taṣdīq), for it is divided into necessary and acquired. And Imam al-Razi held this, and he chose it in his books, for two reasons. One of them: the sought conceptual thing is either completely perceived (mashʿūr bihi muṭlaqan), in which case it is not sought because its attainment is impossible (since acquiring what is already present is impossible by necessity); or it is not perceived at all, in which case it is also not sought because what is completely neglected (al-maghfūl ʿanhu bi-l-kulliyyah), which is called the absolute unknown (al-majhūl al-muṭlaq), cannot be directed by the soul towards it by necessity. And it was answered regarding this reason: that the exclusive division—i.e., confining the sought conceptual thing to what is perceived in all respects or not perceived at all—is denied, because it is possible for it to be known and perceived in one respect but not in another, and he did not show that seeking this division is impossible. So the Imam returned and said: The known aspect is known absolutely, and the unknown aspect is unknown absolutely, so seeking either of them is impossible, due to the aforementioned impossibility of acquiring what is present and the impossibility of the soul's direction towards what is completely neglected. And the answer to this reason after completing the three divisions is to say: We do not accept that the unknown aspect is unknown absolutely, i.e., in all respects, for the absolutely unknown is that whose essence has not been conceptualized in its reality. His statement: (unlike affirmation, etc.) because what affirmation relates to, namely the proposition or relation, is known conceptually but unknown in affirmation, so the aforementioned doubt does not apply to it; and similarly the second reason, and it is clear. His statement: (Indeed, the sought conceptual thing) — its summary: if the sought conceptual thing were acquired, its seeking would not be impossible; but the consequent is false. As for the implication, it is clear; as for the falsity of the consequent: the sought conceptual thing is either perceived or not perceived, and both are impossible to seek, so the sought conceptual thing is impossible to seek. And by what we have written, the objection is refuted: why is it not possible for something to be obtained from it by arranging things to see whether it leads to something or not, and it happens to lead to a specific conceptualization? His statement: (but everything that is obtained from it, etc.) It was said: Why is it not possible for something to be obtained from it by arranging things to see whether it leads to something or not, and it happens to lead to a specific conceptualization? Yes, it is possible to compel one who asserts the possibility of seeking. His statement: (Indeed, the sought conceptual thing is either perceived, etc.) — an objection was raised against it: that it is invalidated by the acquisition of affirmation, since the same argument applies to it. It was answered: what affirmation relates to, like the proposition or relation, is known in terms of conceptualization, so directing the soul towards it is not impossible, but it is unknown in terms of affirmation, so seeking its attainment is not impossible. This is unlike conceptualization, for what is unknown in terms of conceptualization is absolutely unknown, since there is no knowledge prior to conceptualization. The summary: that the object of affirmation can be preceded by the knowledge of conceptualization, unlike the object of conceptualization.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the second weak school (al-madhhab al-thānī) regarding the issue of whether conceptualization (taṣawwur) can be acquired through theoretical reflection (naẓar). The school denies that conceptualization can be acquired; rather, all conceptualizations are necessary (ḍarūrī), occurring without acquisition. This is unlike affirmation (taṣdīq), which can be either necessary or acquired. Imam al-Razi adopted this position with two reasons. 

First reason: The object of conceptual seeking (al-maṭlūb al-taṣawwurī) is either completely perceived (mashʿūr bihi muṭlaqan) or not perceived at all. If completely perceived, seeking it is impossible because one cannot acquire what is already present (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). If not perceived at all, it is the absolute unknown (al-majhūl al-muṭlaq), and the soul cannot direct itself toward what is completely neglected. Therefore, conceptualization cannot be sought, so it cannot be acquired. 

The refutation of this first reason: The dichotomy (ḥaṣr) is denied (mamnūʿ) because it is possible for the sought object to be known and perceived in one respect but not in another. The argument does not prove that seeking this partially unknown object is impossible. 

Imam al-Razi then refined his argument: The known aspect is known absolutely, and the unknown aspect is unknown absolutely, so seeking either of them is impossible. 

The refutation of this second reason: We do not accept that the unknown aspect is unknown absolutely, because the absolutely unknown is only that whose essence has not been conceptualized in its reality (bi-kunhihi). In cases of partial knowledge, the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown; the soul can direct itself toward the known aspect while seeking the unknown aspect. 

The passage also includes clarifications: Unlike conceptualization, affirmation (taṣdīq) can be acquired because its object (the proposition or relation) is already known through conceptualization; therefore, the mind can direct itself to the known subject to seek the unknown truth value. The objection that the same reasoning applies to affirmation is thus refuted. The text also addresses a possible objection: one might ask why conceptualization cannot occur accidentally through arranging premises to see what results; the answer is that such accidental occurrence is possible, but it does not constitute intentional seeking. The passage concludes that the object of affirmation can be preceded by conceptual knowledge, unlike the object of conceptualization itself.

Key Terms
المعلوم
المجهول
المجهول المطلق
الكنه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is partially unknown, that part is absolutely unknown.
Correction: The refutation clarifies that absolute unknowability requires total absence of any conceptualization, not just a lack of specific knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the key point in the refutation of al-Razi's refined argument?
A) That the known aspect is not absolutely known.
B) That the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown because partial conceptualization exists.
C) That the soul cannot direct itself toward anything unknown.
D) That the division into three aspects is incorrect.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's refined reason is refuted by showing that partial knowledge prevents the unknown aspect from being absolutely unknown, thus allowing for directed seeking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a concept in Islam that feels completely foreign to you, do you immediately dismiss it as 'absolutely unknown' and stop trying? How might the refutation of al-Razi's argument challenge you to instead recognize that you already have some concept (even a confused one) of that reality—and that therefore, you are accountable to seek further understanding? What specific step will you take today to pursue knowledge of something you have previously treated as 'absolutely unknown'?
?
Consider an aspect of your belief that you think you already know 'absolutely'—like the meaning of Tawheed or the Names of Allah. Based on this lesson, is your 'absolutely known' actually a partial knowledge that leaves room for deeper ignorance? How does that humble your confidence and drive you to learn more?
?
Al-Razi's refined reason says seeking the unknown aspect is impossible. But the refutation says it is possible because the unknown is not absolutely unknown. Have you ever used the excuse that something is 'too hidden' to avoid seeking knowledge about a hard topic—like the nature of the soul, or the reality of the afterlife? How does this lesson expose that excuse as a fallacy, and what are you going to do about it?

The Two Premises Cannot Both Be True
Proof of inconsistency between two premises via logical conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the two categorical premises in the conceptual request objection are logically inconsistent and how contrapositive and simple conversion reveal their conflict.

Core Idea
The two premises can't both be true—each logically implies the opposite of the other.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks an objection about requesting conceptual knowledge and shows it rests on two premises that cannot both be true. He reduces the objection to a logical syllogism and then uses the tools of contrapositive and conversion to expose the inconsistency. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: first an acknowledgment, then a presentation of the objection as a divided syllogism, followed by a proof of inconsistency.
2
Identify the two premises
Locate the two categorical statements: 'every perceived thing is impossible to request' and 'every non-perceived thing is impossible to request'. Understand how they form the disjunctive syllogism.
3
Follow the conversion argument
The commentator takes the contrapositive of each premise, then applies simple conversion. Trace each step: from 'كل مشعور به يمتنع طلبه' to 'بعض غير المشعور به لا يمتنع طلبه', and similarly for the second premise.
Use the commentary to help if needed.
4
Understand the contradiction
Notice that the converted statements are each more specific than the contradictory of the other premise. This means the premises cannot both be true simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعترف عنه هناك بأن الشيء المعلوم من وجه والمجهول من وجه، يغاير الوجهين، فألزم ها هنا بأن المطلوب التصوري ليس أحد الوجهين بل الشيء الذي له ذانك الوجهان، ويشهد لما ذكرناه أن هذا المثبت قال في نقل تنزيل الأفكار : المطلوب المجهول هو حقيقة الماهية المعلومة ببعض عوارضها فاكتفى بالوجهين، وقال بعض المتأخرين) هو المولى شرف الدين المراغي إن هذه الشبهة إذا ردت إلى قوانين الاستدلال كانت قياساً مقسماً من منفصلة ذات جزءين، وهو من حمليتين هكذا المطلوب التصوري، إما مشعور به وإما غير مشعور به وكل مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وكل غير مشعور به يمتنع طلبه فالمطلوب التصوري يمتنع طلبه، ولا شك أن هذا الانتاج إنما يصح إذا صدقت الحمليتان لكن قولنا : ( كل مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وكل غير مشعور به يمتنع طلبه لا يجتمعان على الصدق، إذ العكس المستوي لعكس نقيض كل منهما ( ينافي الآخر) فإن الأول ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه فهو غير مشعور به وهذا العكس ينعكس بالمستوي إلى قولنا بعض غير المشعور به لا يمتنع طلبه وهذا أخص من نقيض الثاني فينافيه وكذا الثاني ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه فهو مشعور به وينعكس هذا العكس بالمستوي إلى قولنا بعض المشعور به لا يمتنع طلبه، وهو أخص من نقيض الأول فينافيه أيضاً، وإذا كان لازم كل منهما منافياً للآخر لم يتصور اجتماعهما صدقاً
He acknowledged there that the thing known in one respect and unknown in another is different from the two respects, so he concluded here that the requested conceptual thing is not one of the two respects but rather the thing that has those two respects. And what supports what we have said is that this proponent said in the transmission of 'Tanzīl al-Afkār': the requested unknown is the reality of the essence known by some of its accidents, so he was satisfied with the two respects. And some later scholars (he is al-Mawlā Sharaf al-Dīn al-Marāghī) said that this objection, when reduced to the rules of inference, becomes a divided syllogism composed of a disjunctive with two parts, and it is from two categoricals as follows: the requested conceptual thing is either perceived or not perceived, and every perceived thing is impossible to request, and every non-perceived thing is impossible to request, so the requested conceptual thing is impossible to request. There is no doubt that this conclusion is only valid if the two categoricals are true. But our statement: 'every perceived thing is impossible to request, and every non-perceived thing is impossible to request' cannot both be true together, because the simple conversion of the contrapositive of each of them conflicts with the other. For the first converts by contrapositive to our statement: 'everything that is not impossible to request is non-perceived', and this converse converts by simple conversion to our statement: 'some non-perceived things are not impossible to request', and this is more specific than the contradictory of the second, so it conflicts with it. Likewise, the second converts by contrapositive to our statement: 'everything that is not impossible to request is perceived', and this converse converts by simple conversion to our statement: 'some perceived things are not impossible to request', and it is more specific than the contradictory of the first, so it also conflicts with it. And if the necessary consequent of each of them conflicts with the other, it is inconceivable that they are both true together.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the two categorical premises in the objection regarding the request for conceptual knowledge cannot both be true. The commentator first frames the objection as a divided syllogism: the requested concept is either perceived or not perceived, and both are claimed impossible to request, so requesting it is impossible. He then shows that the two premises 'every perceived thing is impossible to request' and 'every non-perceived thing is impossible to request' are inconsistent. He uses logical conversions: he takes the contrapositive of each premise, then applies simple conversion to the result. From the first premise's contrapositive 'everything not impossible to request is non-perceived', simple conversion yields 'some non-perceived things are not impossible to request', which contradicts the second premise's negation (the contradictory of 'every non-perceived thing is impossible to request' is 'some non-perceived things are not impossible to request'). Similarly, from the second premise's contrapositive, simple conversion yields 'some perceived things are not impossible to request', contradicting the negation of the first premise. Since each premise leads to a consequence that conflicts with the other, they cannot both be true together. This demonstrates the logical flaw in the objection.

Key Terms
مطلوب تصوري
قياس مقسم
عكس نقيض
عكس مستوي
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the two premises can both be true simply because they cover the two disjuncts (perceived and non-perceived) and each seems plausible on its own.
Correction: The passage shows that the premises are not independent; each leads, via contrapositive and simple conversion, to a statement that contradicts the other premise. Therefore, they cannot both be true.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot the two premises (كل مشعور به يمتنع طلبه and كل غير مشعور به يمتنع طلبه) both be true?
A) Because they are contradictory in themselves.
B) Because the contrapositive of each, when simply converted, yields a statement that contradicts the other premise's contradictory.
C) Because the divided syllogism is invalid.
D) Because the objector admitted the premises are false.

Key Takeaway
The two premises cannot both be true because each, when transformed, yields a statement that contradicts the other's contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage reveals that two seemingly plausible claims can logically contradict each other. In your own life, have you held two beliefs about your faith or knowledge that, upon honest examination, cannot both be true? What would it cost you to resolve that contradiction?
?
The argument refutes the idea that seeking conceptual knowledge is impossible. When you encounter a difficult question about your ’aqeedah, do you trust that Allah has made the truth accessible, or do you retreat into comfortable vagueness? What is one area of Islamic knowledge you have avoided seeking because it felt impossible to grasp, and how can the logical clarity in this passage give you courage?
?
The objector assumed that the premises exhaust all possibilities and must be jointly true, but the conversion shows they are inconsistent. Are there assumptions in your daily life that you treat as jointly true without testing their logical coherence? Pause and identify one such assumption—what would it mean for your actions if it were false?

The First Answer: Denying the Conversion Rule
The first of two answers: denial of the conversion rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first answer to the inconsistency proof: that the conversion of a universal affirmative by contrapositive to a universal affirmative has no proof, and thus the proof fails.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative does not necessarily convert to itself by contrapositive — a crucial logical insight.

The inconsistency proof relied on a conversion rule from the ancients. But is that rule proven? The commentator presents a decisive challenge: no proof has been established for it. Let's examine this first answer and see how it undermines the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical rule at issue
The proof assumed that a universal affirmative premise converts by contrapositive to a universal affirmative. Recognize that this is the key step the answer targets.
This conversion rule is attributed to the method of the ancients.
2
Read the answer carefully
Read the Arabic and translation: the answer is a simple denial of that conversion rule. The commentator states that no proof has been established for it.
3
Understand the force of the denial
By denying the rule, the entire proof collapses because it depends on that conversion. This shows that not all logical rules are accepted without proof.
In logic, unproven rules cannot be assumed.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the commentator would present this as a valid answer. It highlights the importance of verifying foundational rules before building arguments.
This is a common method in kalam: challenging premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأجيب بمنع انعكاس الموجبة الكلية كنفسها بعكس النقيض تارة فإن انعكاس الموجبة الكلية بعكس النقيض إلى موجبة كلية كما هو طريقة القدماء، مما لم يقم عليه برهان (و) أجيب ( بتقييد الموضوع فيهما بالتصور أخرى ) أي بين شارح المطالع عدم اجتماعهما في الصدق بأن ضم عكس نقيض إحديهما إلى عين الأخرى، لينتج المحال هكذا كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه فهو غير مشعور به وكل غير مشعور به يمتنع ينتج كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه يمتنع طلبه . قوله: (أخص من نقيض (الثاني) لأن نقيضه سالبة جزئية أعني ليس كل ما هو غير مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وهذه موجبة جزئية معدولة تقتضي وجود الموضوع. قوله : ( إلى موجبة (كلية معدولة وأما انعكاسها إلى موجبة كلية سالبة الطرفين كما أثبته شارح المطالع فلا يفيد هاهنا لأن الموجبة السالبة الطرفين في حكم السالبة البسيطة في عدم اقتضاء وجود الموضوع، فحينئذ يجوز أن يقال : يصدق عکس نقيض كل منهما ولازمه بانتفاء الموضوع، فلا ينافي الأصل المقتضي لوجود الموضوع، وكذا ضم عكس نقيض كل منهما مع عين الأخرى لا ينتج لانتفاء إيجاب الصغرى، وبما ذكرنا تبين أن الجواب المذكور تام
So it was answered, once, by denying the conversion of the universal affirmative as itself by contrapositive: for the conversion of the universal affirmative by contrapositive to a universal affirmative, as is the method of the ancients, is something for which no proof has been established. And it was answered, secondly, by restricting the subject in both to 'conceptualization' (al-taṣawwur). That is, the commentator of al-Maṭāliʿ explained that they are not both true together by joining the contrapositive of one to the other itself, to produce an absurdity as follows: everything that is not impossible to request is non-perceived, and every non-perceived thing is impossible to request, so it is concluded that everything that is not impossible to request is impossible to request. His statement: 'more specific than the contradictory of the second' because its contradictory is a particular negative, namely 'not every non-perceived thing is impossible to request', and this is a particular affirmative with a privative term, which requires the existence of the subject. His statement: 'to a universal affirmative with a privative term' – and as for its conversion to a universal affirmative with two privative terms, as the commentator of al-Maṭāliʿ established, it is of no use here because the universal affirmative with two privative terms is in the same ruling as the simple negative in not requiring the existence of the subject. Then it is possible to say: the contrapositive of each and its necessary consequent are true due to the non-existence of the subject, so they do not conflict with the original premise that requires the existence of the subject. Likewise, joining the contrapositive of each with the other itself does not produce a conclusion because of the absence of an affirmative minor premise. And by what we have mentioned, it is clear that the mentioned answer is complete.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents two answers to the proof that the two premises cannot both be true. The first answer denies the premise that a universal affirmative converts by contrapositive to a universal affirmative, as the ancients maintained; the commentator says no proof has been established for this rule. The second answer restricts the subject of both premises to 'conceptualization' (al-taṣawwur), meaning the argument only applies to conceptual requests, not to other types of requests. The commentator then discusses a rival explanation from the commentator of al-Maṭāliʿ, who tried to prove the inconsistency by joining the contrapositive of one premise to the other to produce a contradiction. He argues that this fails because the universal affirmative with two privative terms does not require the existence of the subject, so the conversion is not effective. He then concludes that the second answer (restricting the subject) is sound and completes the refutation.

Key Terms
انعكاس الموجبة الكلية
عكس النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All logical conversion rules are proven and universally accepted.
Correction: Some conversion rules, like the one used by the ancients here, lack proof and are not universally valid. This shows the need to scrutinize every premise.

Checkpoint
What is the first answer to the inconsistency proof?
A) Denying the truth of the premises.
B) Denying the conversion of a universal affirmative by contrapositive to a universal affirmative.
C) Restricting the subject to conceptualizations.
D) Introducing a third premise about existence.

Key Takeaway
The first answer shows that a key conversion rule used in the proof is unproven and therefore unacceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, have you ever accepted a rule or belief simply because it came from a respected source, without demanding proof? How does this lesson challenge you to examine what you assume is true?
?
The denial of an unproven rule here is a logical move, but it also requires courage to challenge a method of 'the ancients.' Are you willing to question long-held assumptions in your faith or practice, even if they feel traditional? What would it take for you to demand proof?
?
Think of a conviction you hold that you have never seriously examined. Could it be based on an unproven 'conversion rule'? Commit today to investigate one such belief and see if evidence supports it.
