The Second Answer: Restricting the Subject to Conceptualizations
The second answer: restricting the subject to conceptualizations, with a critique of a rival proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second answer to the inconsistency proof: restricting the subject of both premises to conceptualizations (al-tasawwur) and how this resolves the issue while also understanding the critique of a rival explanation.

Core Idea
Restricting the subject to conceptual requests shows that the apparent inconsistency disappears.

The second answer takes a different approach: instead of denying a rule, it refines the subject matter of the premises. By limiting them to conceptualizations, we see that the alleged contradiction does not arise. Let's explore this answer and its defense against a rival explanation that attempted to prove inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer itself
The answer is to restrict the subject in both premises to 'conceptualization' (al-tasawwur). Understand what this means: we only consider requests that are conceptual, not existential.
This limits the scope of the premises.
2
Examine the rival explanation
The commentator of al-Maṭāliʿ tried to show that even with this restriction, the premises cannot both be true. He constructs a syllogism to prove an absurdity.
The syllogism: everything not impossible to request is non-perceived; every non-perceived thing is impossible to request; so everything not impossible to request is impossible to request.
3
Analyze the critique of the rival
The commentator points out that the rival's conversion to a universal affirmative with two privative terms does not require subject existence. Therefore, the syllogism fails because the minor premise loses its affirmative force.
A universal affirmative with two privative terms behaves like a simple negative.
4
Conclude with the second answer's completeness
The commentator declares that the second answer is complete and sound. The rival's attempt does not refute it.
The key is that the restricted subject prevents the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأجيب بمنع انعكاس الموجبة الكلية كنفسها بعكس النقيض تارة فإن انعكاس الموجبة الكلية بعكس النقيض إلى موجبة كلية كما هو طريقة القدماء، مما لم يقم عليه برهان (و) أجيب ( بتقييد الموضوع فيهما بالتصور أخرى ) أي بين شارح المطالع عدم اجتماعهما في الصدق بأن ضم عكس نقيض إحديهما إلى عين الأخرى، لينتج المحال هكذا كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه فهو غير مشعور به وكل غير مشعور به يمتنع ينتج كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه يمتنع طلبه . قوله: (أخص من نقيض (الثاني) لأن نقيضه سالبة جزئية أعني ليس كل ما هو غير مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وهذه موجبة جزئية معدولة تقتضي وجود الموضوع. قوله : ( إلى موجبة (كلية معدولة وأما انعكاسها إلى موجبة كلية سالبة الطرفين كما أثبته شارح المطالع فلا يفيد هاهنا لأن الموجبة السالبة الطرفين في حكم السالبة البسيطة في عدم اقتضاء وجود الموضوع، فحينئذ يجوز أن يقال : يصدق عکس نقيض كل منهما ولازمه بانتفاء الموضوع، فلا ينافي الأصل المقتضي لوجود الموضوع، وكذا ضم عكس نقيض كل منهما مع عين الأخرى لا ينتج لانتفاء إيجاب الصغرى، وبما ذكرنا تبين أن الجواب المذكور تام
So it was answered, once, by denying the conversion of the universal affirmative as itself by contrapositive: for the conversion of the universal affirmative by contrapositive to a universal affirmative, as is the method of the ancients, is something for which no proof has been established. And it was answered, secondly, by restricting the subject in both to 'conceptualization' (al-taṣawwur). That is, the commentator of al-Maṭāliʿ explained that they are not both true together by joining the contrapositive of one to the other itself, to produce an absurdity as follows: everything that is not impossible to request is non-perceived, and every non-perceived thing is impossible to request, so it is concluded that everything that is not impossible to request is impossible to request. His statement: 'more specific than the contradictory of the second' because its contradictory is a particular negative, namely 'not every non-perceived thing is impossible to request', and this is a particular affirmative with a privative term, which requires the existence of the subject. His statement: 'to a universal affirmative with a privative term' – and as for its conversion to a universal affirmative with two privative terms, as the commentator of al-Maṭāliʿ established, it is of no use here because the universal affirmative with two privative terms is in the same ruling as the simple negative in not requiring the existence of the subject. Then it is possible to say: the contrapositive of each and its necessary consequent are true due to the non-existence of the subject, so they do not conflict with the original premise that requires the existence of the subject. Likewise, joining the contrapositive of each with the other itself does not produce a conclusion because of the absence of an affirmative minor premise. And by what we have mentioned, it is clear that the mentioned answer is complete.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents two answers to the proof that the two premises cannot both be true. The first answer denies the premise that a universal affirmative converts by contrapositive to a universal affirmative, as the ancients maintained; the commentator says no proof has been established for this rule. The second answer restricts the subject of both premises to 'conceptualization' (al-taṣawwur), meaning the argument only applies to conceptual requests, not to other types of requests. The commentator then discusses a rival explanation from the commentator of al-Maṭāliʿ, who tried to prove the inconsistency by joining the contrapositive of one premise to the other to produce a contradiction. He argues that this fails because the universal affirmative with two privative terms does not require the existence of the subject, so the conversion is not effective. He then concludes that the second answer (restricting the subject) is sound and completes the refutation.

Key Terms
تقيد الموضوع
وجود الموضوع
موجبة كلية معدولة
سالبة الطرفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that the rival explanation fails?
A) Because the conversion used does not require subject existence.
B) Because the premises are false.
C) Because the conclusion is not contradictory.
D) Because the reasoning is circular.

Key Takeaway
The second answer resolves the inconsistency by limiting the premises to conceptualizations, and the rival's attempt to refute it fails because it relies on a conversion that does not preserve subject existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a seeming contradiction in your own beliefs – whether in theology, in daily life, or in your understanding of Islam – do you immediately assume the contradiction is genuine, or do you first examine whether the terms are being used consistently? How can the discipline of restricting the subject (taqyīd al-mawḍūʿ) help you avoid false dilemmas and deepen your conviction?
?
The passage shows that a seemingly valid logical move (conversion) can fail because it does not preserve subject existence. In your own reasoning, are there times when you assume a principle applies universally without checking its preconditions? How might overlooking 'existential import' lead you to conclusions that are not supported by reality?
?
Consider a time you were convinced of a contradiction in something you learned about Allah's attributes. Looking back, could the issue have been resolved by defining the subject more precisely – distinguishing between what is conceptual and what is external? What would it take for you to adopt this habit of mental precision before dismissing apparent contradictions?

Rational Judgments
intermediate

Modal argument that conceptual knowledge cannot be sought
A logical proof using contraposition that conceptual knowledge cannot be acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ījī uses logical conversion (contraposition and simple conversion) to demonstrate that conceptual knowledge cannot be acquired by seeking.

Core Idea
Conceptual knowledge is either already known or unknown; either way, seeking it is impossible.

Al-Ījī presents a logical argument to show that conceptual knowledge cannot be sought. He divides concepts into perceived and unperceived and argues that both are impossible to seek. Then he anticipates a logical objection involving conversion of propositions and demonstrates that no contradiction arises. This passage exemplifies the use of propositional logic in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical structure and the technical terms.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two categorical propositions: 'Every perceived concept is impossible to seek' and 'Every unperceived concept is impossible to seek.' Understand why each holds.
3
Follow the contraposition
See how the first premise is converted by contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) into 'Everything that is not impossible to seek is not a perceived concept.' This is a logical transformation.
4
Understand the simple conversion
Then this result is converted by simple conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustawī) into 'Some of what is not a concept is impossible to seek.' Note that this is a particular statement.
5
Check for contradiction
Observe that the converted propositions do not contradict the original premises because their subjects are more general. The argument is logically consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نحن نستدل هكذا التصور إما تصور مشعور به وإما تصور غير مشعور به وكل تصور مشعور به يمتنع طلبه وكل تصور غير مشعور به يمتنع طلبه، وحينئذ تنعكس الحملية الأولى بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ما لا يمتنع طلبه فهو ليس تصوراً مشعوراً به وينعكس هذا العكس بالمستوي إلى قولنا بعض ما ليس تصوراً يمتنع طلبه وهذا لا ينافي الحملية الثانية لأن موضوعه أعم من موضوعها، ألا ترى أن ما ليس تصوراً مشعوراً به جاز أن لا يكون تصوراً أصلاً، وأن يكون تصوراً غير مشعور به وقس على ذلك حال الحملية الثانية، فإن العكس المستوي لعكس نقيضها هو قولنا بعض ما ليس تصوراً غير مشعور به لا وموضوعه أعم من موضوع الحملية الأولى فلا منافاة بينهما
We argue as follows: The concept is either a perceived concept or an unperceived concept. Every perceived concept is impossible to seek, and every unperceived concept is impossible to seek. Then the first categorical proposition is converted by contraposition to our statement: 'Everything that is not impossible to seek is not a perceived concept.' This converse is further converted by simple conversion to our statement: 'Some of what is not a concept is impossible to seek.' This does not contradict the second categorical proposition because its subject is more general than its subject. Do you not see that what is not a perceived concept may either be not a concept at all or be an unperceived concept? Apply the same to the second categorical proposition, for the simple conversion of its contrapositive is our statement: 'Some of what is not an unperceived concept is impossible to seek,' and its subject is more general than the subject of the first proposition, so there is no contradiction between them.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof that conceptual knowledge cannot be acquired by seeking. The author states that a concept either is already perceived or not perceived. If it is perceived, seeking it is impossible because it is already known. If it is not perceived, seeking it is also impossible because one does not know what to seek. Then the author addresses a potential objection: the logical conversion of these statements might lead to a contradiction, but he shows that by applying contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) and simple conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustawī), no contradiction arises. He explains that the subject of each converted proposition is broader, so they can be true together. This is a sophisticated logical maneuver typical of kalām arguments about the impossibility of acquiring first concepts.

Key Terms
تصور
مشعور به
حملية
عكس النقيض
عكس مستوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The logical conversion of the premises leads to a contradiction that invalidates the argument.
Correction: By using contraposition and simple conversion, the author shows that the converted propositions have broader subjects, so they do not contradict the original premises.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of converting the first categorical proposition by contraposition?
A) To show that the argument is invalid
B) To show that the converse is also true and does not contradict the second proposition
C) To prove that concepts are acquired by seeking
D) To negate the second proposition

Key Takeaway
The argument demonstrates that the impossibility of seeking conceptual knowledge is logically consistent through propositional conversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument concludes that you cannot actively seek conceptual knowledge—either you already have it, or you don't know what to seek. Reflect on a concept you have struggled to understand. Was your struggle itself an illusion? If so, how does that change the way you approach learning new ideas from now on? Will you humbly admit your ignorance and rely on divine guidance rather than your own efforts?
?
If conceptual knowledge cannot be sought, then what is the role of your own striving in acquiring knowledge? Does this argument challenge the common emphasis on 'seeking knowledge' (talab al-'ilm)? How will you reconcile the command to seek knowledge with the logical impossibility of seeking concepts? What does this teach you about the source of true understanding?
?
Consider the concepts you hold most dear—your beliefs about God, the soul, or the afterlife. Are they perceived or unperceived? If they are perceived, you already possess them; if not, you cannot seek them. Does that mean you should stop questioning and simply accept what is given? How does that sit with your conscience? What will you do today to ensure your concepts are grounded in truth rather than assumption?

Refuting the Claim that Necessary Beliefs Are Innate
Jurjani refutes the claim that beliefs necessary for taklif are innate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that foundational beliefs for religious responsibility are innate (daruri) is self-contradictory, because they are obligatory (wajib) and therefore must be within human power.

Core Idea
What is innate cannot be obligatory, but knowing Allah is obligatory — so it cannot be innate.

Jurjani now addresses a third position: that the beliefs required for taklif (religious responsibility) are innate, not acquired. This view, attributed to al-Jahiz, seems convenient, but it leads to a logical contradiction. Let's see how Jurjani refutes it using a simple but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the madhab
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the description of the third madhab: that beliefs necessary for the mukallaf are daruri (innate/necessary).
The passage ends with 'هذا خلف' which means 'this is contradictory'.
2
Note the attribution
The madhab is attributed to al-Jahiz and his followers. Understanding the source helps contextualize the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation's logical structure
The refutation hinges on the consensus that knowledge of Allah is wajib (obligatory). If it were daruri, it would be beyond human power, and nothing beyond human power can be wajib. This creates a contradiction.
The argument is: (1) Knowledge of Allah is wajib (by consensus). (2) If daruri → beyond power → not wajib. (3) Therefore it cannot be daruri.
4
Appreciate the contradiction
The author explicitly concludes 'هذا خلف' (this is contradictory), meaning the madhab is invalid because its claim leads to an impossibility.
This reinforces the importance of rationality in Islamic theology: even consensus-based obligations must be rationally coherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذهب الثالث في هذه المسألة أن ما اعتقاده لازم للمكلف مما يتوقف عليه إثبات التكليف والعلم به نحو إثبات الصانع وصفاته والنبوات ضروري قيل: هذا مذهب الجاحظ ومن تابعه ويبطله أن معرفة الله تعالى واجبة إجماعاً كما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة أو عقلاً كما ذهب إليه المعتزلة فلو كانت ضرورية لكانت غير مقدور عليها ولا شيء من غير المقدور كذلك أي بواجب فلو كانت المعرفة ضرورية لم تكن واجبة هذا خلف
The third madhab in this issue: that whose belief is necessary for the mukallaf, among that on which depends the establishment of taklif and knowledge of it, such as proving the Creator, His attributes, and prophethoods, is necessary (daruri). It is said: this is the madhab of al-Jahiz and those who follow him. And it is refuted by the fact that knowledge of Allah, exalted, is wajib (obligatory) by consensus, as the Ash'aris held, or by reason as the Mu'tazila held. For if it were daruri, it would be beyond one's power, and nothing beyond one's power is wajib. So if knowledge were daruri, it would not be wajib; this is contradictory (khalaf).
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents and then refutes the third madhab regarding the epistemological status of beliefs that are prerequisite for taklif (religious obligation). The madhab, attributed to al-Jahiz and his followers, claims that such beliefs — like the existence of God, His attributes, and the sending of prophets — are daruri (necessary, innate) knowledge. Jurjani refutes this by pointing out that knowledge of God is wajib (obligatory) by consensus (among Ash'aris it is wajib by revelation, among Mu'tazila by reason). If this knowledge were daruri, it would not be within human power (makhura) to acquire or avoid, and something beyond one's power cannot be wajib. Therefore, if it were daruri, it could not be wajib — but it is wajib by agreement, which is a contradiction (khalaf). Thus the madhab is invalid. The next section (the opponent's proof) is not included here to avoid separating objection from its answer.

Key Terms
madhab
daruri
wajib
taklif
ghafil

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that something can be both daruri (innate/necessary) and wajib (obligatory to acquire) at the same time.
Correction: This passage clarifies that daruri means it is beyond human power, while wajib requires volition and effort, so they are incompatible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that knowledge of Allah cannot be daruri?
A) Because it is not proven by reason.
B) Because if it were daruri, it would be wajib by default.
C) Because daruri things are beyond human power, and thus cannot be wajib.
D) Because it is acquired only through following authorities.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to know Allah requires that this knowledge be within human power, so it cannot be innate (daruri).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that knowing Allah is obligatory upon you. But do you live as if this knowledge is something you already have innately, never actively seeking it with intention and effort? When was the last time you set aside time to learn about your Creator as an act of obedience, not just habit?
?
If the obligation to know Allah requires that it be within your power, then what excuse do you have for the moments when you are inattentive (ghafil) or neglectful? Do you treat your faith as a default state rather than a commanded pursuit?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and He asks you: 'Did you know that knowing Me was obligatory upon you? Did you seek that knowledge, or did you rely on something innate?' What will you say? What change must you make today to ensure your answer is not silence?
